In this occasion, you might notice that some menu items are somewhat mysteriously just there, although there are no `according`{.literal} files in the `clippings`{.literal} folder this has to do with packages.
:::
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Packages"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch02.html#ch02lvl3sec15}Packages {.title}
:::

So now, we\'ve[]{#ch02.html#id132 .indexterm} seen externals, abstractions, extras, and []{#ch02.html#id133 .indexterm}clippings that moved to special places in our filesystem, and after a year, we really lost track of everything. We probably have two or three different abstraction folders by now; we\'ve had multiple updates of Max each time the standard clippings get restored that we delete each time because we want to keep our clippings menu clean. Also, at a certain point, you\'d like to share a patch that contains all sorts of references to files and patches on your system. All this sounds pretty messy and confusing right? Here come packages to our rescue!

Go to `Max 6.1/packages`{.literal} and take a look inside the `Vizzie`{.literal} folder there. We find some folders called `extras`{.literal}, `clippings`{.literal}, and `patchers`{.literal}, and some other stuff. These folders just work the same way as the folders in the `Max 6.1/`{.literal} directory. So if we create a folder called `My first project`{.literal}, `my general stuff`{.literal}, or something similar, inside the `packages`{.literal} folder and create these subfolders, we can use these instead of having our files spread out over the system. Within the `packages`{.literal} folder, you\'ll also find an `about packages.txt`{.literal} file, which describes what types of folders are supported and how to use them.
:::

::: {.section title="Projects"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch02.html#ch02lvl3sec16}Projects {.title}
:::

Projects are a[]{#ch02.html#id134 .indexterm} feature that allows us both to keep track of all the []{#ch02.html#id135 .indexterm}dependencies a patcher might have and also to easily pack those together. We can view it as the dependant of the packages feature. If we plan a complex patcher, we might save it as a project, consolidate everything (media, abstractions, code, and so on) automatically, and open the whole thing on another computer without worrying about dependencies.
:::

::: {.section title="Prototypes"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch02.html#ch02lvl3sec17}Prototypes {.title}
:::

Prototypes are []{#ch02.html#id136 .indexterm}saved versions of a specific object. Most[]{#ch02.html#id137 .indexterm} of its attributes are saved and we can load them at any time. Take, for example, a `[led]`{.literal} object; if we create it, we can right-click on it and select [**prototypes**]{.strong} and can choose different styles. Since we can also save our own, and many objects[]{#ch02.html#id138 .indexterm} have many attributes that define their[]{#ch02.html#id139 .indexterm} overall behavior, we might consider building up our own library of prototypes that show up more complex configurations than just a single color. Also, for a consistent GUI design, this comes in handy.
:::
:::
:::
:::

[]{#ch02s02.html}

::: {.section title="Basic Max patching and GUI"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Now, we\'ll []{#ch02s02.html#id140 .indexterm}finally go ahead and start patching. In this section, we\'ll go[]{#ch02s02.html#id141 .indexterm} through the basic functionality of Max and we will talk a bit about the craft of patching. It\'s an easy thing, but we will try to explore a lot about shortcuts and ways to go about things faster. Additionally, we will of course get to know the GUI in more detail. Nearly all patches you see here can of course be used readily as they are from your download. On the other hand, you will learn more if you just replicate them quickly, or even better, understand them and rebuild the principle without looking at them more than once. Often, there are many ways to achieve a goal and the number of ways to achieve the goal increases with the complexity of the goal.

::: {.section title="Objects in Max"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s02.html#ch02lvl2sec15}Objects in Max {.title}
:::

Before we []{#ch02s02.html#id142 .indexterm}dive into patching, let\'s just very briefly[]{#ch02s02.html#id143 .indexterm} think about the Max objects that we have been talking about. We can imagine them as small virtual machines (Cipriani and Giri 2010, 51) that fulfill different tasks for us. I think you have a basic understanding of what they are by now, but there is something left to say about how they work. Look at the following screenshot. We see an `[int]`{.literal} object. The bubbles appear when you hover over the corresponding inlets and outlets but of course, usually not all at the same time, as shown here. As you can see, there is a red ring on the left hot inlet and a blue one on the right cold inlet.

These are called hot and cold and also illustrated that way (as shown in the following screenshot) because if we send a message, for example, an integer into the right or cold inlet, there won\'t be any output. It will be stored there as it says in the bubble, whereas if we send an integer or bang message into the left or hot inlet, it will output the integer or the last stored integer in the case of the bang message:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_12.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/wu0kJa5.jpeg)
:::

The []{#ch02s02.html#id144 .indexterm}concept of hot and cold inlets is everywhere in Max, and []{#ch02s02.html#id145 .indexterm}we will encounter it at the beginning or maybe later when we discuss message ordering. The important thing is to keep this idea in mind.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s02.html#note05}Note {.title}

[**Technical detail**]{.strong}

Max objects are written in C. When we create a Max object, we create an instance of the compiled C code that represents that object. If we send a message into it, we are simply calling a function within that code.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Arguments"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch02s02.html#ch02lvl3sec18}Arguments {.title}
:::

You have []{#ch02s02.html#id146 .indexterm}already seen arguments, for example, in an previous illustration. There, we saw a `[* 0.]`{.literal} object. It is, in fact, a `[*]`{.literal} object with the []{#ch02s02.html#id147 .indexterm}argument `0`{.literal}. In general, arguments can fulfill two purposes:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   To initialize a variable
-   To set a constant regarding the object\'s behavior
:::

In the case of our `[* 0.]`{.literal} object, we do both at the same time. When we start up our patcher and just send something into the left inlet, we do a multiplication by 0. As soon as we send another number, for example, 5 into the right inlet, the zero is overridden and we multiply by 5. So here, we initialized one of the factors. However, what constant did we set at the same time?

We didn\'t just initialize the multiplication with 0. Guess what\'s the default initial value; 0 of course. So why did we do it? Because we didn\'t put 0 in there but 0. Many math (and other) objects operate with integers by default. If we initialize them with a float argument, we tell the object to do floating point calculations, thereby defining its internal behavior constantly, shown as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_17.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/DiZ5h3f.jpeg)
:::

In the preceding[]{#ch02s02.html#id148 .indexterm} screenshot, you can see the different behavior of two differently[]{#ch02s02.html#id149 .indexterm} initialized `[*]`{.literal} objects. The integer one simply performs a floor operation, cutting way everything behind the decimal point. Remember that this is one of the most common mistakes both for beginners and advanced users. It is recommended that you initialize everything explicitly (instead of relying on the default value because we know or believe it will suit our needs) because it makes patches more readable. Also, it\'s a psychological trick; we at least think about the value very shortly and state it explicitly instead of hiding it, setting the value implicitly, and maybe a little more unconsciously.

We can find what arguments are obligatory or optional for any given object in its help patch, in the object\'s reference, or in the descriptions that pop up after you typed a space after the object name of the object you are creating, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_18.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/vGLYdkJ.jpeg)
:::

The []{#ch02s02.html#id150 .indexterm}syntax is important here; we always get the object name, a single []{#ch02s02.html#id151 .indexterm}space, the arguments separated by spaces, and the attributes afterwards, whereas each attribute is preceded by [**@**]{.strong}, and all of them are also separated by spaces:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_16.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/cjlElAB.jpeg)
:::

In the previous screenshot, we can see a `[metro]`{.literal} object that has been given one argument, its interval in ms, and with one attribute set, `active 1`{.literal}, which means it is switched on. Before []{#ch02s02.html#id152 .indexterm}we go looking at attributes, we will consider []{#ch02s02.html#id153 .indexterm}another example of arguments, that is, the ones of the `[counter]`{.literal} object:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_19.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/0L18SLY.jpeg)
:::

In this case, we are provided with three arguments: [**1, 0, 5**]{.strong}. These are as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The mode of the counter where [**0**]{.strong} means count forwards, [**1**]{.strong} means backwards, and [**2**]{.strong} means back and forth
-   The minimum of the count
-   The maximum of the count
:::

So this counter counts from [**5**]{.strong} down to [**0**]{.strong}. Most of the time, we need something that counts from [**0**]{.strong} to some number, so up, not down. In this case, we can provide only one argument and it will be taken as the maximum. If we provide two arguments, it will be taken as minimum and maximum. Pretty confusing, right? That\'s something we will get used to, but if there are more parameters, nobody wants to count how many arguments are provided to identify their function. That\'s where attributes come into play.
:::

::: {.section title="Attributes"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch02s02.html#ch02lvl3sec19}Attributes {.title}
:::

As we []{#ch02s02.html#id154 .indexterm}have seen, attributes are like arguments, but they have the []{#ch02s02.html#id155 .indexterm}advantage of being able to be addressed by writing their name instead of knowing (or looking up often) that the property we are searching for can be set via, say, the third argument.

For example, the `[dosomething 1000 0.5 0.9]`{.literal} object won\'t mean a lot to us in a couple of weeks, whereas `[dosomethin @cutoff 1000 @resonance 0.5 @gain 0.9]`{.literal} is a lot more descriptive.

Most of the time, we cannot choose whether we address something as an attribute or an argument (for our own abstractions we can!); some properties can be set via one while some by the other. The `[metro]`{.literal} object\'s interval can be set via the first argument or the argument `@interval n`{.literal}, but that\'s an exception. For the sake of completeness, the attribute overrides the argument in the rare case in which we will provide both `[metro]`{.literal} and `@interval n`{.literal} at the same time. It might seem that arguments and attributes are doing more or []{#ch02s02.html#id156 .indexterm}less the same work, but internally, they are substantially []{#ch02s02.html#id157 .indexterm}different. Their actual difference doesn\'t matter for us too much, but we just want to get a feeling of whether a property is an attribute or an argument. Arguments initialize and attributes configure an object.

All attributes are collected in the inspector. Remembering the shortcut for the inspector, the shortcut is [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*I*]{.emphasis} (this only works if we have an object selected), will help us a lot since we can then also select an attribute by typing the name of the attribute, then hit [*Enter*]{.emphasis}, and configure everything as we like in a very fast manner. The following screenshot shows the inspector window:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_20.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Qntoikh.jpeg)
:::

The inspector has a couple of built-in gadgets that help us a lot. By pressing the little [**@**]{.strong} sign at the bottom of the inspector\'s screen. We can see the attribute names, so we can remember it and type it into the object box next time we need it. Also, we can just drag-and-drop attributes out of the[]{#ch02s02.html#id158 .indexterm} inspector into our patches. This will cause a little pop up to open, asking []{#ch02s02.html#id159 .indexterm}us what should be done. Try out these options; they are all made to interact with the chosen attribute in a manual or programmatic manner or to monitor it. This saves us a lot of typing.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s02.html#note06}Note {.title}

You can set all attributes with the inspector but you can\'t access all of them with the `@`{.literal} notation or dynamically using messages. There are ways around this limitation, which we will cover in the section on scripting using the `[thispatcher]`{.literal} object.
:::
:::

If we configure a certain attribute in the inspector, it can happen that our newly set value is not saved. Some attributes are simply not saved by default, but we can change this by freezing the attribute. We can find out whether an attribute is being saved if we look at their names. Those that are written in italics won\'t be saved; those that are not will be. Those that are written in italics will also be available through the `@`{.literal} notation and therefore can be set permanently this way as well.
:::
:::
:::

[]{#ch02s03.html}

::: {.section title="Creating our Hello World program"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

So let\'s []{#ch02s03.html#id160 .indexterm}start up Max, close the splash screen if you haven\'t got rid of it already, and make a new patch ([*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*N*]{.emphasis}). You will see something like what\'s shown in the following screenshot; without the objects, the cords, the message box, and the sidebar might not be open or it might be in another mode. You don\'t need to open the sidebar for now, but if you like it, press the button with the number 11 in the following screenshot. We can have the Max window in a separate window. Either way, free floating or in the side bar, open it and I highly recommend that you remember the shortcut for it, which is [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*M*]{.emphasis}. Now, we have everything set up to write the obligatory Hello World program:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/dfC388Z.jpeg)
:::

So this is[]{#ch02s03.html#id161 .indexterm} our Hello World program. How was this created? What do we see here?

::: {.section title="Dissection and construction"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s03.html#ch02lvl2sec16}Dissection and construction {.title}
:::

First of all, we []{#ch02s03.html#id162 .indexterm}see three objects here. A `[button]`{.literal}, a `[message]`{.literal}, and a `[print]`{.literal} object (and of course, a `comment`{.literal} object that reads [**click here!**]{.strong}). So[]{#ch02s03.html#id163 .indexterm} we can create them just like that; we create a new object in[]{#ch02s03.html#id164 .indexterm} the patch by just hitting [*n*]{.emphasis} and start writing. In[]{#ch02s03.html#id165 .indexterm} one object, we write `button`{.literal}; in the second one, we write `message`{.literal}; and in the third one, we write `print`{.literal}. The purpose of this little patch is obviously to print the [**Hello World**]{.strong} message to the Max window, which in this case is attached and not a separate window. Then, we interconnect these objects.

Now, here is the ninja way to do it:

::: {.orderedlist}
1.  Make a new patcher: ([*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*N*]{.emphasis}).
2.  Hit [*B*]{.emphasis} to get the button right away.
3.  Hit [*M*]{.emphasis} to get a message box and position the message box below the button object using arrow keys on the keyboard. Use [*Shift*]{.emphasis} + arrow keys for momentary toggling snap to grid on and off. Hit [*Enter*]{.emphasis} again to edit the contents of the message box and hit [*Shift*]{.emphasis} + [*Enter*]{.emphasis} to finish editing it.
4.  Hit [*N*]{.emphasis} for a new object, type `print`{.literal}, hit [*Enter*]{.emphasis}, and position it below.
5.  If you want to get the selection back on another object to edit or reposition it again, you can use [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + the arrow keys.
6.  Now, if you have the max toolbox (mentioned in the externals section) installed, select them all and hit [*Shift*]{.emphasis} + [*C*]{.emphasis}. Otherwise, just connect them manually, and we are done! Now, this sounds pretty complicated, and you don\'t have to remember all these shortcuts, but if you know some of them, you\'ll have a better time coding with Max, I promise. Just try it out, it\'s not so hard!
:::

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s03.html#tip05}Tip {.title}

There are two files that mainly define our keyboard shortcuts: `Max 6.1/Cycling '74/interfaces/maxinterface.json`{.literal} and `Max 6.1/Cycling '74/init/max-keycommands.txt`{.literal}.

Be sure to take a backup when redefining things there.
:::
:::

If you []{#ch02s03.html#id166 .indexterm}try out your patcher, you might[]{#ch02s03.html#id167 .indexterm} need to [**Lock**]{.strong} the patcher before the button reacts to your clicks[]{#ch02s03.html#id168 .indexterm} in the expected way. In Max, there are two[]{#ch02s03.html#id169 .indexterm} modes of interaction with your patcher: the edit mode and the locked mode. Naturally, one[]{#ch02s03.html#id170 .indexterm} is for editing your program and one is for actual interaction. So in order to be able to click on the button, we have four ways to enter the locked mode:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Clicking on the little lock icon on the bottom left of the patcher window
-   Using the shortcut [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*E*]{.emphasis} to toggle between the modes
-   Using [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + left mouse button to click into an empty area of the patcher to toggle the modes
-   Pressing and holding [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} during the interaction with the GUI
:::

The last option is the preferred one during development since one often needs to only adjust one value or quickly click a button, but actually, one is still patching and not performing.
:::

::: {.section title="Contents"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s03.html#ch02lvl2sec17}Contents {.title}
:::

Let\'s think[]{#ch02s03.html#id171 .indexterm} about the actual contents of this patcher. We []{#ch02s03.html#id172 .indexterm}have a user interface object, namely a button. We have a message box and the `print`{.literal} object. If we click on the button, it actually sends a message out of its outlet, called a [**bang**]{.strong} message. We discussed this in [Chapter 1](#ch01.html "Chapter 1. Getting Started with Max"){.link}, [*Getting Started with Max*]{.emphasis}. It tells the next object to act now and to do whatever it is supposed to do, nearly always to output data.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s03.html#note07}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

Prove that the message that goes from the button to the message box actually is a bang message.
:::
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The [print] object"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s03.html#ch02lvl2sec18}The \[print\] object {.title}
:::

Before we go over the message box, there are a couple of things we need to go over in relation to the `print`{.literal} object. When[]{#ch02s03.html#id173 .indexterm} programming with Max, we must always[]{#ch02s03.html#id174 .indexterm} remember that `print`{.literal} is one of our best friends. As in many other programming languages, there are various ways to output data or debug processes, but the `print`{.literal} statement/object is at the heart of debugging. Whenever we aren\'t sure what\'s happening, we put `print`{.literal} objects everywhere.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s03.html#note08}Note {.title}

[**Caution**]{.strong}

Printing a lot to the max window drains more performance than you might think! Keep track of your `print`{.literal} objects that lie around and consider using mechanisms such as `[gate]`{.literal} and the `[send]`{.literal}/`[receive]`{.literal} system to deactivate printing globally.
:::
:::

The `print`{.literal} object[]{#ch02s03.html#id175 .indexterm} takes an argument that will show up in the Max window\'s object column instead of [**print**]{.strong}, so we can design our printouts to be a bit more informative, as in the following screenshot. Also, if we double-click on a line in the Max window, Max highlights the corresponding print object.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s03.html#note09}Note {.title}

Many beginners struggle with errors in their patchers although Max is actually telling them what\'s wrong or at least gives a hint. Max also communicates with us through the [**Max**]{.strong} window (not only `print`{.literal} objects), so keep an eye on it. A typical window layout to do standard work in Max has a patcher window, an [**Inspector**]{.strong} window, and the [**Max**]{.strong} window open and visible.
:::
:::

Look at the following screenshot to see an example of printing:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_13.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rbzCbTF.jpeg)
:::

The debugging facilities of printing might also be the reason why the Hello World program is so popular for starting to program in a specific language. We will need a simple debugging tool right at the beginning, so we can always see what we are doing. Debugging is not the most exciting topic in the world and nobody wants to learn a lot about how[]{#ch02s03.html#id176 .indexterm} to treat the mistakes before starting to do[]{#ch02s03.html#id177 .indexterm} anything. This book contains a section on debugging, and I invite you to refer to it whenever you feel the need for it. We\'ll cover this rather late and you might want to avoid the situation in which you say \"Well, great, why didn\'t you tell me this earlier?\"
:::

::: {.section title="The message box"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s03.html#ch02lvl2sec19}The message box {.title}
:::

The `message box`{.literal} object is a very versatile special object. You will find it everywhere, and while we []{#ch02s03.html#id178 .indexterm}will use it in various ways in this book, not []{#ch02s03.html#id179 .indexterm}all of its functionality is covered here. As you know, you can always get help if you press [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + click on an object. Here are some of its main features:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   We can edit the content and write any message in there that we would want to send anywhere.
-   We can set the contents of it dynamically by sending anything into the right inlet.
-   We can, as you have seen, send out the contents by sending a bang into its left inlet.
-   Moreover, if we click on it (when in locked mode), it also sends out its contents, so it\'s also a basic text button.
:::

You see it\'s an object that helps us in many situations and the following screenshot shows of some more advanced features of it. We can use it for the basic formatting of messages and lists and even ordering things in time.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_14.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/cHpKDkn.jpeg)
:::

Using the `$n`{.literal} object (where [*n*]{.emphasis} is a number between 1 and 9) notation enables us to generate[]{#ch02s03.html#id180 .indexterm} compound messages on the fly as you can see. Also, the []{#ch02s03.html#id181 .indexterm}use of commas is important to notice here. Commas divide the contents up into several messages in sequence. These messages are sent in sequence, as fast as Max can.

Since we saw that we can display messages using the message box right inlet, it\'s also a good debugging tool. However, the patcher shown in the previous screenshot shows a subtle difference between `print`{.literal} and the `message box`{.literal} object. Look at the right-hand section where we used the commas. The `message box`{.literal} object used for display only shows the sequence. That\'s not because this is a screenshot; try it out, you won\'t see the other messages (Max is too fast!). In contrast to that, `[print]`{.literal} can be used very well for this task; all messages appear in chronological order (by default at least).
:::

::: {.section title="The MSP-Hello World"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch02s03.html#ch02lvl2sec20}The MSP-Hello World {.title}
:::

By now, you[]{#ch02s03.html#id182 .indexterm} should be able read data from any[]{#ch02s03.html#id183 .indexterm} point in Max using either the `message box`{.literal} or the `print`{.literal} statement. But what about streams of numbers and signals? What about MSP? Let\'s have a look at how to get information about an unknown signal. Depending on if it\'s a stream of Max messages or MSP signals, we are going to need different tools, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_15.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/1zoRtyT.jpeg)
:::

In this[]{#ch02s03.html#id184 .indexterm} screenshot, you can see `patcher wysiwyg.maxpat`{.literal}. Open it and play around with it. Don\'t look into the blackbox too []{#ch02s03.html#id185 .indexterm}soon (by double clicking it)! It generates two signals: one in the max domain, a stream of numbers of course, and the other one in the MSP domain. This patch may contain a lot of objects that are new to you, but you can just [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + click on each of them to get to know them. The main point here is again to understand what we are doing. Although a signal has a lot of properties, often, the most important thing about an audio signal is how it sounds; as obvious as this sounds, don\'t forget this.

However, there are many other features a signal may have and many ways of looking at those. Take amplitude, for example; you can look at the numerical value, you can measure in dB or linear, or you can look at the peak value if you want to check whether your signal is clipping or use RMS, if you rather care for a rough guide of loudness. There are a number of new standards since the AES/EBU recommendation R128 that also describes properties of an audio signal that might be even more important than peak or RMS (namely LU, true peak, and dynamic range measurements).

All these more or less describe the amplitude of a signal and there are many more (for example, different frequency weighted measurements). Often, we care a lot more about the spectrum of a signal, hence the amplitudes of a signal\'s components, or even more sophisticated features of a signal.

The []{#ch02s03.html#id186 .indexterm}most important thing is to discover what we[]{#ch02s03.html#id187 .indexterm} really want to know about a signal and how to get this information.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch02s03.html#note10}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

With the given `wysiwyg.maxpat`{.literal} patch, look at all the tools, get to know them, and do some research if you feel you don\'t understand a concept behind a measurement. Before looking into the blackbox, try to guess what\'s in there and build what you think might be in there, or draw a block diagram!
:::
:::
:::
:::

[]{#ch02s04.html}

::: {.section title="A quick overview of GUI"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Let\'s get a []{#ch02s04.html#id188 .indexterm}basic overview of the GUI at this point. The Max GUI is not a static thing and we can\'t cover everything here, but it also is self-describing. The clue window, little pop ups, and the reference help us if there are any questions, but in order to be able to talk about things and to give some directions as to where to look for a certain thing, let\'s go through the interface using the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_02_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rfTXzbf.jpeg)
:::

The following[]{#ch02s04.html#id189 .indexterm} are the steps to install the Max GUI:

::: {.orderedlist}
1.  Lock/unlock the patchers / modify read-only. If the window shows a loaded abstraction, this symbol will show up. If we press the following button, then we can edit the patcher in place, loading the original temporarily:
    ::: {.mediaobject}
    ![9716OT_02_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/0wXiMfg.jpeg)
    :::
2.  The patcher windows: Here, we can access functions as opening another view of the patcher (for instance, when designing GUIs, it\'s really handy to have one locked view and one unlocked view open). Also, we can always open the original here if our patcher is loaded as an abstraction.
3.  Open the object [**Explorer**]{.strong}, which is also accessible via the shortcut key [*P*]{.emphasis}.
4.  The[]{#ch02s04.html#id190 .indexterm} shortcuts menu: You can also access this via the shortcut key [*X*]{.emphasis}. As stated before, we can modify these.
5.  The []{#ch02s04.html#id191 .indexterm}presentation mode: You can also switch between the presentation mode and the edit mode via [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + [*E*]{.emphasis}.
6.  Open the [**Inspector**]{.strong}, which is also accessible via [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*I*]{.emphasis}.
7.  Enable debugging. There is a section about debugging in this book.
8.  Show/hide the grid.
9.  Open the mixer panel. Here, we can mute the patchers\' DSP, see the patchers\' audio CPU usage, and so on.
10. Show/hide the status bar.
11. Open/close the side bar.
:::

The following are Max window-specific:

::: {.orderedlist}
1.  Clear the Max window.
2.  If you clicked on one of the column headings to order the printout by the Max object or message, here you can revert to ordering by time.
    ::: {.itemizedlist}
    -   If you selected a line in the Max window, this will open the [**Inspector**]{.strong} of the corresponding object
    -   If you selected a line in the Max window, this will highlight the corresponding object
    :::
3.  The status bar.
4.  Change the view of the side bar to [**Explorer**]{.strong}, [**Inspector**]{.strong}, [**Reference**]{.strong}, or the [**Max**]{.strong} window.
:::
:::

[]{#ch02s05.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

By now, we shouldn\'t feel lost in the world of Max. We learned how to get help, we saw how basic patching works, and got to know the building blocks, the Max object, the message box, and some GUI elements. In the next chapter, we will start off by building patches that have more relation to praxis, and we will explore Max in depth by the use of patches that actually might be useful and are not only of theoretical interest.
:::

[]{#ch03.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 3. Advanced Programming Techniques in Max"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We\'ve now got the basics out of the way. In fact, for anything that you see in the forthcoming chapters that you don\'t understand, you should have a couple of ideas in mind on how to get information. Therefore, we\'ll increase the tempo here a bit. We\'ll start off with getting into initialization more because it can be a frustrating thing if you aren\'t aware of some methods. After that, we\'ll cover message ordering, bpatchers, subpatchers, lists, and more. All of this will be shown using a practical example: a simple synthesizer. If you aren\'t interested in synthesis at all and if you are more the Musique concrete guy, to use this really old differentiation, you will learn even more if you just implement a sampler, for example, using the shown techniques!

We will be covering the following topics in this chapter:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Initializing a patcher
-   Microscopic timing and message ordering
-   Sending, receiving, and collecting data
-   Structuring the patchers
-   Timing in Max and prioritizing events
:::

::: {.section title="Introducing the synthesizer example"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

As[]{#ch03.html#id192 .indexterm} mentioned before, we\'ll start with a simple synthesizer. It\'s a subtractive one with two oscillators, `[saw~]`{.literal} and `[rect~]`{.literal}; a lowpass filter, `[lores~]`{.literal}; and a standard ADSR,`[adsr~]`{.literal}. In this chapter, we won\'t talk much about the actual synthesis or sound processing. We are going to use the synth to motivate us a bit to learn everything else that\'s needed around the synth and in many other occasions. While learning these methods, we are going to improve the synth in many ways, and as I said already, it doesn\'t really matter if it\'s a synth, a sampler or something else. In fact, I encourage you to build something else and apply the techniques discussed in the upcoming sections to it, rather than working with this synth. One last thing before we dive into it; don\'t expect this first little synth to sound unbelievably great. Again, we\'ll []{#ch03.html#id193 .indexterm}come to more sophisticated sound-processing topics in the next chapter.

So let\'s have a look at where we are going to start off:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/zy1Zoc0.jpeg)
:::

So, there are a lot of things we can talk about here. Basically, we have a MIDI input at the top and an audio output at the bottom, and more or less everything in between is our synth. If you open the patch `firstSynth_v001`{.literal}, you might not quite see what is shown here. In fact, you won\'t even be able to get a sound when clicking on the keyboard. Why? Think about it for a moment. First of all, audio might be switched off. Secondly, the cutoff of the lowpass filter is turned down completely, so only very low frequencies will be able to pass. However, I saved it the way it is shown here, not with all `[live.dial]`{.literal} objects at zero position. Why isn\'t that saved too?

Initializing values correctly and saving presets are two slightly different things; you can see that if we decide to manage values via presets and so on. We can also initialize them by recalling []{#ch03.html#id194 .indexterm}a preset. However, there are often many values that don\'t need to be controlled by presets or other storage management techniques but that need to be initialized. Let\'s first care for initialization, so when we fire up our patcher, we at least get some sound.
:::
:::

[]{#ch03s02.html}

::: {.section title="Initializing a patcher"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Initializing []{#ch03s02.html#id195 .indexterm}means to set up parameters of our patch at startup. There are a couple of ways to initialize values. Look at the following screenshot that depicts the `initialization.maxpat`{.literal} patcher:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/7EQDb8A.jpeg)
:::

In the []{#ch03s02.html#id196 .indexterm}patch, you can see 5 differently initialized versions of the same. The [**\[noise\]**]{.strong} patch[]{#ch03s02.html#id197 .indexterm} is a white noise generator, and [**\[onepole\]**]{.strong} is another lowpass filter (from left to right) and can be explained as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   No or rather default initialization (not recommended)
-   Initialization using initial attributes (through the inspector)
-   The[]{#ch03s02.html#id198 .indexterm} `[loadmess]`{.literal} object that fires out its argument when the patch is opened
-   The[]{#ch03s02.html#id199 .indexterm} `[loadbang]`{.literal} object that fires a bang when the patch\'s open end is connected to a `messagebox`{.literal} object, which in turn contains our initial value
-   Initialization of the processing part using an argument but no/default initialization of the GUI object
:::

The last one is particularly interesting; it can actually be considered as a bug (a bug we built, not Cycling\'74). It has been taken up in this list to re-emphasize the idea of initializing objects via arguments. In this case, we produced a conflict between the GUI and the []{#ch03s02.html#id200 .indexterm}processing; the GUI shows us that the filter has a cutoff of 0 Hz, but it actually has a cutoff of 1,000 Hz. As soon as we move the corresponding `[live.dial]`{.literal} object once, everything is synced again. So if it has this disadvantage, why do we do something like this? We will revisit this idea in the [*Structuring our patches*]{.emphasis} section. Essentially, it\'s about bidirectional communication. For now, stick to one of the other three methods, which only have minor differences from each other. Think about what\'s missing from the previous method and what the advantages might be.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s02.html#note11}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

With the new initialization techniques at hand, try to fix our synth! To turn on audio at startup, look up the `[adstatus]`{.literal} object or you can just send the integer 1 into `[dac~]`{.literal}. The target is to open our patch, hit a key, and want reasonable sound right away.
:::
:::

Here is another neat little trick about initialization; sometimes, we need some object (especially interface objects) to be set but don\'t want it to output its value. For this purpose, we have the `set`{.literal} message. Many objects behave similarly in reception of this message, as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/0grAbzc.jpeg)
:::

A word of caution about initialization; it can often be a source of errors. Wrong or a missing initialization can cause our patches to malfunction or even to crash on startup. To suppress the firing of all our `[loadbang]`{.literal} and `[loadmess]`{.literal} patchers, press and hold [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*shift*]{.emphasis} while opening it. Try this with the `debugging_initialization.maxpat`{.literal} patcher. After that, press the button in the upper-left hand corner to fire all of them with just one click. This little trick allows us to test our startup easily. You[]{#ch03s02.html#id201 .indexterm} can see the patcher in the following screenshot, and at its bottom, a more sophisticated initialization technique. If we need to order lots of processes in time on a macro level, we should consider implementing something like the startup sequence with the addition of an abstraction.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/oNeZCiM.jpeg)
:::

Take the bottom part of this patcher with a grain of salt. Mostly, if things have to be ordered in time, such as loading a sample and then querying its length, they shouldn\'t be done this way. The `[buffer~]`{.literal} object in which we can load samples, for example, sends out a[]{#ch03s02.html#id202 .indexterm} bang on its right outlet if it has finished with reading the file. So, even processes that take an indefinite amount of time should be ordered by logic and not by time. Doing things such as this startup sequence seems professional and can help if you take care of what you are doing. It\'s great if we load a lot of things dynamically, but we should try to stay away from these things, especially for smaller patchers. The `[trigger]`{.literal} object is our best friend when we have to put things in order. If we want something to happen after something else in terms of initialization, simply putting in a delay is unreliable and a bad idea. In seldom cases, we are forced to make use of `[defer]`{.literal}/`[deferlow]`{.literal} to move things to the low priority queue if we want a process to wait for things. Refer to the [*Event priority*]{.emphasis} section[]{#ch03s02.html#id203 .indexterm} for more information on the event queue and the scheduler.
:::

[]{#ch03s03.html}

::: {.section title="Excursus of microscopic timing and message ordering"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We have just mentioned the timing on a macroscopic level; let\'s quickly introduce one of the most []{#ch03s03.html#id204 .indexterm}important things we\'ll learn about Max: microscopic []{#ch03s03.html#id205 .indexterm}timing, as I call it, or message ordering. Consider this situation: we\'d like to use a MIDI `[notein]`{.literal} object to trigger a custom sequence or function. So, we know that we need to construct something that takes pitch and velocity and produces a bang (always a good design strategy; think what\'s coming in and out, and then what needs to be in there). Simple enough. However, `[notein]`{.literal} also sends note-off messages (a velocity of zero), so we need to filter those out. On an incoming velocity, we first need to test it (is it zero?) and then decide whether to pass it or not. That\'s what you can see in the following patcher; it\'s the contents of the `ourStripnote`{.literal} subpatcher contained in the `Micro_Timing_and_Message_Order.maxpat`{.literal} patcher:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/81eEWZH.jpeg)
:::

The first [*do this then do that*]{.emphasis} process is expressed by `[t i i]`{.literal}. It\'s an abbreviation for `[trigger i i]`{.literal}, and `[trigger]`{.literal} is an object that lets us manage timing. It takes its input and sends it out as the given data type ([**i**]{.strong} stands for integer) as many times as the argument is provided, in this case twice, and it sends everything in the right-to-left order, because Max is evaluating things in a right-to-left, depth-first order. So what happens here is that velocity comes in first (since `[notein]`{.literal}, as all the objects, sends out its right outlets first), then it\'s tested for unequality to zero, `[!= 0]`{.literal}, the result being either one or zero, which is sent to the gates and causes them to pass (or not) what\'s coming in their right inlets. Then, the velocity is sent to the right `[gate]`{.literal} object and out (or not), and then the pitch comes through (or not). What if we don\'t care about this? Max needs to order things at least somehow if we don\'t state the order of things explicitly, so it does it in the right-to-left order, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_09.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/RLpV4mq.jpeg)
:::

That\'s not []{#ch03s03.html#id206 .indexterm}what we want. We don\'t want to cause our patches to []{#ch03s03.html#id207 .indexterm}function differently if we were to move stuff around, say, for example, just to tidy up a bit. Study the mentioned patcher and the help-patcher of `[trigger]`{.literal} carefully; there is a reason why there is an abbreviation, `[t]`{.literal}, for it; we\'ll need it all the time.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s03.html#note12}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

Take another example of ordering; we want to detect the change of a running float. For example, we have somehow tracked 3D or 2D data of a person moving, and we just want to use the \"how much is she or he moving\" data to control the amplitude of a track or something similar. Essentially, we want to take the derivative of the data. Therefore, we\'d like to always subtract the last value received from the one at the input of our imagined derivative patch and output the result. Sounds simple, right? Beware; the simple things are the most important and hard ones. Give it a try though! There are multiple solutions but here is a possible hint: you\'ll need these objects: `[- 0.]`{.literal}, `[f ]`{.literal}, and `[trigger f b]`{.literal}, or just the `[- 0.]`{.literal} and `[t f f]`{.literal} objects.
:::
:::

The answer is also in the `Micro_Timing_and_Message_Order.maxpat`{.literal} patcher. Have you been able to make it? If yes, congratulations, you have constructed a simple high-pass filter and you are far ahead in the MSP/gen\~ section of the book!

But wait a moment; so all this is right to left? How unintuitive can this be for a person used to left-to-right writing systems? What\'s the logic here? The logic simply is that if we want the leftmost inlet to be the most important and hot one (following a left-to-right idea), the global right-to-left idea rises as a consequence. Therefore, we typically (take `[f]`{.literal} or `[*]`{.literal} as an example) first store a value at the right inlet and then trigger the output at the left inlet.
:::

[]{#ch03s04.html}

::: {.section title="A bpatcher for MIDI input"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Since we []{#ch03s04.html#id208 .indexterm}have fixed our initialization problems, let\'s look at the MIDI/GUI keyboard or what we could call the nput stage of our synthesizer patcher. For our convenience, both MIDI input and the `[kslider]`{.literal} object (so the keyboard) have been packed into an object called a `bpatcher`{.literal}. Bpatchers can load patchers that are saved in the search path, or they can be edited in place. To get familiar with them, create a new patcher, make a new object, and type in bpatcher. An empty square will appear. Now, we have two options. We can go to its inspector ([*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*I*]{.emphasis}) and enable [**embed patcher in parent**]{.strong}. Then, we can right-click on the bpatcher itself and choose [**Object → New view of \<none\>**]{.strong}. Now, we can edit the contents of the patcher. The second and more common way is to leave the embed patcher in the parent unchecked and provide a patcher file in the `bpatchers`{.literal} patcher attribute in its inspector. In this case, the `bpatcher`{.literal} object is not embedded; let\'s open it and look inside by right-clicking on it and choosing [**Object**]{.strong} \| [**new view of pl.keys.bp**]{.strong}, or if you\'d like to edit it, choose [**Open original**]{.strong}. If you open it, we first meet [**Presentation Mode**]{.strong}. We can toggle [**Presentation Mode**]{.strong} on and off using [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*E*]{.emphasis}. Obviously, presentation mode is made to hide our program and present a beautiful, reduced GUI. Objects can be added to presentation mode using their context menu or by selecting them and hitting [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*shift*]{.emphasis} + [*P*]{.emphasis}.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s04.html#note13}Note {.title}

In the patcher inspector ([*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + shift + [*I*]{.emphasis}), we can enable [**open in presentation mode**]{.strong}.
:::
:::

So let\'s look at how data is coming into our synth in this case:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/OFNAkD6.jpeg)
:::

Again, we won\'t go[]{#ch03s04.html#id209 .indexterm} over every object here in detail as you can easily find out everything about each of them. Two strange objects are here though: `[p mono_poly_switching]`{.literal} and `[p Mono_poly_process]`{.literal}. These aren\'t objects; they are what\'s called a subpatcher. If you double-click on them when in locked mode, you can see their contents. Subpatchers are simply encapsulated parts of a patch and we can create them by making a new object and pressing [*P*]{.emphasis} or typing `patcher`{.literal}. Optionally (highly recommended), we can give them a name as an argument. Another often used way to create these is to select a finished portion of a patch and go to [**Edit**]{.strong} \| [**Encapsulate**]{.strong} or press [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + [*shift*]{.emphasis} + [*E*]{.emphasis}. This even creates the inlets and outlets of the patcher automatically and connects them, if the selected portion is connected to things we didn\'t select.

As you can see, `[notein]`{.literal} is sending two things out to one of the subpatchers. First of all, notice that the patchcords are colored; this greatly improves readability and can be done via the [**context**]{.strong} menu of a patchcord. To select multiple patchcords, press [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + box or simply press [*shift*]{.emphasis} and select each of them.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s04.html#note14}Note {.title}

Readability is key. If you pay attention to it, you will find yourself reading and maintaining a lot more time than writing code. So keep it tidy to have an easier life! Producing chaotic code is very common when experimenting and can be very inspiring and fast. Cleaning it up afterwards makes our experiments usable in the future though.
:::
:::

So what are those two things coming out of `[notein]`{.literal}? They are pitch and velocity, both of which []{#ch03s04.html#id210 .indexterm}range from 0 to 127. Before we think about how these got combined (there\'s just one cord coming out of the `bpatcher`{.literal} object, and we\'ll learn about this in the [*Collections of data*]{.emphasis} section in this chapter), let\'s think about data coming into Max in general.
:::

[]{#ch03s05.html}

::: {.section title="Sending and receiving data"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We got []{#ch03s05.html#id211 .indexterm}a whole range of objects for this purpose, and `[notein]`{.literal} is just one[]{#ch03s05.html#id212 .indexterm} of them. In the following screenshot, you can see the most common objects in that regard:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/t9IYhgE.jpeg)
:::

In the preceding patcher, `getting_data_into_max.maxpat`{.literal} is presenting us with some possibilities if we want to communicate with our patchers. Very often, we will need MIDI, but []{#ch03s05.html#id213 .indexterm}the use of [**Open Sound Control**]{.strong} ([**OSC**]{.strong}) is increasing for good reasons; it has the potential to be faster, has a higher resolution (no limit of 7-bit messages, that is 0 to 127), and we don\'t need to remember that we sent the filter cutoff on CC 84 on channel 13 because the message simply might be `/synth1/filter/cutoff 1000`{.literal}. For OSC communication over the network or locally between applications, we use `[udpsend]`{.literal}. We\'ll use it later a lot also to communicate between Max and TouchDesigner. The `[serial]`{.literal} object is used to do serial communication obviously. While [**USB**]{.strong} stands for [**Universal Serial Bus**]{.strong}, standard USB devices won\'t work unless there is a USB to serial adapter[]{#ch03s05.html#id214 .indexterm} in between. Later, we will use this object when Arduino comes into play, for example (most versions of Arduino have a built-in adapter).

Sometimes, we []{#ch03s05.html#id215 .indexterm}also want to communicate from one patcher to another, although[]{#ch03s05.html#id216 .indexterm} these are totally independent (they are not subpatchers in the same top-level patcher or the like, but they are both top-level patchers). Open the `open_two_independent_patchers.maxpat`{.literal} patch.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s05.html#note15}Note {.title}

This `open_two_independent_patchers.maxpat`{.literal} patch will appear only for a split second, open the two patcher files, and then close itself. Some pretty advanced techniques are used in there, but go on and inspect it! However, if you want to take a look, you\'ll have to suppress the `[loadbang]`{.literal} object in there and close it. Remember how?
:::
:::

What we see are two patches (depicted in the following screenshot) that are independent; they could be just two `.maxpat`{.literal} files lying around anywhere on our filesystem. However, the objects used in these are connecting them (`[udpsend]`{.literal} and `[udpreceive]`{.literal} could also have been used in addition).

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Q095emv.jpeg)
:::

Remember the `[send]`{.literal} object we saw in the patcher shown in the preceding screenshot? It also appeared in the heart of our MIDI bpatcher. There, it was used to send a value from one central []{#ch03s05.html#id217 .indexterm}place, a GUI object, to many places (in this case, just two, both []{#ch03s05.html#id218 .indexterm}within subpatchers). This is a common technique used to avoid what\'s called spaghetti code. I guess you can imagine what\'s meant by that. To improve readability of cordless connections, it\'s common to color both the send and receive signals with the same. Also, by double-clicking on either send or receive, you can find other occurrences of the same send/receive context.
:::

[]{#ch03s06.html}

::: {.section title="The [[n]] notation"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

The `send`{.literal} and `receive`{.literal} objects within the MIDI bpatcher had a strange arguments: `#0-mono-poly`{.literal}. The `#`{.literal} notation[]{#ch03s06.html#id219 .indexterm} allows us to do some very neat things: `#1`{.literal} to `#9`{.literal} accesses the first nine arguments given to the patcher if loaded as an abstraction, and `#0`{.literal} provides us with a unique random number if the patcher is loaded as an abstraction. Open the `Pound-Sign-Examples.maxpat`{.literal} patcher. It doesn\'t do a lot but simply loads the `pound-sign-examples_abstr.maxpat`{.literal} abstraction. You can see both of them in the following screenshot. However, if you double-click on the abstraction to view its interior, you shouldn\'t see the same thing until you unlock the patcher. This is because until you unlock the patch, it is loaded as an abstraction and therefore all the `#`{.literal} numbers are replaced.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_10.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/EFpuPPx.jpeg)
:::

So, that\'s []{#ch03s06.html#id220 .indexterm}pretty handy right? However, what do we need random numbers for? We often build abstractions with the aim of loading multiple instances of them. So, if we naively used `[send]`{.literal} and `[receive]`{.literal} without this notation, we would create interferences between multiple instances; each `send`{.literal} object of each instance will be sending to each occurrence of the corresponding `receive`{.literal} object. Often, we want `send`{.literal} and `receive`{.literal} to only communicate within a single loaded instance.
:::

[]{#ch03s07.html}

::: {.section title="Collections of data"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Now, let\'s []{#ch03s07.html#id221 .indexterm}find out how our initial two patchcords (pitch and velocity) in the MIDI part of our synth became one. Of course, they were packed into a list by an object that\'s conveniently called `[pack]`{.literal}. Lists are great. However, there are not only lists that consist of two integers; for example, there is the `[coll]`{.literal} object that manages larger amounts of data, but also dictionaries in Max 6. Look at the `list_examples.maxpat`{.literal} patchers, which are also depicted in the following screenshot, to get into[]{#ch03s07.html#id222 .indexterm} lists a bit, and `datatypes.maxpat`{.literal} (which is not depicted here) to get a feeling of datatypes and the conversion between them:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/abgrSfS.jpeg)
:::

We will meet lists often and get more and more familiar with them. The key objects are `[pack]`{.literal}/`[unpack]`{.literal}, `[pak]`{.literal}, `[join]`{.literal}/`[unjoin]`{.literal}, `[vexpr]`{.literal}, `[zl]`{.literal}, `[append]`{.literal}/`[prepend]`{.literal}, `[route]`{.literal}, `[table]`{.literal}, `[itable]`{.literal}, `[coll]`{.literal}, and the `[dict]`{.literal} family. To look at a more concrete and usable example of some list-related techniques, let\'s look at the `midi_abbr_configure.maxpat`{.literal} patcher:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_12.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/m58PlaM.jpeg)
:::

This patcher might turn out to be useful if you have a lot of MIDI devices and use abbreviations. Remember that I suggested that you use abbreviations but had to admit that Max tends to forget these? Max forgets these if we open it without the device connected, but we can remind it. We can simply write all of our abbreviations down once and can automatically restore them using this patch. Also, we can start this patch automatically before our []{#ch03s07.html#id223 .indexterm}actual performance patch, for example, just to make sure everything is configured nicely. If you look at the example with the independent top-level `open_two_independent_patchers.maxpat`{.literal} patchers again, in combination with all the trickery we thought about audio settings, you might try to build a patch that first loads a patch that is just there to configure things properly, starts your main performance patch, and closes itself again. Let\'s go through this patch one by one now.

The patch consists of twice the same thing: once for MIDI inlets, and other for outlets. The core here really is the `[coll]`{.literal} object. If you double-click on it, you can see the following code in there:

::: {.informalexample}
``` {.programlisting}
1, "Kenton Killamix Mini" a;
2, "MS-20 Control IN" z;
3, Launchpad i;
4, "1:UM-3G 1" s;
5, "1:UM-3G 2" c;
6, "1:UM-3G 3" d;
7, "828mk3 #3 MIDI Port" g;
8, "to Max 1" m;
9, "to Max 2" n;
10, "MS-20 Synth IN" b;
```
:::

It\'s simply a collection of port names and abbreviations I typed in there. Using the `[coll]`{.literal} object\'s attribute `@embed 1`{.literal}, the data gets saved with the patcher (we could also save it as a `.txt`{.literal} file). So this essentially is the memory we added to Max. Now, we just have to get the data out and tell Max which port should have what abbreviation. We want to dump all the data. We first need to know how many items are in the list, since `coll`{.literal} accepts integers to query data, and we don\'t want to produce errors because of querying items that don\'t exist. There is just one problem; `[coll]`{.literal} sends out the number of items in the same outlet as the actual data. This seems to make things complicated, so we are using the fact that the named `[coll]`{.literal} object contains the same data; they are instances of the same data. In the following screenshot, you can see an illustration of the concept of instancing data. We can store data in one `[coll]`{.literal} object named `foo`{.literal} to retrieve it from another one with the same name.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_31.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/JSKLlrJ.jpeg)
:::

Therefore, the []{#ch03s07.html#id224 .indexterm}procedure is clear; we use one instance to query the number of lines contained in `[coll]`{.literal}. We send this info to a `[uzi]`{.literal} object, which in this case is used to just count to that number as fast as Max can. This count is sent to our data, querying each line and unpacking the list that is found on each line, for example, to Max 1 m is the data found in line 8. That\'s a list of two symbols.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s07.html#note16}Note {.title}

Every entry can be a list right? Therefore, `coll`{.literal} can absolutely be used to store two-dimensional data, for example.
:::
:::

We need to unpack the list because Max will want us to send the letter to be used as an abbreviation in the form of an integer. So [**m**]{.strong} would, for example, need to be converted to 13. Then, we pack those things together again (the symbol and our new abbreviation integer) and send it to this weird message box that contains this:

::: {.informalexample}
``` {.programlisting}
;
max midi portabbrev innum $1 $2
```
:::

This message box seems to be going nowhere.
:::

[]{#ch03s08.html}

::: {.section title="More message box magic"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

What\'s[]{#ch03s08.html#id225 .indexterm} happening is that, as we have seen already, the message box is a very special object. We can also use it to send things to the `[receive]`{.literal} objects, or as in this instance, communicate with Max itself using the semicolon. Look at the `Message_box_morefeatures.maxpat`{.literal} patcher depicted in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_14.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Y8s4iIi.jpeg)
:::

Here, some []{#ch03s08.html#id226 .indexterm}possibilities are shown that arise from the semicolon notation. For more information about what we can do by communicating with Max itself, visit <http://cycling74.com/docs/max6/dynamic/c74_docs.html#messages_to_max>, or simply[]{#ch03s08.html#id227 .indexterm} click on the big message box on the top of the patcher; it opens your default browser, leading directly to that site.
:::

[]{#ch03s09.html}

::: {.section title="Structuring our patches"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We have []{#ch03s09.html#id228 .indexterm}now looked at a lot of techniques that will hopefully inspire you to improve our initial synthesizer patch, or your own instrument, sampler, and so on. If you haven\'t, I recommend that you do this. We\'ll now look at an upgraded version of the synth, and if you\'ve tried it yourself already, you\'ll become more creative in solving Max problems.

Although the synth is a very small nice patch, we\'ll treat it as if it were a bigger one, and identify problems we had with the initial version, as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Initialization
-   Poor overall structure, which was a bit spaghettish
-   No modular, reusable parts
-   No option to save and recall parameters
-   No real GUI
-   Besides pitch and velocity, there is no interfacing to controllers (sequencers in Max and external MIDI controllers)
-   Scalability (easy to expand and duplicate without adding a great amount of chaos?)
-   Debuggability
:::

So I hope you have improved our initial patch along the way a bit already; now, let\'s look at the next []{#ch03s09.html#id229 .indexterm}version here, in which some of the problems have been attacked already. Open the `firstSynth_root_v002.maxpat`{.literal} patch; again, it\'s depicted in the following screenshot too:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_15.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/4PDtzyL.jpeg)
:::

You have probably arrived at something similar already. Here, initialization has been taken care of via `[loadmess]`{.literal} to switch audio on, always using arguments where needed, and all parameters (the dials) are given initial values via the `initial`{.literal} attribute.

Also, the whole structure has changed. The actual synth has been packed into an abstraction called `firstSynth_proc`{.literal}, depicted in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_16.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TZvbJmS.jpeg)
:::

Also, the []{#ch03s09.html#id230 .indexterm}dials have been packed into an abstraction and loaded into a `[bpatcher]`{.literal} object. This `control`{.literal} patcher can be seen in the following screenshot and is called `firstSynth_GUI.maxpat`{.literal}. Notice that all colors of the GUI are still at their default []{#ch03s09.html#id231 .indexterm}values. This makes it very easy to design a consistent GUI later using [**object defaults**]{.strong}.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_17.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/MJNjKdc.jpeg)
:::

Three objects appear in these patches that haven\'t been introduced: `[osc-route]`{.literal}, `[js]`{.literal}, and the `[preset]`{.literal} object (the GUI object at the bottom of the following screenshot). However, before talking about these, let\'s briefly think about why this has been structured this way. The []{#ch03s09.html#id232 .indexterm}structure of patches is a problem for which many strategies and opinions exist and which highly depends on the scale and idea underlying a patch. Structuring a project like this introduces a lot of objects that might seem unnecessary, and indeed, adding a huge amount of structuring to a small patcher is not a very good idea. It simply makes things more complicated than necessary. In programming, we try to write as little code as possible, as much as necessary. According to Ken Thompson:

::: {.blockquote}
> \"One of my most productive days was throwing away 1000 lines of code.\"
:::

This being said, we try to find structures for bigger-scale projects here. One of the most common principles is to at least divide a patch into processing and GUI. This is highly recommended and has been done in this case. Why? Because it makes both of them reusable and easily editable. At least three things have to be considered if we want to ensure reusability:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   [**Information hiding**]{.strong}: This is the idea that the GUI is implementation agnostic. Our GUI in[]{#ch03s09.html#id233 .indexterm} this case doesn\'t care if we are []{#ch03s09.html#id234 .indexterm}deciding to choose a different oscillator, filter, and so on. The important thing here is that we think about what parts are likely to change in the future. These need a communication interface to the controller (GUI) that is not likely to change.
-   [**A proper protocol**]{.strong}: The communication between the processing and the GUI should[]{#ch03s09.html#id235 .indexterm} follow some standard. Sometimes, an ad hoc protocol will prepend each value with an identifier, retrieving the value using `[route]`{.literal}. In this case, OSC has been chosen, using the `[osc-route]`{.literal} CNMAT library external and the `osc-unroute`{.literal} JavaScript also provided by CNMAT. The good thing is that we can simply throw away our GUI and hook up another application (that supports OSC) that controls our processing directly. Another possibility would be to completely stick to the MIDI standards, for example (which we actually partly do; we send in pitch and velocity into the MIDI range).
-   [**The interface**]{.strong}: In this case, we have chosen to give our processing part of the synth two []{#ch03s09.html#id236 .indexterm}inlets, one for the notes and another for the parameters. This is due to a variety of reasons but mainly it\'s due to the possibility of moving on to wrapping it into a `[poly~]`{.literal} object, which does automatic voice allocation for us and provides other advantages as we will soon see. When working with `[poly~]`{.literal}, we\'ll find that it\'s very convenient this way. Nevertheless, as you can see, we implemented a central single communication line; the `[s ctrl]-[r ctrl]`{.literal} send and receive pair. This makes it a lot easier to provide a different UI than if we, for example, had an inlet for each parameter in the processing patch. Also, I\'m sure you have already []{#ch03s09.html#id237 .indexterm}noticed that the whole communication is sent to a `print`{.literal} object if we choose so by checking the [**enable debugging**]{.strong} toggle.
:::

So now []{#ch03s09.html#id238 .indexterm}you know (if you haven\'t at least assumed already) what the `osc`{.literal} objects are doing. What\'s `[preset]`{.literal}? Tough question, hmm? It stores the parameters in our GUI patcher, providing us with presets (in this case, presets are stored with the GUI patcher(!) file). We can click on the little buttons to recall a preset and press [*Shift*]{.emphasis} and click on it to save one. The[]{#ch03s09.html#id239 .indexterm} `[preset]`{.literal} object is nice for smaller projects but not as powerful as the `[pattr]`{.literal} family.

::: {.section title="The pattr family – a communication system"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch03s09.html#ch03lvl2sec21}The pattr family -- a communication system {.title}
:::

If you []{#ch03s09.html#id240 .indexterm}open the `firstSynth_root_pattr.maxpat`{.literal} patch, you will hardly notice any difference. What happened is[]{#ch03s09.html#id241 .indexterm} that the `[preset]`{.literal} object is now merely a GUI object that []{#ch03s09.html#id242 .indexterm}controls a `[pattrstorage]`{.literal} object. The `[pattrstorage]`{.literal} object []{#ch03s09.html#id243 .indexterm}now stores our presets; if you double-click on it, you can see all the objects it is aware of, as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_18.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/K1iWMp2.jpeg)
:::

The first line in this window, [**GUI**]{.strong}, simply stands for our `bpatcher`{.literal} object, which now has the scripting name, [**GUI**]{.strong}. The `[pattrstorage]`{.literal} object is aware of these objects, but is not aware of the menu in our `bpatcher`{.literal} MIDI input, our `[live.gain]`{.literal} object, and others. This is because we made it aware by modifying our initial GUI patcher. Although this new one looks nearly the same, two changes have been made; the `preset`{.literal} object has been taken []{#ch03s09.html#id244 .indexterm}out of there and an `[autopattr]`{.literal} object has been inserted. The `[autopattr]`{.literal} object, by default, exposes all named objects (objects given a scripting name in their inspector) within its patcher level (not above or its subpatchers) to the pattr system. The `pattrstorage`{.literal} object, for example, allows us to interpolate between[]{#ch03s09.html#id245 .indexterm} presets, gives us more control on what values are saved, and more control in general compared to the `[preset]`{.literal} object on its own.

Another difference in this version of our synth is that there is MIDI input that controls our attack parameter. We use `[pattmarker]`{.literal} to make our root patch globally available for the []{#ch03s09.html#id246 .indexterm}pattr system in order to access []{#ch03s09.html#id247 .indexterm}the `attack`{.literal} dial via a `[pattr]`{.literal} object in the subpatcher []{#ch03s09.html#id248 .indexterm}called `Midi_CC_to_Attack`{.literal} whose contents are depicted in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_19.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/pRiCULq.jpeg)
:::

This simply sends the received value to the `attack`{.literal} dial (after it has been brought to the appropriate range). Also, we don\'t want to save the value that this `[pattr]`{.literal} object holds, since it is the same as the dial we already have on our client list of `[pattrstorage]`{.literal}. That\'s why we set [**\@invisible 1**]{.strong}. However, do we really want to send our MIDI CCs to a GUI object and from there to the actual parameter? Well, we do want to save the values received or rather the parameters set by the incoming MIDI, but we don\'t want the values to run through a GUI object. It\'s not a big problem, but we\'ll find a different method soon, solving other problems as well.

Now, we have arrived at a point in which we can be quite happy with our structure, but what if we create an LFO that\'s supposed to modulate the filter and add a sequencer that produces notes? Both of these are controlled by either MIDI or the GUI, and they also control the processing or even each other. Where to put them, or how to think about such a structure?

Well, there []{#ch03s09.html#id249 .indexterm}is no definite answer. However, there are interesting systems, such as the [**Model-View-Controller**]{.strong} ([**MVC**]{.strong}) system. This helps us both to think about how to develop a system and gives a suggestion on how to implement it. The `[pattr]`{.literal} family can provide us with the global communication bus. Let\'s first consider a most basic example, the `MVC.maxpat`{.literal} patcher, shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_20.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/p55QXpx.jpeg)
:::

As you can see, we split up our patch in three parts instead of just two. The MVC system is a concept that is supposed to help us; there is nothing wrong with just connecting the MIDI input directly into the oscillator. However, we do have to rescale the value, and we most likely []{#ch03s09.html#id250 .indexterm}will want to save the state; we[]{#ch03s09.html#id251 .indexterm} probably want to inspect its value on the screen and modify it []{#ch03s09.html#id252 .indexterm}there, but it should also react to MIDI/OSC or something similar. If we want all that, well there we are, this is a system that provides us with all that and a lot more due to the power of `[pattr]`{.literal}.

If we now add an LFO or other audio-rate modulation sources, an indicator on the dial that shows us the actual value of the parameter (the dials adjustment plus the modulation), a modulation matrix, and preset interpolation, we might end up with something like the `matrix_pattr_1.maxpat`{.literal} patcher. Beware that, the indication of the actual value might not be that important and makes the whole construction more complicated and CPU intense (although, there is room for optimization, for example, to convert the signal to a Max domain message, which is increasing the `[snapshot]`{.literal} object\'s attribute). In the following screenshot, one part of the patcher and one possible implementation of the system are shown. Both LFOs in that patcher are essentially constructed the same way as the depicted oscillator, but as much as possible has been packed into two abstractions: `mod_helper`{.literal} and `mod_param`{.literal}.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_21.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/aIwQVvE.jpeg)
:::

The top []{#ch03s09.html#id253 .indexterm}part of the preceding screenshot []{#ch03s09.html#id254 .indexterm}shows the [**view**]{.strong} part of the patch; the bottom part is corresponding []{#ch03s09.html#id255 .indexterm}to the [**model**]{.strong} part. As you can see, the small orange indicator shows a different (the actual) value of the frequency the oscillator has. I recommend that you look at the patch itself; it\'s a bit too big to be depicted here in a useful way. Of course, the different parts of such MVC systems can be in different abstractions too.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s09.html#note17}Note {.title}

As an exercise, implement a MVC system using subpatches or abstractions. Make one patch for the view, one for the model, and one for the controller. Either take the `matrix_pattr_1`{.literal} patch or use one of your own. Take care, as the paths have to be changed and you have to assign scripting names to the `[patch]`{.literal} objects that contain your abstractions/subpatchers.
:::
:::

Before we conclude with this section, notice both the `[matrix]`{.literal} object used to dispatch audio []{#ch03s09.html#id256 .indexterm}signals and the `[matrixctrl]`{.literal} GUI object. The `[matrixctrl]`{.literal} object is very versatile; if you create it, it will look quite different and work as only binary (`On`{.literal}/`Off`{.literal} instead of attenuating). Study its helpfile and try to set up a `[matrixctrl]`{.literal} object []{#ch03s09.html#id257 .indexterm}like the one in the `matrix_pattr_1`{.literal} patch! The[]{#ch03s09.html#id258 .indexterm} key is to provide a correct file in the [**Cell Image File**]{.strong} attribute in[]{#ch03s09.html#id259 .indexterm} its inspector to activate [**dial mode**]{.strong} and configure the [**cell range**]{.strong} attribute. To find the correct image file, look at the one used here and the `[matrixctrl]`{.literal} object\'s helpfile. Also, consider using the freeware[]{#ch03s09.html#id260 .indexterm} [**knobman**]{.strong} to create your own, which can be found at <http://www.g200kg.com/en/software/knobman.html>.

So, we have now seen some strategies to structure our ideas beforehand and also for implementation. The size and complexity of a project very much determines what kind of sophistication is demanded in structure. So, again, there is nothing wrong with doing things more simply. However, as soon as things can be reusable (as soon as something works properly) or scalable and expandable, one should spend some time thinking about the structure. Also, the concepts presented here are just proposals. There are many ways to structure things and there are many ways to use the pattr system. You should visit <http://mspcafe.g2312.de/tLb/framework/patchdesign/> for more information on MVC by Thomas Seelig, who also was an inspiration for this section.
:::
:::

[]{#ch03s10.html}

::: {.section title="Timing in Max"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We have []{#ch03s10.html#id261 .indexterm}mentioned sequencers and LFOs already, so let\'s start giving our little synth more dynamic behavior. So far, we have only worked with milliseconds as units of time but there is more: metric timing, samples, and ticks (scheduler ticks, which is the smallest available unit of time in the Max domain). Look at the following screenshot in which our metro there has been given the argument [**4n**]{.strong}, meaning quarter notes instead of a number in milliseconds. Also, we tell it to be quantized by the same amount, so it doesn\'t only have the interval of a quarter note but fires on the actual quarter notes of Max\'s internal global transport.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_22.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/mWXpmuR.jpeg)
:::

The state[]{#ch03s10.html#id262 .indexterm} of global transport can be viewed by navigating to [**Extras**]{.strong} \| [**Global transport**]{.strong} by double-clicking on the `[transport]`{.literal} object or by querying a `[transport]`{.literal} object, as shown in the following screenshot.

We can see a very basic 8-step sequencer, the `seq1.maxpat`{.literal} patch. By layering the two `[multislider]`{.literal} objects in presentation mode, we get a simple step indicator. If we refine this patch, we are good to go with sticking it into a `bpatcher`{.literal} object and controlling a synth (although, I don\'t recommend having a sequencer\'s clock inside instead of keeping things more modular. However, for the sake of this tutorial, let\'s stick to that for now.). If you open the `firstSyth_root_v003`{.literal} patch, you\'ll see both notes and the cutoff being modulated, and hopefully at this point, you think \"so that\'s what I\'m doing this for\", although the synth itself still sounds a little poor of course. We can get quite a lot of variety out of just three little basic sequencers. Note that our `firstSynth_proc`{.literal} patch has also been modified. When we control MSP things out of the Max world, we need to smooth things a bit. This is what has been changed in there with a custom abstraction that contains a `[line~]`{.literal} object. Why do we have to smooth things? Well, that\'s how audio behaves; sudden jumps are equal to high frequency content, so if we change a filter\'s cutoff frequency rapidly without interpolating, we are causing clicks.

Now, timing in Max is quite straightforward, but how about syncing external stuff or syncing Max to external software such as a DAW? Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_23.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/qGqn93f.jpeg)
:::

Here, we []{#ch03s10.html#id263 .indexterm}can see parts of the `clock-sync_in-out.maxpat`{.literal} patcher. If you take a look at the patcher and wonder about the numbers 250, 252, and 248, refer[]{#ch03s10.html#id264 .indexterm} to the Wikipedia page about the MIDI beat clock at <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIDI_beat_clock>.

These are just messages to say start, stop, and a clock tick. We can have many `[transport]`{.literal} objects in Max, either all referring to the same transport state, or by giving them names, with which we can make them independent.
