Many objects can in turn be told to refer to[]{#ch03s10.html#id265 .indexterm} a specific transport via the `@transport`{.literal} attribute. There are quite a few new objects in this patch; study them carefully. Timing is an important topic and you will want to be comfortable with syncing things up. Also, when you try to sync external environments, or when working with timing in general, don\'t forget about the settings we discussed to get accurate timing in Max (overdrive, I/O vector size, in audio interrupt, but also the advanced scheduler settings).

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch03s10.html#note18}Note {.title}

Now, look into `[live.step]`{.literal} and try to set up a simple synth/sequencer patch that can be synced to an external clock!
:::
:::

Now, before finishing this part about sequencers and timing (there are many more timing-related objects we haven\'t talked about, such as `[translate]`{.literal}, `[hostsync~]`{.literal} for rewire, and many more), let\'s look into a more generative approach to sequencers. Open the `adv_seq_1.maxpat`{.literal} patch. You will be presented with a little synth and a couple of `bpatchers`{.literal} objects. If you look into the sequencers, you will find that there is quite some list-processing happening. The idea behind the sequencer is to create an ever-changing sequence based on an adjustable one. Have a look at the sequencer in the following screenshot; we have one (green) sequence that we can adjust, but the gray one that lies behind is actually used. This gray sequence is always changing if adjusted that way, but tries to stick to the green one.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_24.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/6rzNih3.jpeg)
:::

Sadly, there[]{#ch03s10.html#id266 .indexterm} is not enough space here to explain the contents thoroughly, but this should motivate you to get into `[uzi]`{.literal}, `[coll]`{.literal}, and their list-processing friends. If you think \"man, I\'d like to create some crazy sequencers\", maybe you should also think \"I really have to learn some list-processing\" because, well, a sequencer is something that queries entries of a possibly interesting list (or multidimensional data? Analog sequencers often output many values per step, for example, triggers, gates and multiple CVs) in a possibly interesting way. In this case, it\'s the sequence itself that could be described as interesting; the way it is queried is completely boring. You can probably think of a better way.

I\'d like to give you an example of more interesting ways to query data. There are things such as Markov chains and other more sophisticated sequencing techniques that rely on variable data, or data that has more sophisticated features such as probabilities and so on. Look into the help-patch of the `[anal]`{.literal} object for an example of a Markov chain. Here\'s an example:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_25.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/OywtiFZ.jpeg)
:::

What you can see in the preceding screenshot is an attempt to think about multidimensional sequencers. Here, the sequencing is 3D; 2 to the power of 3 makes eight steps. This []{#ch03s10.html#id267 .indexterm}might not be the most usable sequencer on earth, but it probably inspires you to implement other ways of going through a couple of values also, instead of just thinking about creating interesting values.
:::

[]{#ch03s11.html}

::: {.section title="The event priority"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

The event[]{#ch03s11.html#id268 .indexterm} priority in Max is something one might not be aware of all the time, but having an idea about it helps a lot. To put it simply, there is a high priority thread (often called the scheduler) and a low priority thread (often called the queue) for Max messages if we turn on [*Overdrive*]{.emphasis}. Otherwise, these are executed in one thread, with the same priority. Max just has two lists of things to do and does the things on both lists, but it can interrupt things being done on the low-priority list, to first finish the high-priority thread. What events are in which thread? Things such as GUI interaction get in the low-priority thread while MIDI input, for example, is in the high priority one. We can use the `[deferlow]`{.literal} object[]{#ch03s11.html#id269 .indexterm} to place an event at the end of the low-priority queue, or `[defer]`{.literal}, to put it at the top of the low-priority list. The `[defer]`{.literal} object, therefore, might mess up the order of events since there most likely are things on the low-priority list to be done and we just jump ahead (but staying in low-priority) using `[defer]`{.literal}. To put an event into high priority coming from low priority, we can use [*(ab)use*]{.emphasis} `[delay]`{.literal} or `[pipe]`{.literal}. Only high-priority events contain timing information, so by adding a very small delay, we generate this information. Again, most of the time, we don\'t care a lot about these things, but if you are doing extremely intensive tasks very fast, the computer becomes slow and other processes get disturbed; this should ring a bell in your head. Maybe you just need to defer things. Look at the help-patcher of `[defer]`{.literal} for a nice example with `[uzi]`{.literal} that slows down your computer unnecessarily. For []{#ch03s11.html#id270 .indexterm}more in-depth information about the scheduler, visit <http://cycling74.com/2004/09/09/event-priority-in-max-scheduler-vs-queue/>.

Also, the help system provides some useful insights, which can be found at <http://cycling74.com/docs/max6/dynamic/c74_docs.html#scheduler_preferences>.
:::

[]{#ch03s12.html}

::: {.section title="Debugging"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Finally, let\'s now []{#ch03s12.html#id271 .indexterm}talk about how to fix problems. You probably arrived at this section early because you ran into problems along the way. Everything presented here is quite basic, quite boring, and very important.

::: {.section title="Smart ways to debug"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch03s12.html#ch03lvl2sec22}Smart ways to debug {.title}
:::

It has to be said here. Everybody produces chaos sometimes. Max is great as a rapid prototyping tool, but if we want to understand our patches in two weeks or even just after taking a break, we []{#ch03s12.html#id272 .indexterm}need to keep things tidy and add comments. I can present a completely chaotic patch here as compared to a nice and tidy one, but I think you know how they look. It has been stated already that we read a lot more code than we write, so to make things simple and tidy things up. I personally often do this when I\'m stuck. I don\'t mean when I\'m really stuck, but there are moments in which one is a bit distracted or moments in which one loses interest in a problem. These are good opportunities for you to take some time to tidy up slightly. If I\'m really stuck, everything is tidy already, otherwise I\'d see hope. In this case, there are some options left: go for a walk, ask somebody else, or delete everything and start from scratch. All these can lead to success in a surprisingly fast way most of the time. If you have nobody to ask, ask the forum or explain your problem to somebody you think is able to help you. Simply talking about a problem is often enough to start thinking in a different way about it.
:::

::: {.section title="The debugger"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch03s12.html#ch03lvl2sec23}The debugger {.title}
:::

We already[]{#ch03s12.html#id273 .indexterm} talked about the `[print]`{.literal} object. This object and its []{#ch03s12.html#id274 .indexterm}counterparts in MSP, such as `[meter~]`{.literal}, `[number~]`{.literal}, or `[jit.pwindow]`{.literal} and `[jit.fpsgui]`{.literal} for Jitter are our most powerful tools in order to know what\'s happening. We have already mentioned that there are many ways to display features of audio and video signals. Also, don\'t forget to enable probing in the [**debug**]{.strong} menu; this is really helpful. For Max processes, there is something else that might help us, especially when we deal with feedback and branching. The debugger is shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_26.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/o3CYvxy.jpeg)
:::

We can access[]{#ch03s12.html#id275 .indexterm} the debugger by navigating to [**Debug**]{.strong} \| [**enable debugging**]{.strong} and []{#ch03s12.html#id276 .indexterm}it also has a little icon in the toolbar, so we can switch it on there. To let the debugger do something, we need to add the so-called [**Watchpoints**]{.strong}. We []{#ch03s12.html#id277 .indexterm}can do this by right-clicking on a patch cord and choose either [**Add Watchpoint -- Break**]{.strong} or [**Add Watchpoint -- Monitor**]{.strong}. All the little orange and red dots in the preceding screenshot are such Watchpoints. The red ones are `break`{.literal} watchpoints, and the orange ones are of the `watch`{.literal} type, as you can also see in the [**Watchpoints**]{.strong} window. This window can be accessed by navigating to [**Debug**]{.strong} \| [**Watchpoints**]{.strong}. So what []{#ch03s12.html#id278 .indexterm}does it do? The `break`{.literal} watchpoints allow us to step through what\'s happening. They stop Max from executing further until we tell it to go on. Meanwhile, we can inspect values and see where the things are going and coming from. The debugger is also a great tool to understand what I call microscopic timing in the preceding screenshot. If you rebuild this little patch or a similar one, you will notice that the feedback connection going up into the cold inlet of the `[+]`{.literal} operator is happening one step before it\'s going to the `[number]`{.literal} object. The debugger is quite new to Max, and I have the impression that people who have used Max for a while have developed their bug-finding strategies already and don\'t use it a lot. Nevertheless, it is a very intuitive and helpful tool, so try it out!
:::

::: {.section title="Optimizing"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch03s12.html#ch03lvl2sec24}Optimizing {.title}
:::

Somebody []{#ch03s12.html#id279 .indexterm}once stated that optimizing is not about small details but []{#ch03s12.html#id280 .indexterm}about intelligent algorithms. While that might be true and one should sometimes question whether the whole idea behind a patch is efficient, there are most likely lots of small improvements one can make. In the Max domain, this often has something to do if things are done at the right point in time and not too often. If we need a certain number, we shouldn\'t be computing it all the time, but compute it once, store it, and query it[]{#ch03s12.html#id281 .indexterm} as often as we like obviously. In computing, there generally[]{#ch03s12.html#id282 .indexterm} is often the possibility to trade-off memory versus CPU. For example, the `[cycle~]`{.literal} object is a wavetable. Cycling \'74 decided to compute a good sine wave once and store it, and we can read it out as often as we like. Actually, computing the `sine`{.literal} function is a very complicated CPU-intensive task. There is, however, a `gen~`{.literal} patch, `gen~.computed_sine.maxpat`{.literal}, in the `Max 6/examples/gen/`{.literal} folder. Now, if we have no idea of which one is better performance-wise, how can we find out? This can be done by doing the things really often and comparing the results as objectively as possible, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_27.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/wvGdOem.jpeg)
:::

The preceding patcher, `optimization.maxpat`{.literal}, shows an example from the audio realm. I\'ve modified the original example patcher only slightly to fit inside a `[poly~]`{.literal} object. The `poly`{.literal} object, besides other great things, gives us the ability to seamlessly load any number (in this case, 20) of instances of a given patcher. So if there are any small performance differences, we just multiply them up until we see them. We can see them very clearly, nicely colored, by going to [**View**]{.strong} \| [**Show CPU Usage**]{.strong}. Also, if we switch that off again, the Max window prints out the statistics. In this case, the left `poly`{.literal} object consumed 81 percent of Max\'s total Audio CPU consumption and the right one 15 percent (to monitor non-audio CPU/memory consumption, we can use the top command in a terminal on Mac, or the task manager on Windows). Yeah, but a real sine sounds better, one might say. Often, one has ideas to do something really clever and then, when it\'s finished, one is definitely a bit biased towards thinking it is better. Even I, who knows he hasn\'t changed a thing about the DSP part during GUI design, sometimes have the impression that my stuff sounds better when I made a nice GUI, as stupid as it might be. In audio, a lot of these effects come into play all the time, so why not test it. I made the `Abcompare`{.literal} abstraction exactly for this purpose and I use it all the time. It\'s simple; you attach a `numberbox`{.literal} or `messagebox`{.literal} object to see what inlet is passed through. Then, you close your []{#ch03s12.html#id283 .indexterm}eyes and hit the Space bar numerous times to switch back []{#ch03s12.html#id284 .indexterm}and forth between the inlets until you don\'t know anymore which input you are listening to. Then, compare, see if you like one of them better or if you can hear a difference at all, open your eyes, and see if you were right! This is just an example of trying to identify problems. One thing should be mentioned before we go to the next section: don\'t start to optimize too early, and if you do, try to be sure that it really helps what you are doing, so put test it out.
:::
:::

[]{#ch03s13.html}

::: {.section title="Scripting and the this patcher"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Now, we\'ll[]{#ch03s13.html#id285 .indexterm} come to a topic that really gives us wings. There are sometimes []{#ch03s13.html#id286 .indexterm}situations when we think \"come on, doesn\'t this work in Max? It should!\" and often, scripting is there to help us. For example, can you remember when we talked about attributes; I mentioned that some attributes can\'t be changed dynamically. Now, let\'s change them all!

Consider this situation: you build an oscillator and it doesn\'t have any MIDI in or the one in which you just want to give the user the possibility to determine the frequency in Hz or as a note. Simple enough. However, we don\'t want to have two dials and switch between them; we want one `[live.dial]`{.literal}, and we want to just switch units. Try to move the `[live.dial]`{.literal} object\'s unit attribute into the patcher to create a message box; it won\'t work. Alright, this might have something to do with the automations in Ableton Live and Max4Live devices, but we won\'t use our filter mini app in Live anyway. So, let\'s try something different; we make one small patcher with just the live dial in one mode, and another with the dial in the other mode. Then, we make a `bpatcher`{.literal} object and dynamically load one of the two patchers. But that won\'t work either; where do we send the message with the name attribute? So to give you the simplest solution for this problem, we can just include and remove these from the presentation mode. But all the other thoughts? Both of the other ideas are valid too. This is how it works; if an attribute is hidden, as shown in the following screenshot, we can still access its name by copying the attribute and pasting it into a text editor or a message box:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_28.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/YAaeAqf.jpeg)
:::

Therefore, we []{#ch03s13.html#id287 .indexterm}arrive here:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_29.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/MT8SyeQ.jpeg)
:::

So what[]{#ch03s13.html#id288 .indexterm} about `bpatcher`{.literal}? Here, `[thispatcher]`{.literal} comes to the rescue. The `[thispatcher]`{.literal} object can do a lot for us; it can tell us where the patcher in which it lies is located on the filesystem. It allows us to dynamically create objects, connect them, move them around, delete them, send messages to them, and much more. In this case, we are using it to send the `bpatcher`{.literal} object itself a message, although our `bpatcher`{.literal} object doesn\'t even have an inlet:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_03_30.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/hN1Qrie.jpeg)
:::

We use its scripting name, which is [**varbp**]{.strong} in this case, to communicate with it and load a different patcher into it. We could also delete the `bpatcher`{.literal} object that contains one patcher and recreate another one that contains another patcher. As you can imagine, this might be the[]{#ch03s13.html#id289 .indexterm} worst way of doing it, but sometimes, it is absolutely []{#ch03s13.html#id290 .indexterm}necessary to create objects on the fly, for example, if we want to create an abstraction[]{#ch03s13.html#id291 .indexterm} with a variable number of inlets based on the given argument. The `[thispatcher]`{.literal} object allows us to do the patching automatically. Look[]{#ch03s13.html#id292 .indexterm} through the `[thispatcher]`{.literal} object\'s help-patch to get a feeling of what we are capable of. However, don\'t get too enthusiastic while spawning, deleting objects, and so on; there are often simpler solutions. For example, to have a variable GUI, a solution is presented in the same patch as all the other examples of the preceding screenshots are taken from `variable bpatcher.maxpat`{.literal}. Here, the offset message is sent to a `[thispatcher]`{.literal} object inside a `bpatcher`{.literal} object to shift around what\'s shown by the `bpatcher`{.literal} object.
:::

[]{#ch03s14.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

So in this chapter, we learned a lot about conceptualizing and structuring patches. By now, I hope you feel comfortable with the general handling of Max and know how to go about things in a tidy, reusable, and well-structured way. Don\'t forget to mess around sometimes though; Max is great in helping us try out things or producing spaghetti code that makes some beautiful noise.

We covered many different topics in this chapter, such as debugging, structuring, presets, some ways to elegantly pass around data, useful GUI techniques such as the `bpatcher`{.literal} object, and even how to create dynamic GUIs using the latter. This chapter might have seemed a bit much if you are eager to patch little experiments, but as it has been stated in the chapter many times, having some methods in mind about how to go about bigger projects is useful, but it\'s always a balance. It\'s very likely you will create more small projects than big ones, and therefore, keep in mind what we learned here, or remember to come back here when a big project comes up.

In the next chapter, we\'ll finally dive right into how to make some sounds instead of just thinking about how to control things.
:::

[]{#ch04.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 4. Basic Audio in Max/MSP"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

In this chapter, we will focus on the audio-specific examples. We will take a look at the following audio processing and generation techniques:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Additive synthesis
-   Subtractive synthesis
-   Sampling
-   Wave shaping
:::

One problem we will face in this and the next two chapters is that we are now working in very deep waters, so to speak. Nearly every example provided here might be understood intuitively or taken apart in hours of math and calculations. It\'s up to you how deep you want to go, but in order to develop some intuition; we\'ll have to be using some []{#ch04.html#id293 .indexterm} [**Digital Signal Processing**]{.strong} ([**DSP**]{.strong}) theory. We will briefly cover the DSP theory, but it is highly recommended that you study its fundamentals deeper to clearly understand this scientific topic if you are not familiar with it already. You will also find some references and learning material at the end of this chapter.

::: {.section title="Basic audio principles"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We already []{#ch04.html#id294 .indexterm}saw and stated that it\'s important to know, see, and hear what\'s happening along a signal way. If we work in the realm of audio, there are four most important ways to measure a signal, which are conceptually partly very different and offer a very broad perspective on audio signals if we always have all of them in the back of our minds. These are the following important ways:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Numbers (actual sample values)
-   Levels (such as RMS, LUFS, and dB FS)
-   Transversal waves (waveform displays, so oscilloscopes)
-   Spectra (an analysis of frequency components)
:::

There are many more ways to think about audio or signals in general, but these are the most common and important ones. Let\'s use them inside Max right away to observe their different[]{#ch04.html#id295 .indexterm} behavior. We\'ll feed some very basic signals into them: DC offset, a sinusoid, and noise. The one that might surprise you the most and get you thinking is the constant signal or DC offset (if it\'s digital-analog converted). In the following screenshot, you can see how the different displays react:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/kWD1lqe.jpeg)
:::

In general, one might think we don\'t want any constant signals at all; we don\'t want any DC offset. However, we will use audio signals a lot to control things later, say, an LFO or sequencers that should run with great timing accuracy. Also, sometimes, we just add a DC offset to our audio streams by accident. You can see in the preceding screenshot that a very slowly moving or constant signal can be observed best by looking at its value directly, for example, using the `[number~]`{.literal} object. In a level display, the `[meter~]`{.literal} or `[levelmeter~]`{.literal} objects will seem to imply that the incoming signal is very loud, in fact, it should be at -6 dB [**Full Scale**]{.strong} ([**FS**]{.strong}). As it is very loud, we just can\'t hear anything since the frequency is[]{#ch04.html#id296 .indexterm} infinitely low. This is reflected by the spectrum display too; we see a very low frequency at -6 dB. In theory, we should just see an infinitely thin spike at 0 Hz, so everything else can be considered an (inevitable but reducible) measuring error.
:::
:::

[]{#ch04s02.html}

::: {.section title="Audio synthesis"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Awareness[]{#ch04s02.html#id297 .indexterm} of these possibilities of viewing a signal and their constraints, and knowing how they actually work, will greatly increase our productivity. So let\'s get to actually synthesizing some waveforms. A good example of different views []{#ch04s02.html#id298 .indexterm}of a signal operation is [**Amplitude Modulation**]{.strong} ([**AM**]{.strong}); we will also try to formulate some other general principles using the example of AM.

::: {.section title="Amplitude modulation"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec25}Amplitude modulation {.title}
:::

AM []{#ch04s02.html#id299 .indexterm}means the multiplication []{#ch04s02.html#id300 .indexterm}of a signal with an oscillator. This provides a method of generating sidebands, which is partial in a very easy, intuitive, and CPU-efficient way. Amplitude modulation seems like a word that has a very broad meaning and can be used as soon as we change a signal\'s amplitude by another signal. While this might be true, in the context of audio synthesis, it very specifically means the multiplication of two (most often sine) oscillators. Moreover, there is a distinction between AM and [**Ring Modulation**]{.strong}. But[]{#ch04s02.html#id301 .indexterm} before we get to this distinction, let\'s look at the following simple multiplication of two sine waves, and first look at the result in an oscilloscope as a wave:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/AIdOYEM.jpeg)
:::

So in the preceding screenshot, we can see the two sine waves and their product. If we imagine every pair of samples being multiplied, the operation seems pretty intuitive as the result is what we would expect. But what does this resulting wave really mean besides looking like a product of two sine waves? What does it sound like? The wave certainly seems to have stayed in there, right? Well, viewing the product as a wave and looking at the whole process in the time domain rather than the frequency domain is helpful but slightly misleading. So let\'s jump over to the following frequency domain and look at what\'s happening with the spectrum:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/CgP40s5.jpeg)
:::

So we []{#ch04s02.html#id302 .indexterm}can observe here that if we multiply []{#ch04s02.html#id303 .indexterm}a sine wave [**a**]{.strong} with a sine wave [**b**]{.strong}, when [**a**]{.strong} has a frequency of 1000 Hz and [**b**]{.strong} has a frequency of 100 Hz, we end up with two sine waves, one at 900 Hz and another at 1100 Hz. The original sine waves have disappeared. In general, we can say that the result of multiplying [**a**]{.strong} and [**b**]{.strong} is equal to adding and subtracting the frequencies. This is shown in the [**Equivalence to Sum and difference**]{.strong} subpatcher (in the following screenshot, the two inlets to the spectrum display overlap completely, which might be hard to see):

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/SPaXhKX.jpeg)
:::

So in the preceding screenshot, you see a basic `AM`{.literal} patcher that produces sidebands that we can predict quite easily.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch04s02.html#note19}Note {.title}

Multiplication is commutative; you will say, 1000 + 100 = 1100, 1000 - 100 = 900; that\'s alright, but what about 100 - 1000 and 100 + 1000? We get -900 and 1100 once again? It still works out, and the fact that it does has to do with negative frequencies, or the symmetry of a real frequency spectrum around 0.
:::
:::

So you []{#ch04s02.html#id304 .indexterm}can see that the two ways of looking at []{#ch04s02.html#id305 .indexterm}our signal and thinking about AM offer different opportunities and pitfalls. Here is another way to think about AM: it\'s the convolution of the two spectra. We didn\'t talk about convolution yet; we will at a later point. But keep it in mind or do a little research on your own; this aspect of AM is yet another interesting one.

::: {.section title="Ring modulation versus amplitude modulation"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec20}Ring modulation versus amplitude modulation {.title}
:::

The[]{#ch04s02.html#id306 .indexterm} difference between ring modulation []{#ch04s02.html#id307 .indexterm}and what we call AM in this context is that the former one uses a bipolar modulator and the latter one uses a unipolar one. So actually, this is just about scaling and offsetting one of the factors. The difference in the outcome is yet a big one; if we keep one oscillator unipolar, the other one will be present in the outcome. If we do so, it starts making sense to call one oscillator on the carrier and the other (unipolar) on the modulator. Also, it therefore introduces modulation depth that controls the amplitude of the sidebands. In the following screenshot, you can see the resulting spectrum; we have the original signal, so the carrier plus two sidebands, which are the original signals, are shifted up and down:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/QLE3y8c.jpeg)
:::

Therefore, you[]{#ch04s02.html#id308 .indexterm} can see that AM has the[]{#ch04s02.html#id309 .indexterm} possibility to roughen up our spectrum, which means we can use it to let through an original spectrum and add sidebands.
:::

::: {.section title="Tremolo"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec21}Tremolo {.title}
:::

Tremolo (from the Latin word [*tremare*]{.emphasis}, to shake or tremble) is a musical term, which means to []{#ch04s02.html#id310 .indexterm}change a sound\'s amplitude in regular short []{#ch04s02.html#id311 .indexterm}intervals. Many people confuse it with vibrato, which is a modulating pitch at regular intervals. AM is tremolo and FM is vibrato, and as a simple reminder, think that the V of vibrato is closer to the F of FM than to the A of AM.

So multiplying the two oscillators results in a different spectrum. But of course, we can also use multiplication to scale a signal and to change its amplitude. If we wanted to have a sine wave that has a tremolo, that is an oscillating variation in amplitude, with say a frequency of 1 Hertz, we would again multiply two sine waves, one with 1000 Hz for example and another with a frequency of 0.5 Hz. Why 0.5 Hz? Think about a sine wave; it has two peaks per cycle, a positive one and a negative one.

We can visualize all that very well if we think about it in the time domain, looking at the result in an oscilloscope. But what about our view of the frequency domain? Well, let\'s go through it; when we multiply a sine with 1000 Hz and one with 0.5 Hz, we actually get two sine waves, one with 999.5 Hz and one with 100.5 Hz. Frequencies that close create beatings, since once in a while, their positive and negative peaks overlap, canceling []{#ch04s02.html#id312 .indexterm}out each other. In general, the frequency []{#ch04s02.html#id313 .indexterm}of the beating is defined by the difference in frequency, which is 1 Hz in this case. So if we look at it this way, we come to the same result again of course, but this time, we actually think of two frequencies instead of one being attenuated.

Lastly, we could have looked up trigonometric identities to anticipate what happens if we multiply two sine waves. We find the following:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_47.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/uqdhPAg.jpeg)
:::

Here, φ and θ are the two angular frequencies multiplied by the time in seconds, for example:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_48.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/BciV4Sb.jpeg)
:::

This is the equation for the 1000 Hz sine wave.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Feedback"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec26}Feedback {.title}
:::

Feedback always brings the complexity of a system to the next level. It can be used to stabilize a system, but can also make a given system unstable easily. In a strict sense, in the context of DSP, stability means that for a finite input to a system, we get finite output. Obviously, feedback can give us infinite output for a finite input. We can use attenuated feedback, for[]{#ch04s02.html#id314 .indexterm} example, not only to make our AM patches recursive, adding more and more sidebands, but also to achieve some surprising results as we will see in a minute. Before we look at this application, let\'s quickly talk about feedback in general.

In the []{#ch04s02.html#id315 .indexterm}digital domain, feedback always demands some amount of delay. This is because the evaluation of the chain of operations would otherwise resemble an infinite amount of operations on one sample. This is true for both the Max message domain (we get a stack overflow error if we use feedback without delaying or breaking the chain of events) and the MSP domain; audio will just stop working if we try it. So the minimum network for a feedback chain as a block diagram looks something like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/SOXqcVu.jpeg)
:::

In the preceding screenshot, X is the input signal and `x[n]`{.literal} is the current input sample; Y is the output signal and `y[n]`{.literal} is the current output sample. In the block marked [![9716OT_04_62.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/xWE9WAN.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} comes from a mathematical []{#ch04s02.html#id316 .indexterm}construct named the Z-transform. The [**a**]{.strong} term is []{#ch04s02.html#id317 .indexterm}also a constant used to attenuate the feedback circle. If no feedback is involved, it\'s sometimes helpful to think about block diagrams as processing whole signals. For example, if you think of a block diagram that consists only of multiplication with a constant, it would make a lot of sense to think of its output signal as a scaled version of the input signal. We wouldn\'t think of the network\'s processing or its output sample by sample. However, as soon as feedback is involved, without calculation or testing, this is the way we should think about the network. Remember [Chapter 1](#ch01.html "Chapter 1. Getting Started with Max"){.link}, [*Getting Started with Max*]{.emphasis}?, we got to know the difference equation representation of a network there, so before we look at the Max version of things, let\'s look at the difference equation of the network to get a better feeling of the notation. Try to find it yourself before looking at it too closely!

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_49.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/5YEePII.jpeg)
:::

In Max, or rather in MSP, we can introduce feedback as soon as we use a `[tapin~]`{.literal} `[tapout~]`{.literal} pair that introduces a delay. The minimum delay possible is the signal vector size. Another way is to simply use a `[send~]`{.literal} and `[receive~]`{.literal} pair in our loop. The `[send~]`{.literal} and `[receive~]`{.literal} pair will automatically introduce this minimum amount of delay if needed, so the delay will be introduced only if there is a feedback loop. If we need shorter delays and feedback, we have to go into the wonderful world of `gen~`{.literal}. Here, our shortest delay time is one sample, and it can be introduced via the `[history]`{.literal} object. In the `Fbdiagram.maxpat`{.literal} patcher, you can find a Max version, an MSP version, and a `[gen~]`{.literal} version of our diagram. For the time being, let\'s just pretend that the gen domain is just another subpatcher/abstraction system that allows shorter delays with feedback and has a more limited set of objects that more or less work the same as the MSP ones. In the following []{#ch04s02.html#id318 .indexterm}screenshot, you can see the difference between the[]{#ch04s02.html#id319 .indexterm} output of the MSP and the `[gen~]`{.literal} domain. Obviously, the length of the delay time has quite an impact on the output. Also, don\'t forget that the MSP version\'s output will vary greatly depending on our vector size settings.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TAHggRO.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s return to AM now. Feedback can, for example, be used to duplicate and shift our spectrum again and again. In the following screenshot, you can see a 1000 Hz sine wave that has been processed by a recursive AM to be duplicated and shifted up and down with a 100 Hz spacing:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/AzS1HLk.jpeg)
:::

In the maybe surprising result, we can achieve with this technique is this: if the modulating oscillator and the carrier have the same frequency, we end up with something that []{#ch04s02.html#id320 .indexterm}almost []{#ch04s02.html#id321 .indexterm}sounds like a sawtooth wave.
:::

::: {.section title="Frequency modulation"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec27}Frequency modulation {.title}
:::

Frequency[]{#ch04s02.html#id322 .indexterm} modulation or FM is a technique[]{#ch04s02.html#id323 .indexterm} that allows us to create a lot of frequency components out of just two oscillators, which is why it was used a lot back in the days when oscillators were a rare, expensive, or good, or CPU performance was low. Still, especially when dealing with real-time synthesis, efficiency is a crucial factor, and the huge variety of sounds that can be achieved with just two oscillators and very few parameters can be very useful for live performance and so on. The idea of FM is of course to modulate an oscillator\'s frequency. The basic, admittedly useless, form is depicted in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_09.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/a9M5eZl.jpeg)
:::

While trying to visualize what happens with the output in the time domain, we can imagine it as shown in the following screenshot. In the preceding screenshot, you can see the signal that is controlling the frequency. It is a sine wave with a frequency of 50 Hz, scaled and offset to range from -1000 to 5000, so the center or carrier frequency is 2000 Hz, which is modulated to an amount of 3000 Hz.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_13.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/NqCvUSY.jpeg)
:::

You []{#ch04s02.html#id324 .indexterm}can see the output of the modulated oscillator in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_14.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Qd9cjI1.jpeg)
:::

If we[]{#ch04s02.html#id325 .indexterm} extend the upper patch slightly, we end up with this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_10.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/JZUu8tu.jpeg)
:::

Although you can\'t see it in the screenshot, the sidebands appear with a 100 Hz spacing[]{#ch04s02.html#id326 .indexterm} here, that is, with spacing equal to the []{#ch04s02.html#id327 .indexterm}modulator\'s frequency. Pretty similar to AM right? But depending on the modulation amount, we get more and more sidebands.

::: {.section title="Controlling FM"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec22}Controlling FM {.title}
:::

If the[]{#ch04s02.html#id328 .indexterm} ratio between [**F(c)**]{.strong} and [**F(m)**]{.strong} is an integer, we []{#ch04s02.html#id329 .indexterm}end up with a harmonic spectrum, therefore, it may be more useful to rather control [**F(m)**]{.strong} indirectly via a ratio parameter as it\'s done inside the `SimpleRatioAndIndex`{.literal} subpatcher. Also, an `Index`{.literal} parameter is typically introduced to make an FM patch even more controllable. The modulation index is defined as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_50.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Dvicfas.jpeg)
:::

Here, [**I**]{.strong} is the index, [![9716OT_04_64.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/bjwBHc6.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} is the modulator\'s frequency. So finally, after adding these two controls, we []{#ch04s02.html#id330 .indexterm}might arrive here:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/8e0tx9l.jpeg)
:::

FM offers[]{#ch04s02.html#id331 .indexterm} a wide range of possibilities, for example, the fact that we have a simple control for how harmonic/inharmonic our spectrum is can be useful to synthesize the mostly noisy attack phase of many instruments if we drive the ratio and index with an envelope as it\'s done in the `SimpleEnvelopeDriven`{.literal} subpatcher. However, it\'s also very easy to synthesize very artificial, strange sounds. This basically is due to the following two reasons:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Firstly, the partials that appear have amplitudes governed by Bessel functions that may seem quite unpredictable; the partials sometimes seem to have random amplitudes.
-   Secondly, negative frequencies and fold back. If we generate partials with frequencies[]{#ch04s02.html#id332 .indexterm} below 0 Hz, it is equivalent to[]{#ch04s02.html#id333 .indexterm} creating the same positive frequency. For frequencies greater than the sample rate/2 (sample rate/2 is what\'s called the Nyquist rate), the frequencies reflect back into the spectrum that can be described by our sampling rate (this is an effect also called aliasing). So at a sampling rate of 44,100 Hz, a partial with a frequency of -100 Hz will appear at 100 Hz, and a partial with a frequency of 43100 kHz will appear at 1000 Hz, as shown in the following screenshot:
    ::: {.mediaobject}
    ![9716OT_04_12.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/JrJLPCz.jpeg)
    :::
:::

So, for frequencies between the Nyquist frequency and the sampling frequency, what we hear is described by this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_51.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/763mP7h.jpeg)
:::

Here, [![9716OT_04_67.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/XY4Ga3i.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} is the frequency we are trying to synthesize. Since FM leads to many partials, this effect can easily come up, and can both be used in an artistically interesting manner or sometimes appear as an unwanted error. In theory, an FM signal\'s partials extend to even infinity, but the amplitudes become negligibly small. If we want to reduce this behavior, the `[poly~]`{.literal} object can be used to oversample the process, generating a bit more headroom for high frequencies. The phenomenon of aliasing can be understood by thinking of a real (in contrast to imaginary) digital signal as having a symmetrical and periodical spectrum; let\'s not []{#ch04s02.html#id334 .indexterm}go into too much detail here and look at it []{#ch04s02.html#id335 .indexterm}in the time domain:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_15.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/d3KjuOQ.jpeg)
:::

In the previous screenshot, we again tried to synthesize a sine wave with 43100 Hz (the dotted line) at a sampling rate of 44100 Hz. What we actually get is the straight black line, a sine with 1000 Hz. Each big black dot represents an actual sample, and there is only one single band-limited signal connecting them: the 1000 Hz wave that is only partly visible here (about half its wavelength).
:::

::: {.section title="Feedback"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec23}Feedback {.title}
:::

It is very []{#ch04s02.html#id336 .indexterm}common to use feedback with FM. We can even[]{#ch04s02.html#id337 .indexterm} frequency modulate one oscillator with itself, making the algorithm even cheaper since we have only one table lookup. The idea of feedback FM quickly leads us to the idea of making networks of oscillators that can be modulated by each other, including feedback paths, but let\'s keep it simple for now. One might think that modulating one oscillator with itself should produce chaos; FM being a technique that is not the easiest to control, one shouldn\'t care for playing around with single operator feedback FM. But the opposite is the case.

A single operator FM yields very predictable partials, as shown in the following screenshot, and in the `Single OP FBFM`{.literal} subpatcher:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_16.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/f2Wj1il.jpeg)
:::

Again, we []{#ch04s02.html#id338 .indexterm}are using a `gen~`{.literal} patch, since we want to create []{#ch04s02.html#id339 .indexterm}a feedback loop and are heading for a short delay in the loop. Note that we are using the `[param]`{.literal} object to pass a message into the `gen~`{.literal} object. What should catch your attention is that although the carrier frequency has been adjusted to 1000 Hz, the fundamental frequency in the spectrum is around 600 Hz. What can help us here is switching to phase modulation.
:::

::: {.section title="Phase modulation"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec24}Phase modulation {.title}
:::

If you[]{#ch04s02.html#id340 .indexterm} look at the `gen~`{.literal} patch in the previous[]{#ch04s02.html#id341 .indexterm} screenshot, you see that we are driving our sine oscillator with a phasor. The cycle object\'s phase inlet assumes an input that ranges from 0 to 1 instead of from 0 to 2π, as one might think. To drive a sine wave through one full cycle in math, we can use a variable ranging from 0 to 2π, so in the following formula, you can imagine [**t**]{.strong} being provided by a phasor, which is the running phase. The 2π multiplication isn\'t necessary in Max since if we are using `[cycle~]`{.literal}, we are reading out a wavetable actually instead of really computing the sine or cosine of the input:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_52.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rorDVL7.jpeg)
:::

This is the most common form of denoting a running sinusoid with frequency [![9716OT_04_66.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ozDyexS.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} and phase φ. Try to come up with a formula that describes frequency modulation!

Simplifying the phases by setting it to zero, we can denote FM as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_53.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/PgV4uVR.jpeg)
:::

This can be shown to be nearly identical to the following formula:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_54.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Fbnxgx3.jpeg)
:::

Here, [![9716OT_04_70.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/7fV8paQ.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} is the frequency of the modulator, and [**A**]{.strong} is the modulation amount.

Welcome []{#ch04s02.html#id342 .indexterm}to phase modulation. If you compare it, the []{#ch04s02.html#id343 .indexterm}previous formula actually just inserts a scaled sine wave where the phase φ used to be. So phase modulation is nearly identical to frequency modulation. Phase modulation has some advantages though, such as providing us with an easy method of synchronizing multiple oscillators. But let\'s go back to the Max side of things and look at a feedback phase modulation patch right away (ignoring simple phase modulation, since it really is so similar to FM):

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_17.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/roimhoG.jpeg)
:::

This `gen~`{.literal} patcher resides inside the `One OP FBPM`{.literal} subpatcher and implements phase modulation using one oscillator and feedback. Interestingly, the spectrum is very similar to the one of a sawtooth wave, with the feedback amount having a similar effect to a low-pass filter, controlling[]{#ch04s02.html#id344 .indexterm} the amount of partials. If you[]{#ch04s02.html#id345 .indexterm} take a look at the subpatcher, you\'ll find the following three sound sources:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Our feedback FM `gen~`{.literal} patcher
-   A `[saw~]`{.literal} object for comparison
-   A `poly~`{.literal} object
:::

We have already mentioned the problem of aliasing and the `[poly~]`{.literal} object has already been proposed to treat the problem. However, it allows us to define the quality of parts of patches in general, so let\'s talk about the object a bit before moving on since we will make great use of it throughout the audio part of this book. Before moving on, I would like to tell you that you can double-click on it to see what is loaded inside, and you will see that the subpatcher we just discussed contains a `[poly~]`{.literal} object that contains yet another version of our `gen~`{.literal} patcher.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The poly~ object"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec28}The poly\~ object {.title}
:::

The `poly~`{.literal} object has []{#ch04s02.html#id346 .indexterm}now been mentioned a couple of times; I[]{#ch04s02.html#id347 .indexterm} hope by now you have the impression that it\'s pretty important, because it is, and can be a bit confusing at first. First of all, it is a container for an abstraction. We write patches, mostly knowing beforehand that we will be loading them into a `poly~`{.literal} object and therefore constructing them slightly differently. Here is an overview of what `poly~`{.literal} can do for us:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The `poly~`{.literal} object up sample/down sample audio in the loaded patch
-   Define a different vector size for the loaded patch
-   Take a patch and instantiate it multiple times
-   Manage polyphony automatically (voice allocation)
-   Load/unload patches dynamically
-   Turn on/off audio processing for a loaded patch automatically or manually
:::

This list seems to imply that the object is only interesting for audio purposes, but behold point three. It can be extremely useful to take any patch and load it multiple times at once. To load a patch into `[poly~]`{.literal}, it has to be in Max\'s search path or in the same folder as the host patch as it\'s usual for abstractions. The `[poly~]`{.literal} object\'s first argument is the patcher to load:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_18.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/DFZBFf6.jpeg)
:::

You see that the poly object shown contains a patcher called `testPatch1.maxpat`{.literal}. This patcher has inlets and outlets that appear on the `[poly~]`{.literal} object as usual when dealing with abstractions. Note that the inlets and outlets are not actual `[inlet]`{.literal} and `[outlet]`{.literal} objects. If []{#ch04s02.html#id348 .indexterm}we are dealing with poly, they need to be `[in]`{.literal}/`[in~]`{.literal} and `[out]`{.literal}/`[out~]`{.literal} objects that explicitly state whether it\'s a message or a signal port. Also, the []{#ch04s02.html#id349 .indexterm}numbering might lead to confusion in objects that have a running numbering scheme, so independent of a message or signal domain, we can use a running number. Our objects, on the other hand, have separate numbering for the message and signal domain, both starting at 1. Why is that?

Well, we can imagine the `poly`{.literal} object\'s inlets and outlets managing the separation of message and signal domain automatically. We can, for example, have only one inlet to our `poly`{.literal} object, but send in both messages and signals. The two `in`{.literal} objects, one `[in 1]`{.literal} and the other `[in~ 1]`{.literal} in our `poly`{.literal} patcher will divide these for our convenience. The `out`{.literal} objects, on the other hand, are also supposed to provide us with this separation on the receiving side: the `host`{.literal} patcher or the `poly~`{.literal} object\'s actual outlets. In the following screenshot, you can see this at the beginning maybe confusing concept used to simplify things:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_19.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ho0RPyu.jpeg)
:::

Up or down sampling can be achieved by providing arguments such as down 16. The amount of up or down sampling must be a power of 2. This can be extremely useful; we already saw that some processes produce a lot of high frequencies that could alias, so up sampling the process might give us more detail in the high frequency range. Also, some processes just sound better or are designed to work better at higher sampling frequencies such as many filters. What comes out of the `poly~`{.literal} object is of course at our normal sampling rate, the up sampled signal that has been filtered and down sampled. You can imagine this process to be similar to an analog-to-digital conversion.

Down sampling, on the other hand, can be very useful for processes that are somewhat CPU-expensive; we would want this to happen in the signal domain without actually producing an audible signal. A good example of this is a [**Low Frequency Oscillator**]{.strong} ([**LFO**]{.strong}). Depending []{#ch04s02.html#id350 .indexterm}on what we are controlling with the LFO, it can be down sampled a lot to save CPU cycles. But to really make this efficient, we shouldn\'t forget to also turn off the filter we just talked about, by using the attribute `@resampling 0`{.literal}.

Before looking at what we just discussed, we\'ll quickly examine what it means to load multiple[]{#ch04s02.html#id351 .indexterm} instance of a patcher. The first argument of `poly~`{.literal} is the []{#ch04s02.html#id352 .indexterm}number of voices or instances. So `[poly~ myPatch 10]`{.literal} will load the `myPatch`{.literal} patch 10 times simultaneously. It simply sums up all instances\' audio outputs and serializes all instances\' message outputs. In the previous screenshot, you see the loaded `testPatch1`{.literal} patcher; its window title also shows a [**(1)**]{.strong}. This means we are looking at the first voice or instance. Other instances can be opened and looked at if we send the message [**open n**]{.strong} to the `[poly~]`{.literal} object, where [**n**]{.strong} stands for the voice number of the course.

The most simple use case scenario is a really handy one: if you ever want to know in which processing audio consumes more CPU, you can just turn on [**Show CPU usage**]{.strong}. If the processes are very lightweight and don\'t show a lot of differences, it\'s a good idea to put them into `poly`{.literal}, make many instances, and compare the results. Statistically, these numbers are much more valid and reliable. Both of what we just talked about is illustrated in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_20.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/LQIZd0I.jpeg)
:::

::: {.section title="Managing instances and patcher loading"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec25}Managing instances and patcher loading {.title}
:::

If we []{#ch04s02.html#id353 .indexterm}have, for example, ten instances of a patcher loaded, it[]{#ch04s02.html#id354 .indexterm} often comes down to making small []{#ch04s02.html#id355 .indexterm}differences in the configuration of each instance. For example, we []{#ch04s02.html#id356 .indexterm}may want to create a primitive reverb-like effect with 20 delays, each delay with a slightly different delay time. We have the following two basic options to do this:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The `target`{.literal} message
-   The `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object
:::

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch04s02.html#note20}Note {.title}

A very common pitfall when using the `poly~`{.literal} object is this: unlike signals, messages sent to the patch inside the `poly`{.literal} object are by default only sent to the first instance.
:::
:::

As illustrated in the following screenshot, the `target`{.literal} message can be used to direct a message to any instance we want:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_21.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rF4weHH.jpeg)
:::

As you see, we can always know the instance number inside the instance via the `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object. This can be used to access the `[coll]`{.literal} objects, for example, or to pick values out of lists we are sending in and so on, to individualize each instance\'s parameters.

We can not only have multiple instances inside a `poly`{.literal} object but also dynamically load and unload patches to switch between different processing algorithms, and we can also use the `mute`{.literal} message to turn off audio processing in order to save processing power.

So if we only have a couple of patchers that only feature audio processing (Max processing won\'t be stopped using the `mute`{.literal} message), it might be wiser to use muting. So we would just make, say, five `poly`{.literal} objects and mute all that are not needed. If, on the other hand, we have 50 patches and always need only one of them, dynamically loading them will be more useful. The `dynamic loading`{.literal} subpatcher illustrates this, although there are []{#ch04s02.html#id357 .indexterm}only three different patches to choose from. However, we[]{#ch04s02.html#id358 .indexterm} may like to expand this list further. We[]{#ch04s02.html#id359 .indexterm} could even go so far to make a directory in our disk with a `[umenu]`{.literal} object []{#ch04s02.html#id360 .indexterm}referencing it, where all the patches are where we might want to load. To expand this list, we can simply drop a new patcher in that directory. In the subpatcher mentioned, we made three different oscillators and could switch between them via a `[live.tab]`{.literal} object. Each of the oscillators uses the second inlet differently, so using a `[coll]`{.literal} object allows us to put a name on the dial for us to always know what we are controlling. Note the `[deferlow]`{.literal} object that makes our patch wait until the new oscillator is loaded and then updates its internal state to be in sync without `[live.dial]`{.literal}. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_22.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/DjIjUJ0.jpeg)
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Polyphony and voice allocation"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec26}Polyphony and voice allocation {.title}
:::

To conclude[]{#ch04s02.html#id361 .indexterm} our talk about the `[poly~]`{.literal} object, let\'s see what `[poly~]`{.literal} can[]{#ch04s02.html#id362 .indexterm} do for us when it comes to actual polyphony. In the next []{#ch04s02.html#id363 .indexterm}example, we are going to take our dynamic []{#ch04s02.html#id364 .indexterm}oscillator construction and put it inside another `[poly~]`{.literal} to manage polyphony for us. So you see, we can also have the `[poly~]`{.literal} objects inside the `[poly~]`{.literal} objects. There are a couple of things we have to change about our original patch.

First, we will want to control it from outside the `[poly~]`{.literal} objects, so we\'ll need to isolate GUI objects from sound processing/generation and think of some way in which these can communicate. Secondly, we\'ll need to add an envelope, namely an `[adsr~]`{.literal} object, which goes particularly well with the `[poly~]`{.literal} object\'s voice allocation capabilities. Lastly, we have to connect up the `[adsr~]`{.literal} object with the voice allocation system via a `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object and use a sample and hold function to actually make use of the voice allocation. You can see the result in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_23.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/VEZLp58.jpeg)
:::

If you look at the patch and try to forget everything inside the `[panel]`{.literal} object or substitute it in your mind with a `[cycle~]`{.literal} object, you have the basic structure for a working []{#ch04s02.html#id365 .indexterm}polyphonic patch. The use of `send`{.literal} and `receive`{.literal} in[]{#ch04s02.html#id366 .indexterm} combination with `[route]`{.literal} and `[prepend]`{.literal} is handy but optional[]{#ch04s02.html#id367 .indexterm} of course. What\'s really important is the use of a `[sah~]`{.literal} object[]{#ch04s02.html#id368 .indexterm} to hold the pitch for this specific voice until the `[adsr~]`{.literal} object starts a new envelope. The `adsr~`{.literal} object communicates with the `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object to tell the `[poly~]`{.literal} object\'s voice allocation busy states. Let\'s think about it more in detail.

A new note comes in at `[in 1]`{.literal}; it\'s a list that contains pitch and velocity. This message needs to be prepended with `midinote`{.literal} for `[poly~]`{.literal} to know that this is supposed to be considered by the voice allocation system. So we, for example, send `midinote`{.literal} 64 127, but inside, we only receive the list composed of two ints. We unpack the message and use the `[swap]`{.literal} object. What `[swap]`{.literal} does is just swap its two inlets, but what\'s important is that the order of events is therefore changed. Pack gives us the rightmost element first and the velocity, and by the use of `[swap]`{.literal}, we get the pitch first. This is important since the sample and hold need the new pitch to be present at its inlet when the new envelope is triggered.

After being brought[]{#ch04s02.html#id369 .indexterm} into the range 0 to 1, the velocity goes into our envelope. On a non-zero velocity, the `[adsr~]`{.literal} object does the following:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   It tells the `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object to unmute the instance (the second outlet from right to left)
-   It sends out a click or an impulse (the third outlet from right to left), which triggers a new value to be sampled and held by the `[sah~]`{.literal} object unit sample
-   It generates the envelope that controls our output amplitude (the fourth outlet from right to left)
:::

Also, this envelope on the fourth outlet is being sent to the `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object. The `[thispoly~]`{.literal} object essentially looks at this signal, and if it is zero, `[thispoly~]`{.literal} will automatically mute the voice.

Some last things to note; it\'s a good idea to mute all voices on startup, so the system doesn\'t consume CPU unnecessarily. On the other hand, be aware that dynamically muting and activating voices not only means that we don\'t process things we don\'t need, it also means that the overall CPU consumption of a system becomes less predictable. If we design a system that is supposed to run in real time, maybe for a concert or an installation, we definitely should try how it behaves with all active voices. If we don\'t try this, it might happen that we demand too much from our machine just by playing more notes at the same time than we ever tried at home.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Additive synthesis"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec29}Additive synthesis {.title}
:::

Additive []{#ch04s02.html#id370 .indexterm}synthesis is very simple at first sight, but very[]{#ch04s02.html#id371 .indexterm} deep, versatile, and complex at the second one. The basic idea is to use multiple sine waves as building blocks for more complex spectra. To put it very short, the idea of Fourier analysis, and therefore of a spectrum, is that any periodical signal can be decomposed to sum up an infinite number of sinusoids.

The only problems we face with this potentially infinitely powerful approach is that we often need a very high number of sine oscillators to create complex spectra and have to come up with some method to control their frequency, amplitude, and phase in a meaningful way. So we have an approach that can do anything but how to control it? There are several methods to do this, but let\'s first look at simply doing it manually. After that, we\'ll look at something called [**Discrete Summation Formula**]{.strong} ([**DSF**]{.strong}), and[]{#ch04s02.html#id372 .indexterm} we\'ll go to inverse Fourier Transform in [Chapter 5](#ch05.html "Chapter 5. Advanced Audio in Max/MSP"){.link}, [*Advanced Audio in Max/MSP*]{.emphasis}.

We just learned how to run multiple instances of one patcher simultaneously, so a `[poly~]`{.literal} object that contains a `[cycle~]`{.literal} object would of course be a way of implementing an oscillator bank, which can be used for additive synthesis. Luckily, there is the `[oscbank~]`{.literal} object, which does more or less exactly this in an optimized way. This object accepts, for example, a list that contains frequencies and amplitudes, so we can define any spectrum we like.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_24.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/DHcpZxE.jpeg)
:::

The []{#ch04s02.html#id373 .indexterm}preceding screenshot depicts the `Oscillator Bank`{.literal} subpatcher inside `Additive Synthesis.maxpat`{.literal}. It\'s a basic setup for synthesizing[]{#ch04s02.html#id374 .indexterm} arbitrary waveforms. Using a `[multislider]`{.literal} object, we []{#ch04s02.html#id375 .indexterm}can set the amplitudes of the different harmonics and implement some presets using formulas to set amplitudes and frequencies for standard waveforms such as a sawtooth, rectangle, and triangle wave. In this patch, we don\'t care for the phases at all, which is why our waveforms have correct spectra and sound right but don\'t have the right waveform (in the preceding screenshot, the rectangular wave is looking right by coincidence).

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch04s02.html#note21}Note {.title}

Also, beware that although this patch sets up the oscillator bank to have a harmonic spectrum and only uses integer multiples of the fundamental frequency, this is not necessary at all. We can use any frequency and synthesize completely inharmonic spectra too.
:::
:::

I hope you see that this approach becomes very tedious if we set the amplitudes, frequencies, and even phases by hand, or it gets quite mathematical quickly if we try to come up with formulas that set things up for us automatically.

::: {.section title="Discrete Summation Formulae"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec27}Discrete Summation Formulae {.title}
:::

The idea of a DSF is to basically take a shortcut. If[]{#ch04s02.html#id376 .indexterm} we want a large number of sine waves, it might not be []{#ch04s02.html#id377 .indexterm}necessary to sum all of them up; there are formulas out there that do it for us with a lot less effort and provide us with a more meaningful set of controls. The downside is that we have to go through some slightly scary-looking math. This might be the reason for this technique not being used a lot, although there are plenty of papers on the Web that contain formulas ready to be implemented in Max. In the following example, we\'ll use the paper, [*The synthesis of complex spectra by means of discrete summation formulae*]{.emphasis} by James A. Moorer. This paper can be found at <https://ccrma.stanford.edu/files/papers/stanm5.pdf>.

As an example, we\'ll implement this formula that we found in formula 1 of the paper:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_55.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/1spvptc.jpeg)
:::

I strongly recommend that you try to implement this yourself, take a look at the original paper to get some additional notes on how to actually use this formula for synthesis, and try to do the math to Max translation. The idea here really is to be able to do that translation, to find a formula, and be able to evaluate it in Max. This can help a lot in actually understanding a formula and can be done without actually understanding where it comes from.

So let\'s now[]{#ch04s02.html#id378 .indexterm} look at the MSP version of it. Try to implement it []{#ch04s02.html#id379 .indexterm}yourself before looking at this! Generally, it will actually be easier to go from math to Max than to understand a Max patch implementing it. Max has a lot of advantages but compactness and readability are not its strongest points. Compare what space the formula takes up in this book with the space the Max patch needs. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_25.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/qLXPRBP.jpeg)
:::

This subpatch is called DSF, and is extremely versatile in generating dynamic spectra. For more information on the parameters and their use, try it out or look at the quoted paper. As you can see, many sine waves will be required to build up such complex spectra, so we save a lot of CPU by using this approach and end up with a tiny number of parameters (compared to the number of sines x 3, for amplitude, phase, and frequency), which really allows us to control the algorithm in an intuitive way.

What\'s presented []{#ch04s02.html#id380 .indexterm}here is the most direct translation from the formula to[]{#ch04s02.html#id381 .indexterm} the MSP domain, so lots of optimizations can be made here. We\'ll come to the `gen~`{.literal} environment soon; this will probably be easier to implement in a codebox inside `gen~`{.literal}. If you choose to use this algorithm, think about going to the `gen~`{.literal} domain; think if we really need all calculations to happen in the [**signal/gen\~**]{.strong} domain or if we can do some things in the message domain, and try to move to wavetables, that is `[cycle~]`{.literal}, instead of computing the sine and cosine in real time.

DSF approaches have the problem that they need to be normalized. In the patch, `[tanh~]`{.literal} has been used to soft clip the result after an empirical attenuation. A web search can reveal different approaches to normalizing different DSFs, and the quoted paper also provides some.

Finally, as Moorer also shows in the paper, for harmonic spectra, the formula can be greatly simplified, take a shot at this one:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_56.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/M3X9D4b.jpeg)
:::
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Subtractive synthesis and filtering"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl2sec30}Subtractive synthesis and filtering {.title}
:::

If what[]{#ch04s02.html#id382 .indexterm} we just discussed can be compared to making[]{#ch04s02.html#id383 .indexterm} a sculpture by gluing lots of tiny things (a sine wave[]{#ch04s02.html#id384 .indexterm} is the most tiny thing we can find, it is a single frequency) together, now[]{#ch04s02.html#id385 .indexterm} we take the approach of taking a big block of marble and cutting away everything until we are left with a beautiful sculpture. Beware though that our block of marble has to contain the beautiful sculpture beforehand. The cutting away is done using filters and the block of marble is a signal with a rich spectrum, so we have something to cut away.

In Max, we have a couple of filters that can be used right away. Before going into more detail, let\'s look at the following screenshot to get an overview of what we have:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_26.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/aiFPpAW.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s quickly []{#ch04s02.html#id386 .indexterm}cover the parameters of a typical filter[]{#ch04s02.html#id387 .indexterm} in order to get a more firm grasp of filter parameters[]{#ch04s02.html#id388 .indexterm} in general. If we take a low-pass filter as an []{#ch04s02.html#id389 .indexterm}example, one can say that it leaves frequencies below its cutoff frequency unaltered; everything above will be increasingly attenuated. Technically speaking, it usually attenuates the cutoff frequency with -3 dB already. The resonance, or [**Q**]{.strong}, controls the amount of feedback in a filter, emphasizing frequencies around the cutoff. The gain parameter, which is sometimes available, just allows us to regulate a gain that is constant for all frequencies.

We\'ll now quickly look at an example that\'s supposed to show that filtering is a process that also allows us to emphasize on certain frequencies so much that we won\'t recognize the filter\'s output as a filtered version of the input. Let\'s synthesize the sine wave out of white noise as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_27.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/VuBBQKh.jpeg)
:::

The []{#ch04s02.html#id390 .indexterm}values for the parameters of the two resonant[]{#ch04s02.html#id391 .indexterm} bandpass filters are chosen empirically, and the sine wave []{#ch04s02.html#id392 .indexterm}at[]{#ch04s02.html#id393 .indexterm} the output is not completely stable (can you think why?). Nevertheless, I hope you see that filtering can be used to design spectra in a nearly additive way too.

::: {.section title="The classic approach"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec28}The classic approach {.title}
:::

Let\'s[]{#ch04s02.html#id394 .indexterm} practice some more and talk about the classic[]{#ch04s02.html#id395 .indexterm} setup of subtractive synthesis. In general, in an analogue synthesizer, we\'ll find these waveforms:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Sine
-   Triangle
-   Saw
-   Square
-   Noise
:::

In the section about additive synthesis, we already saw a patch that implemented the formulae for these, but here are the harmonics of each of them again in detail.

When building a classic synthesizer, we can, for example, use these waveforms, which are all available in Max as bandlimited, so nearly aliasing free, versions, `[cycle~]`{.literal}, `[tri~]`{.literal}, `[saw~]`{.literal}, `[rect~]`{.literal}, and `[noise~]`{.literal}. Loosely inspired by the legendary [**Korg MS20**]{.strong} (choose another classic synth after this and rebuild it in Max!), we came up with a design that features a lowpass and a highpass in series, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_28.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/VKi43Pg.jpeg)
:::

Now, this[]{#ch04s02.html#id396 .indexterm} is more or less something we have already[]{#ch04s02.html#id397 .indexterm} seen; you can find it as a subpatch called `MS201`{.literal} inside the `SubractiveSynthesis.maxpat`{.literal} path. Let\'s take it a step further right away. We are going to use the `[biquad~]`{.literal} filter, which is the most versatile filter available inside Max. Biquad stands for biquadratic, and implements a very commonly-used two-pole filter, a very generic formula, but we\'ll get to how to think about filters later. The `[Biquad~]`{.literal} object is a great filter, but we have to provide it with coefficients instead of cutoff/center frequency, gain, and resonance. These coefficients can be computed manually in many programs such as Matlab or inside Max using either `[filtergraph~]`{.literal} if we need a GUI, `[filterdesign]`{.literal} if we want to be very explicit, or `[filtercoeff~]`{.literal} if we want to modulate the filter\'s cutoff, for example, using a signal. So the next example does exactly this: a `[biquad~]`{.literal} object is controlled via a `[filtercoeff~]`{.literal} object in low-pass mode. These two are inside a `[poly~]`{.literal} object, as upsampling a filter can increase sound quality quite a bit. For convenience and tidiness, the `[filtercoeff]`{.literal} object is also inside the `poly~`{.literal} object. This is not optimal in terms of efficiency since upsampling the coefficient calculation won\'t increase audio quality, and this is quite a costly task regarding CPU consumption. The filter coefficient calculation, on the other hand, relies on the sampling frequency of the filter, so calculating the coefficients outside the upsampled process leads to faulty coefficients.

Modulation of the cutoff frequency in this patch is possible using the amplitude envelope and/or an LFO, as most conventional synthesizer designs offer. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_29.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TYptRJQ.jpeg)
:::

You[]{#ch04s02.html#id398 .indexterm} already know some synthesis techniques, so go []{#ch04s02.html#id399 .indexterm}on and refine the oscillators used, implement some FM or a second filter, and introduce some slight randomness in the pitch control to make the oscillators drift out of phase a bit. There are a lot of things that can be added to this!
:::

::: {.section title="Building an equalizer"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec29}Building an equalizer {.title}
:::

Quite often, we []{#ch04s02.html#id400 .indexterm}need a bread and butter EQ. This []{#ch04s02.html#id401 .indexterm}can also be done using cascaded `[biquad~]`{.literal} objects; luckily, there is an object that combines multiple `[biquad~]`{.literal} objects in series: the `[cascade~]`{.literal} object. The `[filtergraph~]`{.literal} object can be configured to contain up to 24 filters[]{#ch04s02.html#id402 .indexterm} using the `nfilters`{.literal} attribute. Its output can be sent to the `cascade`{.literal} object and voila, we have an EQ. In the following screenshot that shows the `EQgraphic`{.literal} subpatcher, the `[filtergraph~]`{.literal} object that has been configured to be transparent was put over the `[spectrumdraw~]`{.literal} external object to see a nice graph of the resulting spectrum (beware that we have to give `[filtergraph~]`{.literal} and `[spectrumdraw~]`{.literal} the same amplitude and frequency ranges for this to show us something useful):

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_30.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/0ndRP4I.jpeg)
:::

Also, a[]{#ch04s02.html#id403 .indexterm} parametric EQ is created easily. There are []{#ch04s02.html#id404 .indexterm}two different versions inside the `SubtractiveSynthesis.maxpat`{.literal} patcher, both of which are inspired by the typical channel strip EQ on a mixing desk. These can be found in the `EQparametric`{.literal} and `EQparametric2`{.literal} subpatchers. One is done by cascading `[biquads~]`{.literal} manually, and the other one is using the `cascade`{.literal} object directly. Both make use of `[poly~]`{.literal} for the possibility of upsampling it and since we can mute the `poly~`{.literal} object. By muting it automatically when the EQ is bypassed, we can make it so the EQ consumes virtually no CPU when it\'s bypassed. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_31.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/6d0MtG0.jpeg)
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The filter theory: an introduction"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch04s02.html#ch04lvl3sec30}The filter theory: an introduction {.title}
:::

Honestly, we []{#ch04s02.html#id405 .indexterm}won\'t be able to cover a lot of theory here. Really []{#ch04s02.html#id406 .indexterm}understanding filters just means understanding signal and system theory in general, and this is way beyond the scope of this book. Also, there are many ways to represent a given filter and fully describe it as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   It is a difference equation (which we use most)
-   It is a block diagram
-   It is a transfer function
-   It is an impulse response
:::

Hopefully, we can obtain a feeling of how filters work, and thus how systems in general work.

Often, the following distinction between different effects is used:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   [**Amplitude**]{.strong}: Gates, expanders, compressors, and[]{#ch04s02.html#id407 .indexterm} so on
-   [**Time**]{.strong}: Delays, reverbs, and []{#ch04s02.html#id408 .indexterm}so on
-   [**Frequency response**]{.strong}: Filters
:::

While[]{#ch04s02.html#id409 .indexterm} this distinction might be useful, it is, from a technical point of view, very blurry and not very handy. A very precise and helpful distinction is to look at a system and try to find out whether it is a [**Linear Time Invariant**]{.strong} ([**LTI**]{.strong}) system or not. While we will not give a formal description of LTI systems, linearity means summing[]{#ch04s02.html#id410 .indexterm} up two signals; sending them into the system results in[]{#ch04s02.html#id411 .indexterm} the same single output signal as sending[]{#ch04s02.html#id412 .indexterm} the two signals into the system individually and summing the outputs. You can easily see that this is, for example, not true for a compressor. A compressor reacts depending on the input\'s level, so it is not a linear system. Digital filters, on the other hand, most of the time, are LTI systems. Let\'s look at a very basic filter, that is, a running average:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_57.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Nfv9yaD.jpeg)
:::

Putting it in words, the output is the input plus the last input. If you think about it, we will have to actually divide the sum by two to get the average of the last two samples, as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_58.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/zWglOR6.jpeg)
:::

The two occurrences of [**0.5**]{.strong} are called the coefficients. You can imagine that the more we increase the first [**0.5**]{.strong} and the more we decrease the second [**0.5**]{.strong}, we are reducing the effect of the filter more and more. We are weighing the input and the delayed version of the input. This is essentially all that a certain filter class does, the so-called [**Finite Impulse Response**]{.strong} ([**FIR**]{.strong}) or []{#ch04s02.html#id413 .indexterm}non-recursive filters. We can generalize the previous formula to as many terms we like, as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_59.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/j174W7U.jpeg)
:::

Or we can also put it as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_04_60.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/LK9W8N4.jpeg)
:::

Here, [**m**]{.strong} is the maximum delay in samples, and [![9716OT_04_72.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TItGpaQ.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject} are the coefficients of the filter. We did not talk about convolution yet, but we know that what we are doing here is time-domain convolution. The coefficients of an FIR filter are the samples of its impulse response.
