We just []{#ch05s03.html#id549 .indexterm}modulated the amplitudes of a signal by another signal\'s amplitudes. So, of course, we can also multiply them by predefined values. A very typical application would be to store the amplitudes for all frequencies in a buffer and use a lookup to get the gain for each frequency. The following result is very similar to filtering:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_31.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Yzmaz4g.jpeg)
:::

Although []{#ch05s03.html#id550 .indexterm}this is a very simple patch, it is able to produce a broad variety of sparse, glass-like textures and sounds. It\'s a good example of how very simplistic approaches inside the frequency domain can yield quite impressive results.
:::

::: {.section title="Spectral reverb and freezing"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch05s03.html#ch05lvl2sec40}Spectral reverb and freezing {.title}
:::

Now, all []{#ch05s03.html#id551 .indexterm}that we have seen here was pretty simple as it was just multiplication[]{#ch05s03.html#id552 .indexterm} in the frequency domain. If we wish to actually[]{#ch05s03.html#id553 .indexterm} do []{#ch05s03.html#id554 .indexterm}some more processing in the frequency domain, we will face the problem of all the data being packed in vectors. For example, if we wish to smooth out amplitude data in time, we would need to have an individual low-pass filter on each sample in a signal vector. Luckily, the `[vectral~]`{.literal} object offers this functionality and more.

Of course, this object needs to know both the signal vector size and what bin we are working on at every instant. On the other hand, we probably want to smooth out amplitude values within one frame, so if we have a peak at 1 kHz, we also have some positive values around that. This filter should not bleed into the next frame though. The `[framesmooth~]`{.literal} object is made for this purpose. In the following patcher, we use both of them and a short buffer to reverberate, smear, and freeze the spectrum:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_32.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/RPp78M8.jpeg)
:::

Since by[]{#ch05s03.html#id555 .indexterm} doing a slow envelope following on the input amplitudes, we []{#ch05s03.html#id556 .indexterm}are introducing frequencies that are not present in the []{#ch05s03.html#id557 .indexterm}momentary[]{#ch05s03.html#id558 .indexterm} input (but have been), we need to give them some phase. If we don\'t, they will just have the same phase, which doesn\'t sound very pleasing. So in this patch, we simply add noise to the phase of the resynthesized sound. As you will surely notice, this patch allows for very long reverbs and a very easy control of rise and fall times. Don\'t hesitate to make a clipping out of it to add it to our reverb building blocks!
:::

::: {.section title="Recording and playback of FFT data"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch05s03.html#ch05lvl2sec41}Recording and playback of FFT data {.title}
:::

A very []{#ch05s03.html#id559 .indexterm}nice []{#ch05s03.html#id560 .indexterm}feature of FFT data is that we can resynthesize the data at any playback rate without affecting the pitch, essentially performing time stretching/compression. All we need to do is analyze sound once, write all the data to a file, and resynthesize the data. Here is an example of how to achieve this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_33.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/XI47HXq.jpeg)
:::

The only []{#ch05s03.html#id561 .indexterm}part of this patch that might be a bit surprising is that we do []{#ch05s03.html#id562 .indexterm}some processing on the phase of the original signal before recording it. The `[framedelta~]`{.literal} object computes the delta of successive frames, resulting []{#ch05s03.html#id563 .indexterm}in the derivative of all the phases. When we play back the recording, we use `[frameaccum~]`{.literal} to reconstruct the running phase. This needs to be done since we want to play back at arbitrary speeds.

Also, note that the format of the buffer/file we write to is of importance. Compressed or low bit-depth formats might compromise our data slightly, which might lead to distorted output.
:::

::: {.section title="Transient detection"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch05s03.html#ch05lvl2sec42}Transient detection {.title}
:::

In all []{#ch05s03.html#id564 .indexterm}sorts of occasions, especially when visualizing music, it []{#ch05s03.html#id565 .indexterm}can be handy to be able to detect beats or transients. While the usage of both, envelope following to extract a signal that represents amplitude, and a greater than operation, might lead to usable results, there are other techniques that are also able to detect greater subtlety.

The next step could be to look at and ensure that the amplitude\'s derivative is greater than some value instead of the amplitude itself. This way, we should be able to see whether there is a sudden []{#ch05s03.html#id566 .indexterm}rise in amplitude; therefore, we should be detecting[]{#ch05s03.html#id567 .indexterm} beats. This is shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_34.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/cDJdqye.jpeg)
:::

Well, this works more or less okay. We could implement an adaptive threshold instead of the constant [**0.1**]{.strong} used here. This could be done by doing very slow envelope following, which gives us a rough estimate of the signal\'s average level. We could start comparing to that value instead of a fixed one.

Although we are now going to take another route, this approach is promising and might be added to what we are going to explore next. What we are going to use is called [**spectral flux**]{.strong}. We []{#ch05s03.html#id568 .indexterm}are doing more or less the same thing we just looked at, but inside a `[pfft~]`{.literal} patcher, looking at the delta of the amplitudes of successive FFT frames. This gives us a signal that represents an opportunity in frequency content, which in turn is a very good indicator for transients. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_35.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/wEQloc3.jpeg)
:::

Inside []{#ch05s03.html#id569 .indexterm}the `[pfft~]`{.literal} object, we don\'t do a lot. We use `[vectral~]`{.literal} to[]{#ch05s03.html#id570 .indexterm} construct a more versatile per-frame highpass than `[framedelta~]`{.literal}, and use the square root to approximate a logarithmic behavior, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_36.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Hc6jtzo.jpeg)
:::

Some more filtering and envelope following give us a signal that is quite reliable as an indicator []{#ch05s03.html#id571 .indexterm}for transients. The `[thresh~]`{.literal} object is used instead of a []{#ch05s03.html#id572 .indexterm}simple comparison, since it allows us to use [**hysteresis**]{.strong}. It has[]{#ch05s03.html#id573 .indexterm} two thresholds: a high one and a low one. The `[thresh~]`{.literal} object\'s []{#ch05s03.html#id574 .indexterm}output goes high if the high threshold is exceeded, but it does not reset to zero until its input is lower than the low threshold again. This is a very effective method to avoid double triggering.
:::
:::

[]{#ch05s04.html}

::: {.section title="Sample-accurate sequencing"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

As you[]{#ch05s04.html#id575 .indexterm} know, the Max scheduler does not compute at the sample rate. Also, we know that we have to be careful when adjusting certain parameters (vector size, overdrive, scheduler in audio interrupt, and so on) that affect the timing accuracy of our sequencing. However, there is a different way to ensure timing accuracy: doing all the computations at the signal rate. So the whole chain from timing generation to sequencing up to the audio synthesis/playback should be at the signal rate. As soon as we go back to the Max domain, we lose the increased accuracy we ensured by working in the signal domain.

Let\'s look at a very simple example:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_37.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/FrUMQnd.jpeg)
:::

As you []{#ch05s04.html#id576 .indexterm}see, we are making a phasor signal, controlling a selector, and going directly into our synthesis algorithm (which is completely in the signal domain too). Of course, we can generate or even modulate the values to be sequenced in the Max domain, and of course we can use the Max domain to display the current output. Now, this is nice and simple, but it\'s not scalable. What if we\'d like to have 16 steps? So what did we actually do? We created an array of values we could access at the signal rate; this sounds a lot like `[buffer~]`{.literal}, right? So let\'s just use a `[multislider]`{.literal} object to adjust our values, store them inside a `[buffer~]`{.literal} object, and retrieve the values via `[index~]`{.literal}. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_05_38.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/UWJHRmz.jpeg)
:::

So the []{#ch05s04.html#id577 .indexterm}generation of our values can happen at message rate, and we can do things like our iterative sequencer just as before. We\'ll have to start thinking when it comes to different play modes of our signal rate sequencer, and when it comes to processing its output (how to build a signal rate pitch quantizer?).

The previous object implements some very basic alternative play modes as reverse and random. What might surprise you is that here, we have switched from using a phasor as a pointer for the values to using an accumulated value that is controlled by a pulse clock. I personally find it more convenient to work with pulses than with a phasor; also, it\'s nearer to the logic of analog CV sequencers. However, using a phasor directly works equally well.
:::

[]{#ch05s05.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

This concludes our overview of some more advanced techniques of audio processing. Everything we discussed was meant to get you started, though there are tons of possibilities of going beyond what we did. We discussed dynamic range control and how you can build a system dedicated for transient shaping. We talked about reverberation and how you can go about building a more physically-based one. We talked about FFT and how you can build a pitch shifter (take a look at `[gizmo~]`{.literal}). The possibilities are endless.

We are going to talk a bit more about audio processing in the next chapter, and then, quite soon, start creating visualizations. Remember what we learned in the previous sections about analyzing signals. This will help you understand how to visualize the data, be it amplitude, average power, or transients.
:::

[]{#ch06.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 6. Low-level Patching in Gen"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We are now going to dive into the world of Gen. By the end of this chapter, we will be able to create very efficient external-like objects that can be reused and exported to other kinds of code such as GLSL and C++. In fact, Gen patches are like regular patches, but they get converted to text-based code and compile right away to more efficient code. We could say that Gen patches are some strange objects that are somewhere between a subpatch, an abstraction, and an external written in C++. However, besides the advantages regarding performance, there is another big advantage on the audio side of Gen. We can []{#ch06.html#id578 .indexterm}use single sample delays that feature feedback, which is a problem in the MSP domain. Therefore, in this chapter, we will concentrate on examples that require short delays that feature feedback such as filters and physical modeling. Since this book proposes to use Max/MSP for audio and TouchDesigner for video generation, we will emphasize on the audio side of Gen here. In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Introducing Gen
-   The Gen workspace
-   Exploring the differences between Max and Gen
-   Examples
:::

::: {.section title="Introducing Gen"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Gen []{#ch06.html#id579 .indexterm}can offer us the following features:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Performance[]{#ch06.html#id580 .indexterm} improvements over regular patches
-   Single sample feedback
-   Text-based coding inside a visual environment
:::

On the other hand, we are constrained to a limited set of objects, which is one of the reasons we don\'t do []{#ch06.html#id581 .indexterm}everything inside Gen. Also, there are four versions of Gen, which are as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   [**gen\~**]{.strong}: For[]{#ch06.html#id582 .indexterm} audio/signal processing
-   [**jit.gen**]{.strong}: For[]{#ch06.html#id583 .indexterm} general Jitter matrix processing on the CPU
-   [**jit.pix**]{.strong}: For[]{#ch06.html#id584 .indexterm} the four-plane image matrix processing on the CPU
-   [**jit.gl.pix**]{.strong}: For[]{#ch06.html#id585 .indexterm} image/texture processing on the GPU (shader programming)
:::

Another[]{#ch06.html#id586 .indexterm} reason for not using Gen all the time is that `gen~`{.literal}, for example, can\'t deal with Jitter matrices; it can only deal with audio. So we can imagine each of them living in their own little domain, processing only audio, matrices, or textures. Also, it might be wise to not reinvent the wheel all the time. If we need a low-passed `phasor~`{.literal} object, let\'s just use `[phasor~]`{.literal} and `[onepole]`{.literal} instead of building a phasor and a pole inside `[gen~]`{.literal}.

As soon as we start using `[gen~]`{.literal}, we really arrive at digital signal processing in a more narrow sense. So, if you ever have problems understanding or building a complex network, it can help to pick up a book about DSP and apply the techniques presented in there, such as the transfer function calculation and the Z-transform. Let\'s get to know the environment a bit more before actually doing something with it.
:::
:::

[]{#ch06s02.html}

::: {.section title="The Gen workspace"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Let\'s now[]{#ch06s02.html#id587 .indexterm} take a look at an actual Gen patcher. If we create a new Max patch, then create a new Gen object, `[gen~]`{.literal} as an audio example, and double-click on it; we can edit the contents of the Gen patcher. Something similar to the following screenshot will pop up:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TfoaFCR.jpeg)
:::

First of all, let\'s cover the GUI. You should be familiar with most of it, since it\'s the same for a regular Max patcher. In the toolbar at the bottom, we find the following three new symbols:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The reset button
-   The compile button
-   The auto-compile button
:::

The reset []{#ch06s02.html#id588 .indexterm}button is particularly handy if we deal with feedback. Digital []{#ch06s02.html#id589 .indexterm}systems that feature feedback need some kind of delay, and feedback systems can blow up. Resetting means we zero all delay buffers, so any []{#ch06s02.html#id590 .indexterm}circulating infinities or [**Not a Number**]{.strong} ([**NaN**]{.strong}) values are eliminated. Also, all parameters are reset to their default values (we\'ll come to parameters in a second).

The compile button[]{#ch06s02.html#id591 .indexterm} is only available if we changed something []{#ch06s02.html#id592 .indexterm}about the Gen patch and had auto-compile turned off. That is to say, it\'s only available if there is something to compile. That\'s right! Gen patches compile. Since auto-compile is turned on by default, and the compile process is very fast, we won\'t notice this fact in many cases. We do notice it though if we create an error somewhere in the Gen patch that keeps Gen from recompiling. As a result, we will still hear the version that compiled successfully before. Therefore, we should always watch the Max window for errors or the code that results from the patch, which is displayed on the right of the previous screenshot. If recompiling is successful, the code also changes.

::: {.section title="Exploring the differences between Max and Gen"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl2sec43}Exploring the differences between Max and Gen {.title}
:::

Gen[]{#ch06s02.html#id593 .indexterm} works just like Max; if you can patch in []{#ch06s02.html#id594 .indexterm}Max, you can do it in Gen too. The set of objects[]{#ch06s02.html#id595 .indexterm} is a little different, but that shouldn\'t be much of a problem. However, there are still some differences one should be aware of. First of all, how do we communicate with the processing inside a Gen patcher?

::: {.section title="Parameters through param"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl3sec38}Parameters through param {.title}
:::

You\'ve []{#ch06s02.html#id596 .indexterm}already seen that there are `[in]`{.literal} and `[out]`{.literal} objects, and they do exactly what you assume they do. They create inlets[]{#ch06s02.html#id597 .indexterm} and outlets and we can feed signals through our Gen patches that way. This would be audio signals (MSP signals) for `gen~`{.literal} and Jitter matrices for `jit.pix`{.literal}, `jit.gen`{.literal}, and `jit.gl.pix`{.literal} (`jit.gl pix`{.literal} is special in this regard; we feed in matrices or textures and get out textures). However, there is a Gen object called `[param]`{.literal} that allows us to communicate with the Gen patcher through messages too.

If you take a look at the following screenshot, you can see that as soon as we put down a `[param]`{.literal} object[]{#ch06s02.html#id598 .indexterm} with a name, we can use that name as if it were an attribute of our Gen object:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rDF2uhF.jpeg)
:::

Inside[]{#ch06s02.html#id599 .indexterm} the Gen patcher, we can use the value []{#ch06s02.html#id600 .indexterm}of that parameter directly or access it through the name. In []{#ch06s02.html#id601 .indexterm}order to use the named parameter (as it\'s done in []{#ch06s02.html#id602 .indexterm}the left multiplication), we []{#ch06s02.html#id603 .indexterm}have to declare the variable though, so a `param`{.literal} object with that name has to be somewhere in the patcher. Also, note that we get rid of the tilde in `gen~`{.literal} although we are dealing with audio signals. Why? Simply because there is no message domain inside Gen.
:::

::: {.section title="Buffers and data"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl3sec39}Buffers and data {.title}
:::

Inside `[gen~]`{.literal}, we will often need to store and retrieve values as we do it with buffers in the MSP[]{#ch06s02.html#id604 .indexterm} domain. Inside `[gen~]`{.literal}, there is the `data`{.literal} object []{#ch06s02.html#id605 .indexterm}to []{#ch06s02.html#id606 .indexterm}temporarily write and read []{#ch06s02.html#id607 .indexterm}sample data. We need to give it a name, and the resulting array can be accessed via the `[poke]`{.literal} or `[peek]`{.literal} object. However, we can also reference buffers that reside outside a `gen~`{.literal} patcher. If you look at the following screenshot, you\'ll see the syntax is quite obvious:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/U8z5pR2.jpeg)
:::

If we\'d like to reference a `[buffer~]`{.literal} object, we need to put down a `[buffer]`{.literal} object inside[]{#ch06s02.html#id608 .indexterm} Gen[]{#ch06s02.html#id609 .indexterm} as []{#ch06s02.html#id610 .indexterm}well; only []{#ch06s02.html#id611 .indexterm}then will the Gen environment find it.
:::

::: {.section title="Subpatchers and abstraction inside Gen"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl3sec40}Subpatchers and abstraction inside Gen {.title}
:::

Subpatchers and abstraction work pretty much straightforward inside Gen, although there are[]{#ch06s02.html#id612 .indexterm} some points to note about it. In Max, we []{#ch06s02.html#id613 .indexterm}first use the `[patcher]`{.literal} object, or `[p]`{.literal}, to create a []{#ch06s02.html#id614 .indexterm}subpatcher; inside Gen, it\'s `[gen]`{.literal}. Abstractions[]{#ch06s02.html#id615 .indexterm} are just the same as in Max, a saved[]{#ch06s02.html#id616 .indexterm} Gen []{#ch06s02.html#id617 .indexterm}patcher must be []{#ch06s02.html#id618 .indexterm}somewhere in the search path. In the following screenshot, you can see an example for both a subpatcher and an abstraction (rpole):

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/FIP3fcK.jpeg)
:::

We can save a Gen patcher just by double-clicking on it to view it and choosing [**Save as**]{.strong} under the [**File**]{.strong} option. Depending on if the opened Gen patcher resides inside `gen~`{.literal} or a Jitter Gen object, we can save it as an `*.gendsp`{.literal} or `*genjit`{.literal} file.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch06s02.html#note28}Note {.title}

For now, once we save a Gen patcher in this way, we can\'t simply open it by locating it in a file browser or opening it via the Max application directly. However, we can open it in a text editor and copy-and-paste the whole text/XML code into a new Gen patcher. We will be presented with our Gen patch and can inspect our patch that way or overwrite the original.
:::
:::

You[]{#ch06s02.html#id619 .indexterm} can []{#ch06s02.html#id620 .indexterm}probably try to[]{#ch06s02.html#id621 .indexterm} use a `param`{.literal} object inside an abstraction/subpatcher. You\'ll []{#ch06s02.html#id622 .indexterm}quickly notice that this doesn\'t work. We need to use the `[setparam]`{.literal} object to access the `param`{.literal} objects inside subpatchers, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/BQBVREo.jpeg)
:::

Besides using the saved `*.gendsp`{.literal} or `*.genjit`{.literal} files inside Gen, we can also load them from outside using the `@gen`{.literal} attribute, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/fe1XWWr.jpeg)
:::

So []{#ch06s02.html#id623 .indexterm}there are the `*.gendsp`{.literal} and `*.genjit`{.literal} files. However, there is a third one we can use for abstraction: `*.genexpr`{.literal}. This file []{#ch06s02.html#id624 .indexterm}type is reserved for written text-based code to be []{#ch06s02.html#id625 .indexterm}called inside Gen. So let\'s now take a look at Gen\'s powerful text-based coding capabilities.
:::

::: {.section title="Genexpr and the CodeBox"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl3sec41}Genexpr and the CodeBox {.title}
:::

Inside Gen, we []{#ch06s02.html#id626 .indexterm}can use our visual programming []{#ch06s02.html#id627 .indexterm}paradigm as we are used to, but we can also go for text-based []{#ch06s02.html#id628 .indexterm}coding. The beauty lies in the fact that there is no[]{#ch06s02.html#id629 .indexterm} performance difference whatsoever between the different methods, since Gen compiles and optimizes what we do anyway. So we can switch between graphical programming and code all the time, always using what\'s more convenient. In the following screenshot, you can see three versions of doing a power of two operation. So they all do exactly the same thing:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/dhXAxPS.jpeg)
:::

In the preceding screenshot, the CodeBox on the left is of course created by just making a new object and typing `codebox`{.literal}. In there, a code similar to C/JavaScript can be entered.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch06s02.html#note29}Note {.title}

While it is convenient to code inside a CodeBox directly, there is another option. We can, of course, use our favorite text/code editor to write the code and paste it in there. For Sublime Text, snippets and syntax highlighting are available, so it can be a lot easier to write the code outside Max.
:::
:::

Although []{#ch06s02.html#id630 .indexterm}we cannot go through the whole[]{#ch06s02.html#id631 .indexterm} syntax of `genexpr`{.literal} here, let\'s at least look at how to []{#ch06s02.html#id632 .indexterm}define functions and how to reference the `*.genexpr`{.literal} files. If you look at the following screenshot, you\'ll see that in the first CodeBox, the one sent to `[out 1]`{.literal}, we generated a sine wave via the `cycle`{.literal} function. In the second one, we defined a function inside the CodeBox and called it inside the same CodeBox. The third CodeBox just used the `require`{.literal} command to load a `*.genexpr`{.literal} file that contains just our function definition. After that, we called our function. Obviously, all three versions lead to the same result.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/LNsVFTV.jpeg)
:::

To[]{#ch06s02.html#id633 .indexterm} learn the `genexpr`{.literal} language, it is useful to []{#ch06s02.html#id634 .indexterm}watch the code window on the right while patching. For []{#ch06s02.html#id635 .indexterm}example, if we\'d like to find out the `genexpr`{.literal} syntax []{#ch06s02.html#id636 .indexterm}for a delay, we can just patch []{#ch06s02.html#id637 .indexterm}one[]{#ch06s02.html#id638 .indexterm} together and take a look []{#ch06s02.html#id639 .indexterm}at the following resulting code:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_09.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/6kGACF9.jpeg)
:::

The Sublime Text snippets make writing a delay a matter of a couple of keystrokes. You can find the Sublime []{#ch06s02.html#id640 .indexterm}Text package at <http://cycling74.com/forums/topic/genexpr-for-sublimetext/>.
:::

::: {.section title="Efficiency"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch06s02.html#ch06lvl3sec42}Efficiency {.title}
:::

In general, networks inside `gen~`{.literal} will be more efficient than native MSP networks. Gen does add some overhead, so having, for example, just an addition inside a `gen~`{.literal} object will likely be more inefficient than an MSP `[+~]`{.literal}. The bigger the Gen network, the more sense it will make efficiency-wise. The `jit.gl.pix`{.literal} object is an exception here since it actually creates a shader that runs on the GPU. Processing a texture on the GPU is generally []{#ch06s02.html#id641 .indexterm}faster than processing a matrix[]{#ch06s02.html#id642 .indexterm} on the CPU. Since most Jitter-native operations []{#ch06s02.html#id643 .indexterm}are []{#ch06s02.html#id644 .indexterm}computed on the CPU, even simple things can be a lot faster inside `jit.gl.pix`{.literal} (or `jit.gl.shader`{.literal}/`jit.gl.slab`{.literal}).

Additionally, there[]{#ch06s02.html#id645 .indexterm} is the possibility to measure the CPU []{#ch06s02.html#id646 .indexterm}consumption of a Gen patcher using the `dump-outlet`{.literal} object, which has to be enabled via an attribute as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_10.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/cBUTIKt.jpeg)
:::

Using this technique, the view CPU usage feature, and the `[poly~]`{.literal} techniques, we learned to compare CPU consumption more accurately. We can find out whether a `gen~`{.literal} object makes sense for a particular task, if the performance is very crucial.
:::
:::
:::

[]{#ch06s03.html}

::: {.section title="Examples"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

As mentioned[]{#ch06s03.html#id647 .indexterm} previously, we will mainly go through examples that require short delay feedback inside `gen~`{.literal}. This still is a vast field, and we will try to narrow it down further by exploring physical modeling at this point since `gen~`{.literal} is an exceptionally well-suited tool for it.

Physical modeling[]{#ch06s03.html#id648 .indexterm} means observing physical phenomena and trying to simulate it using digital systems with the aim of synthesizing their sound. Obviously, this is quite a difficult task, and also, often a very CPU-consuming one. Physical modeling, therefore, often deals with simplifications. Also, it might be a hard task to describe real physical systems as differential equations in the first place, so in practice, we\'d rather translate differential equations to digital systems or try to find digital algorithms right away and modify them.

::: {.section title="The Karplus-Strong synthesis"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch06s03.html#ch06lvl2sec44}The Karplus-Strong synthesis {.title}
:::

The Karplus-Strong algorithm is another technique to synthesize a plucked string. For now, we\'ll []{#ch06s03.html#id649 .indexterm}not go into details about its possible[]{#ch06s03.html#id650 .indexterm} correlation with physical forces, since it is a great simplification, which also makes it very efficient. The basic idea is shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ho0a2nF.jpeg)
:::

We simulate the string using a delay with feedback. The loop filter is a low-pass filter that simulates losses at the terminations of the string; the delay length determines the pitch of the algorithm. The resulting frequency will simply be the inverse of the delay time in seconds. We excite the resonator (the delay/string) using an impulse or a short burst of noise.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch06s03.html#note30}Note {.title}

An impulse will always be the same; a short burst of noise will always be a bit different. So an impulse is good to build/test resonators since it makes the results more comparable. A short burst of noise will bring in variety and might sound a bit more natural, which is often desirable for a finished instrument.
:::
:::

Optionally, we might filter this burst before feeding it to the resonator to vary the simulated material of the striking object, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_12.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ZIdw4lv.jpeg)
:::

So you see, Gen []{#ch06s03.html#id651 .indexterm}implementation is quite []{#ch06s03.html#id652 .indexterm}straightforward. There is some conversion necessary to get a MIDI note converted to delay time in samples; the frequency input has also been used to offset the cutoff of the low-pass filter (which doesn\'t have any physical analogy). The low-pass filter is an abstraction, a simple one-pole low-pass filter.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch06s03.html#note31}Note {.title}

In physical modeling and derived techniques, we often divide a model of sound into the exciter and resonator. This has the big advantage that once we are happy with a resonator, we can feed in any exciter. How would you go about creating a simple bowed string synthesizer?
:::
:::

Since `gen~`{.literal} is such a great environment for creating filters, let\'s take a break here to look at the one-pole abstraction for a moment. It is just another low-pass, IIR filter. We already know the simple recursive circuit. However, what about its coefficient generation, and how do we get to two gain coefficients from a cutoff in Hertz? The different solutions that you could adopt are shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_13.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Z4tFyag.jpeg)
:::

In the []{#ch06s03.html#id653 .indexterm}preceding screenshot, you see three different []{#ch06s03.html#id654 .indexterm}solutions to the problem. Sadly, we don\'t have the time here to go through all the details since it would require us to dive into the z-plane. However, let\'s put things in a simple way:

::: {.orderedlist}
1.  First, we calculate the cutoff frequency in radians per sample from the given cutoff in cycles per second or Hertz.
2.  Then, we use that value as follows:
    ::: {.itemizedlist}
    -   Directly, as an approximation; this works well if the cutoff is way under sample rate/4.
    -   Using [![9716OT_06_22.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/nLFx1wX.jpeg)]{.inlinemediaobject}; we\'ll explain this later.
    -   Using the sine of omega. The circuit we have previously is like a portion of a bi-quad, and using the sine is like extracting the a1 coefficient.
    :::
:::

As you can see, we are controlling two multiplications with the resulting value, one inside the loop and the other before the loop. This is because our one-pole low-pass actually boosts low frequencies instead of attenuating high frequencies. We are compensating this by attenuating the input, the multiplication before the loop. What about the solution using Euler\'s number now? First of all, we can drop the [**1-**]{.strong} for our explanation since you can see that we are always using a number for one multiplication and [**1**]{.strong} minus that number for the other one. The solution using [**e**]{.strong} is pretty handy:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_23.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/QraLWPq.jpeg)
:::

This can be[]{#ch06s03.html#id655 .indexterm} used, [*d*]{.emphasis} being the time constant []{#ch06s03.html#id656 .indexterm}of the filter. This means that it will take the filter [*d*]{.emphasis} samples to decay to 36.8 percent. So this is pretty useful if we use a filter to smooth values or create envelope followers. On the other hand:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_24.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/OLP9s7a.jpeg)
:::

This will allow us to determine its cutoff frequency with omega being the following:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_25.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/HzBHTz6.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s get back to our string. As the next step, we can go ahead and use our plucked string algorithm to model a violin. In the second Karplus-Strong patch, you\'ll see a very simple approach using a sawtooth in combination with noise to model a bow. Note that here, we completely ignore that there is an interaction between the string and the bow. The generated string sound is then sent into a couple of resonant bandpass filters that model the body of a violin. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_14.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/NxFm0PG.jpeg)
:::

This is just[]{#ch06s03.html#id657 .indexterm} an example, so don\'t take the frequencies []{#ch06s03.html#id658 .indexterm}of the bandpass filters too seriously. If we\'d like to model a resonating body, we tend to use bandpass filters and need to know their correct parameters: the amplitude, decay rate (related to Q), and frequency. For a given body, in general, there are a number of options to find out these parameters, and they are as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   We can calculate them, which grows very complicated, unless the body is not an ideal string, tube, plate, and so on. On the other hand, many bodies can be simplified to a combination of basic shapes.
-   We can measure them, recording the impulse response of the body and analyzing the sample. In practice, this means we hit the body, trying to get as little room reflections into the mic as possible. After that, we can take a look at the most important resonances and estimate the parameters.
    ::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
    ::: {.inner}
    ### []{#ch06s03.html#note32}Note {.title}

    A very handy[]{#ch06s03.html#id659 .indexterm} piece of software to analyze the sample is Praat, which can be found at <http://www.fon.hum.uva.nl/praat/>.
    :::
    :::
-   We can also use professional simulation software for the task. Very conveniently, construction engineers and product designers also need to know resonance frequencies of the parts they design. Therefore, many big CAD software packages offer the simulation of what we just talked about: hitting the body and measuring the modes. So this approach requires us to build our body in 3D, in the correct scale, and assign the correct materials inside CAD software to get a list of resonance frequencies.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="A mass-spring system"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch06s03.html#ch06lvl2sec45}A mass-spring system {.title}
:::

We just []{#ch06s03.html#id660 .indexterm}used the Karplus-Strong algorithm to arrive at a []{#ch06s03.html#id661 .indexterm}more or less useable result; let\'s now pause for a moment and look at really simulating a physical system. Probably, the most important system for our wave-related tinkering is the mass-spring system. Here, we will use the differential equation of the mass-spring system to arrive at a very meaningful but slightly naive and inefficient digital simulation. A more efficient version would feature a scattering junction, which we will explore later.

An ideal mass-spring system is governed by the following equation:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_26.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/avrjbWM.jpeg)
:::

The force is always equal to the position multiplied by a constant [*k*]{.emphasis}. This means that a spring always wants to go to its equilibrium position. Where does the mass come into play?

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_27.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/iDH2Gdx.jpeg)
:::

So force equals to mass multiplied by acceleration. This is a famous formula and we can just put it in there to arrive at the following equation:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_28.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/BxWRSBS.jpeg)
:::

Since acceleration is the second derivative of the position [*x*]{.emphasis}, we can simplify this differential equation:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_29.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/TlzU9gN.jpeg)
:::

There are a[]{#ch06s03.html#id662 .indexterm} number of methods to discretize differential []{#ch06s03.html#id663 .indexterm}equations, for example, the binomial transform, which is often used to discretize analogue filters. Another very common one is the Euler\'s method, which is very handy because of its simplicity. Here, we are not going to differentiate at all but need a discrete method for integration, since what we want is to build a system that takes a force as an input and outputs the position of our spring. Leaky integrators are going to do the following for us:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_15.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/bSRx7tK.jpeg)
:::

We end up []{#ch06s03.html#id664 .indexterm}with quite a complicated system that takes []{#ch06s03.html#id665 .indexterm}an impulse and outputs an exponentially decaying sine wave. You might say \"But we already got that; it\'s called a bandpass filter!\"; well yes, but physical models like this have the advantage of offering the possibility to apply physically meaningful manipulations. Also, they take physically meaningful parameters: what are the coefficients for a bandpass if we like to simulate a spring with [*k*]{.emphasis} equals to 25 and a mass of 3 kg? So you see, our model indeed has some use if it\'s about simulation instead of production, and simulation is an excellent tool to understand systems or phenomena in general.

If you have looked at the `gen~`{.literal} abstractions we already used, you have already seen the [**history**]{.strong} operator. It is used extensively in the previous screenshot since it is an enormously important object when it comes to building filters (the whole mass-spring system can be considered a filter). It applies a single sample of delay and allows feedback connections.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch06s03.html#tip06}Tip {.title}

Now, try[]{#ch06s03.html#id666 .indexterm} to come up with a physical model yourself! A good []{#ch06s03.html#id667 .indexterm}one is a leaky tank. Given a tank filled with water and a hole in the bottom, try to simulate water running out, outputting the height of the water inside the tank at every instant. The tricky thing is that the water runs out more quickly if there is more water in the tank. Search for the differential equation online or try to find it out yourself; draw a block diagram for a continuous system, one for a discrete system, and then implement it in Gen\~!
:::
:::

Let\'s get back to our string and we are going to use a more sophisticated technique now: a [**waveguide**]{.strong}.
:::

::: {.section title="Waveguides and scattering junctions"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch06s03.html#ch06lvl2sec46}Waveguides and scattering junctions {.title}
:::

A digital []{#ch06s03.html#id668 .indexterm}waveguide implements the idea that at any position on an ideal elastic[]{#ch06s03.html#id669 .indexterm} medium (such as an ideal string, plate, or tube) at []{#ch06s03.html#id670 .indexterm}any point in time (we have two variables []{#ch06s03.html#id671 .indexterm}here, space and time), we can sample the waveform digitally. We can follow the geometry of the body and build up a simulation model out of delay lines, filters, and nonlinear operations.

A basic waveguide is made up of two delay lines, if we think about a plucked string; one delay contains a wave traveling to the right, and the other one to the left along the string. At both ends of the string, the wave gets reflected, so it is sent into the other delay. Also, the reflection causes an inversion of the wave, so it gets multiplied by [**-1**]{.strong}. To not end up with an infinitely vibrating system, we simply reduce the absolute value of one of the multiplications, introducing a loss of energy as shown in the following figure:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_16.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/4qjEuKb.jpeg)
:::

\"Alright, where is the input? Where is the output?\", I\'m hearing you. If you do a little research about waveguides, you will find quite many of these no input no output systems. The reason for this is that depending on where we make the output, we get a signal with a different physical meaning or no physical signal at all. The idea here is to sample the string spatially[]{#ch06s03.html#id672 .indexterm} too, so we can construct the two delays[]{#ch06s03.html#id673 .indexterm} out of many smaller delays, each representing a part []{#ch06s03.html#id674 .indexterm}of the[]{#ch06s03.html#id675 .indexterm} string. Depending on which of these small delays we excite, we decide where to pluck the string, and depending on which delays we feed out, we decide where to listen to the string. The reflection coefficients, [**-0.99**]{.strong} and [**-1**]{.strong} in the previous figure, can be combined with a filter to mimic frequency-dependent reflection properties. Let\'s do something about our input-output problem, going into Max right away, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_17.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/YotpI3h.jpeg)
:::

You can see[]{#ch06s03.html#id676 .indexterm} that we excite the string by feeding a[]{#ch06s03.html#id677 .indexterm} signal into both the delays and we get our output from[]{#ch06s03.html#id678 .indexterm} both. Two low-pass filters simulate two different[]{#ch06s03.html#id679 .indexterm} materials at the string\'s termination. A common method to simulate different pluck positions is feeding our exciter through a comb filter. Now that\'s pretty simple and leaves our waveguide untouched, which is nice because we\'ll now modify it to get different listening positions. We just imagined that we have many small delays; let\'s just divide each of our two delays into two, resulting in four delays. Now, let\'s put our listening position in the middle of both the delays. We end up with something like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_18.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/GlOssGn.jpeg)
:::

Here, [*k + m*]{.emphasis} is always[]{#ch06s03.html#id680 .indexterm} equals to half the total delay time. By[]{#ch06s03.html#id681 .indexterm} changing the ratio between [**k**]{.strong} and [**m**]{.strong}, we can vary the []{#ch06s03.html#id682 .indexterm}listening[]{#ch06s03.html#id683 .indexterm} position. A Max implementation could look like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_19.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/EGuGvvk.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s now turn to a blown tube. The beautiful thing is that we simulate a tube in the same way as a string, using a waveguide. If you find a long tube and tap on one of its ends, you will hear that it sounds very similar to a plucked string. You can imagine that for a simple blown tube, we[]{#ch06s03.html#id684 .indexterm} would therefore just send filtered noise into[]{#ch06s03.html#id685 .indexterm} our existing waveguide. However, we are going []{#ch06s03.html#id686 .indexterm}to move on at this point and simulate two tubes that []{#ch06s03.html#id687 .indexterm}are connected but have a different diameter. This introduces the concept of a scattering junction.

A scattering junction is a construction that allows us to simulate changes in media, changes in bore size in the case of two tubes, and so on. It is a way to connect two waveguides and simulate these parts of the wave so that they get reflected and other parts get through. Its parameters are determined by relating the impedance of the two elements to each other. In the following screenshot, you can see a scattering junction as a block diagram, since it\'s a bit tidier than a Max patch:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_20.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/29UvYhu.jpeg)
:::

When connecting two tubes, the value [*k*]{.emphasis} can be calculated by relating the two cross sections of the tubes [*A1*]{.emphasis} and [*A2*]{.emphasis}:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_30.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/0p2is00.jpeg)
:::

In the []{#ch06s03.html#id688 .indexterm}previous tube example, each of the four [*k*]{.emphasis}-dependent[]{#ch06s03.html#id689 .indexterm} coefficients had a meaning of its own too:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   [*k*]{.emphasis} was[]{#ch06s03.html#id690 .indexterm} the reflection coefficient of the traveling pressure wave
-   ([*1+k*]{.emphasis}) was[]{#ch06s03.html#id691 .indexterm} the transmission coefficient of the traveling pressure wave
-   [*-k*]{.emphasis} was[]{#ch06s03.html#id692 .indexterm} the reflection coefficient of volume velocity
-   ([*1-k*]{.emphasis}) was []{#ch06s03.html#id693 .indexterm}the transmission coefficient of volume velocity
:::

For []{#ch06s03.html#id694 .indexterm}the string, you can exchange the term volume velocity []{#ch06s03.html#id695 .indexterm}with the string velocity, and the traveling pressure wave with the string displacement. So we arrive at a `gen~`{.literal} patch that features two waveguides interconnected with a scattering junction. Beware, this is a very basic model that is ready to be extended. As mentioned, it could also be used to simulate a string, so feel free to experiment, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_06_21.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/HT1yQz4.jpeg)
:::

Obviously, we[]{#ch06s03.html#id696 .indexterm} have two scattering junctions here, one []{#ch06s03.html#id697 .indexterm}at the entrance of our tubes and one that connects the []{#ch06s03.html#id698 .indexterm}two[]{#ch06s03.html#id699 .indexterm} tubes. This model is directly derived from the so-called Kelly-Lochbaum Vocal tract model, which tries to model the human vocal tract by piece-wise approximation. In reality, since tubes are often slightly conical, we approximate the conical shape via several ideal tubes that have different diameters. The original model, therefore, has more delay stages, but you can hopefully see the bigger picture.
:::
:::

[]{#ch06s05.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

This concludes our journey through the world of `gen~`{.literal}. With `gen~`{.literal}, we can export code by just using the `exportcode`{.literal} message, which produces C++ code that is ready to use. In this chapter, we unfortunately couldn\'t explain the whole huge topic of signal processing, and therefore, you might feel a little lost in trying to come up with custom filters inside `gen~`{.literal}. However, you now have all the knowledge about the tool you will need, so pick up a book about signal processing, and you can start off by building filters. Some very good material is presented later, and there is a wealth of design and analysis methods waiting for you.
:::

[]{#ch07.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 7. Video in Max/Jitter"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

We already talked a bit about the format Jitter uses in the very first chapter: the Jitter matrix. Now, we\'ll take a look at Jitter and video/matrix processing in Max, and probably take a second look at what we talked about in the first chapter; don\'t get scared of planes, cells, and data formats. It\'s really logical and we will quickly revisit the concepts to get accustomed with them. But first of all, we\'ll try to get some input and output techniques going in order to get some tools to analyze what we are building.

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Inputting and outputting Jitter data
-   Matrix processing and feedback/delay
-   Geometry manipulation and shaders
:::

::: {.section title="Inputting and outputting Jitter data"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

First[]{#ch07.html#id703 .indexterm} and foremost, we\'d like to get video input going, both to get an[]{#ch07.html#id704 .indexterm} image from a camera into Max and to load prerecorded movies. As the next step, we\'d like to display these images. The `jit.qt.grab`{.literal} object[]{#ch07.html#id705 .indexterm} allows us to grab a camera input and the `jit.qt.movie`{.literal} object[]{#ch07.html#id706 .indexterm} allows us to play back a movie. All Jitter objects start with `jit`{.literal}, and to save us some typing, if we create a new Jitter object, we can just type `j`{.literal} instead of `n`{.literal} to create a new object with the text `jit.`{.literal} in it. The `qt`{.literal} term in `jit.qt.grab`{.literal} and other objects stands for quick time, so an up-to-date quick time version is required.

In the following screenshot, you can see we used `jit.qt.grab`{.literal} to feed in a live camera image. In the case of multiple available cameras, the help file of `jit.qt.grab`{.literal} tells us how to query available devices and how to choose which one to use. The `jit.qt.grab`{.literal} object is a good example of how Jitter works; we need to send in bangs to make something happen, and it outputs a Jitter matrix, which is also illustrated by the greenish patch cords. So the frame rate of a Jitter network is typically determined by a `metro`{.literal} (or `qmetro`{.literal}) object. Right beneath `jit.qt.grab`{.literal}, we have an in-patcher display, a very handy object called `jit.pwindow`{.literal}. So this in-patcher display actually is one of our most used debugging tools when dealing with Jitter. We can put them everywhere to always know what\'s happening at every point of our network. Although putting these everywhere really drains our CPU quite a bit, and we will therefore look at more useful ways to always be aware of our signals, it\'s still a very useful technique.

Another []{#ch07.html#id707 .indexterm}very useful object to inspect signals is `jit.fpsgui`{.literal}. In the following[]{#ch07.html#id708 .indexterm} screenshot, it is located right next to our [**jit.pwindow**]{.strong} that shows us the current frame rate. However, it can also show us the resolution or the number of planes. By clicking on [**fps**]{.strong}, we can choose what it should tell us about its input signal.

As you see in the following screenshot, we have some more options to inspect the data; there is also [**jit.window**]{.strong}, which opens an additional window that we can render to and put to fullscreen. Also, there is [**jit.scope**]{.strong}, which is capable of drawing basic image/color analysis graphs, and there is [**jit.cellblock**]{.strong} to look at the numerical values directly.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/3EW3vr0.jpeg)
:::

We\'ll use `jit.cellblock`{.literal} in a second to have a look at the different data formats that Jitter offers, but first, let\'s stay in this patcher and have a look at Matrix probing. Think of MSP; if we hover over a connection and have probing enabled, we see both the numerical []{#ch07.html#id709 .indexterm}value and a little meter that indicates the signal level, and []{#ch07.html#id710 .indexterm}there is something similar that comes handy for Jitter signals. If you activate [**Matrix Probe**]{.strong} found under [**Debug**]{.strong}, a little floating window pops up:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/EKuo8rN.jpeg)
:::

In there, we will always see the signal that is sent through the connection we last hovered over. Obviously, it also shows us the plane count, resolution, and data type and has some more features. Having this window open so that we can see it while working with Jitter is very handy and is highly recommended.
:::
:::

[]{#ch07s02.html}

::: {.section title="Getting started with the Jitter matrix"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

So let\'s[]{#ch07s02.html#id711 .indexterm} revise. Do you still remember what the plane count is and what dim means? It\'s probably a bit strange, but in Max, we talk about resolution as the dimensions of the matrix, and color channels as planes. Since a `jit.matrix`{.literal} object is reserved memory space, we also have to tell Max what the format should be. So now, let\'s generate some matrices with different formats, dimensions, and plane counts to get more comfortable with the whole system, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/AlKm3gA.jpeg)
:::

In the []{#ch07s02.html#id712 .indexterm}previous screenshot, you can see how we first make a matrix with 4 planes, [**ARGB**]{.strong} (Alpha, Red, Green, and Blue), in the character format, with a resolution[]{#ch07s02.html#id713 .indexterm} of 1\*1 cells or pixels. We use the `[swatch]`{.literal} object to set the values []{#ch07s02.html#id714 .indexterm}of our single pixel and then convert the matrix to a 4-plane, float 32, 3 x 3 pixel matrix. After that, we use `[jit.unpack]`{.literal} to access the individual planes, look at the values via `[jit.cellblock]`{.literal}, and use `[jit.spill]`{.literal} to get a list of values from one of the planes into the Max domain.

To the right of the previous screenshot, you also see how we can unpack and pack planes to access different channels, or to get rid of the alpha channel in this case.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch07s02.html#note33}Note {.title}

While this example is easy to understand, we can also use a single `[jit.matrix]`{.literal} object to achieve the same result using its `@planemap`{.literal} attribute.
:::
:::

Also, to further stress the point that `[jit.matrix]`{.literal} is a very versatile object, take a look at `jit.crop`{.literal}, the abstraction used inside the patcher depicted first in this chapter, `basics.maxpat`{.literal}. In there, we achieve cropping only using `jit.matrix`{.literal} and some coordinate generation out of float values.

::: {.section title="Matrix processing"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch07s02.html#ch07lvl2sec47}Matrix processing {.title}
:::

So what can []{#ch07s02.html#id715 .indexterm}we do with a matrix once we have it? Where are all the FX? The really cool and efficient stuff will have to wait a bit until we arrive at OpenGL (open graphics Library, a standard API used to program 2D and 3D graphics), since all the spectacular FX should be done on the [**Graphics Processing Unit**]{.strong} ([**GPU**]{.strong}) (the processor on our graphics cards); otherwise, we will run out of CPU cycles quickly. There []{#ch07s02.html#id716 .indexterm}is `jit.qt.effect`{.literal}, which will apply quick time FX, but honestly, this won\'t make us happy for more than a couple of minutes. So[]{#ch07s02.html#id717 .indexterm} let\'s look at more low-level tools for matrix processing; `jit.op`{.literal} used to be our Swiss army knife for matrix operations before the arrival of Gen, and is still very important. In the following screenshot, several modes of `[jit.op]`{.literal} are demonstrated:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/Qz1jx54.jpeg)
:::

As you can see, a lot of math operations can be realized with `[jit.op]`{.literal}, and we can therefore use it for basic compositing as well.
:::

::: {.section title="Feedback and delay"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch07s02.html#ch07lvl2sec48}Feedback and delay {.title}
:::

Feedback[]{#ch07s02.html#id718 .indexterm} cannot be achieved directly but is simply done using[]{#ch07s02.html#id719 .indexterm} named []{#ch07s02.html#id720 .indexterm}matrices. We can name matrices to make []{#ch07s02.html#id721 .indexterm}two matrices share the same data, just like how the `[coll]`{.literal} objects work, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ECs02br.jpeg)
:::

As you []{#ch07s02.html#id722 .indexterm}can see from the previous screenshot, we created a little []{#ch07s02.html#id723 .indexterm}feedback network, a one-pole filter if you will. We assigned[]{#ch07s02.html#id724 .indexterm} a name to two matrices and used this to get the output back to the input. The []{#ch07s02.html#id725 .indexterm}input also makes the feedback matrix output by triggering it with a bang. As you can see, before we enter our feedback network, we perform a data-format conversion. In general, it is wise to use high resolution in feedback networks as we just get more beautiful gradients.

Let\'s now look at a delay. As `[jit.matrix]`{.literal} stores a matrix, there is also an object to store and[]{#ch07s02.html#id726 .indexterm} retrieve multiple matrices: the `[jit.matrixset]`{.literal} object. It works in a simple way; we can send in Jitter matrices and tell it at which index to store that incoming matrix. This way, we fill its internal buffer. On the other hand, we can always get it to output any matrix in its storage by use of the `outputmatrix`{.literal} message. This way, we can easily build a delay by just generating series of numbers for storage and read positions, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/eCBuRRG.jpeg)
:::

In the []{#ch07s02.html#id727 .indexterm}previous screenshot, you can see an example of a video[]{#ch07s02.html#id728 .indexterm} delay.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch07s02.html#tip07}Tip {.title}

Go ahead and build a delay with feedback! Max is waiting for your custom `jit.delay`{.literal} abstraction!
:::
:::

So []{#ch07s02.html#id729 .indexterm}we have []{#ch07s02.html#id730 .indexterm}seen some basic matrix operations; let\'s leave it like that to now dig into the wonderfully efficient world of OpenGL!
:::
:::

[]{#ch07s03.html}

::: {.section title="Using OpenGL in Jitter"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

OpenGL[]{#ch07s03.html#id731 .indexterm} is a great framework for hardware-accelerated rendering. Before []{#ch07s03.html#id732 .indexterm}losing any more word about it, let\'s consider a more basic rendering setup to draw a 3D sphere to a floating window, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/f3MBpj4.jpeg)
:::

So what []{#ch07s03.html#id733 .indexterm}we[]{#ch07s03.html#id734 .indexterm} have here are as follows:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   A `jit.window`{.literal} object[]{#ch07s03.html#id735 .indexterm} that provides us with rendering destination or rendering context. Its name is referenced by the other OpenGL objects.
-   A `jit.gl.render`{.literal} object[]{#ch07s03.html#id736 .indexterm} that controls and drives our rendering. We need to send a bang to it to draw the next frame. But before that, we send it the message erase, so the last frame is overwritten with the background color. An `erase_color`{.literal} message with 4 numbers sets up a new background color. If this color has an alpha value smaller than 1, the last frame will still be partly visible, causing feedback-like effects. Play around with it to get a better understanding of the erase message and the process of drawing a frame!
-   A `jit.gl.gridshape`{.literal} object[]{#ch07s03.html#id737 .indexterm} that contains the data for a spherical model and that draws this model to our rendering context. At the moment, we have just one attribute set, but you will see that attributes are very important in the world of Jitter.
-   A `Jit.gl.handle`{.literal} object, which lets us interact with a model by dragging the object []{#ch07s03.html#id738 .indexterm}around in the window. We can rotate by dragging, use [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + dragging up and down to dolly, and use [*command*]{.emphasis}/[*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} + dragging to move around.
-   A `jit.gl.asyncread`{.literal} object[]{#ch07s03.html#id739 .indexterm} that allows us to read the OpenGL frame buffer in an asynchronous (very fast) way. By doing this, we can actually get a matrix out of our rendering if we need it (notice that until this point, no matrices have been involved).
:::

This certainly isn\'t very impressive yet, so let\'s expand on what we have. In the next version, we[]{#ch07s03.html#id740 .indexterm} will be making extensive use of attributes. Many[]{#ch07s03.html#id741 .indexterm} objects in Jitter just have so many parameters that it makes sense to just type them in there; many of them won\'t be very interesting to interact with too. For example, the `smooth_shading`{.literal} attribute gets us [**Phong**]{.strong} shading, so we don\'t see each []{#ch07s03.html#id742 .indexterm}individual polygon, and the model will look smooth. This is something we will just want to activate regularly using an attribute, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/6fcXCVF.jpeg)
:::

Here, we also added a camera to have some control over perspective and a `[jit.gl.light]`{.literal} object that is set up to be a spotlight to give the scene a bit more character. Note that we also added a material here. The `[jit.gl.material]`{.literal} object offers []{#ch07s03.html#id743 .indexterm}some very sophisticated shaders and even allows us to design and experiment with materials with an interactive editor. Just double-click on it to open a material editor window. Materials are the key to getting interesting renderings; take some time to play around with them!

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch07s03.html#note34}Note {.title}

You might find that the renderings somehow don\'t look very pretty. A big difference can be[]{#ch07s03.html#id744 .indexterm} achieved by turning on [**fsaa**]{.strong} for the window we render to, which stands for full scene anti-aliasing. The edges will become a lot less jagged, and the overall result will look a lot more beautiful and smooth. We will talk a bit more about aliasing in the TouchDesigner section of this book. In the screenshots here, we didn\'t use `[jit.window name @fsaa 1]`{.literal} because `[jit.gl.asyncread]`{.literal} does not support fsaa. There are workarounds though, using `[jit.gl.sketch]`{.literal}.
:::
:::

Before[]{#ch07s03.html#id745 .indexterm} we come to putting FX on the results of our renderings, that is, how []{#ch07s03.html#id746 .indexterm}to use shaders to finish up what we render, let\'s quickly look at how geometry can be manipulated.

::: {.section title="Geometry manipulation"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch07s03.html#ch07lvl2sec49}Geometry manipulation {.title}
:::

After all, geometry[]{#ch07s03.html#id747 .indexterm} data is just numbers, and []{#ch07s03.html#id748 .indexterm}since we have some pretty neat tools inside Jitter to look at multidimensional data, don\'t hesitate to look at these numbers. However, somehow, by default, we can\'t access the data that a `jit.gl.gridshape`{.literal} object produces. That\'s because the `@matrixoutout`{.literal} attribute is off by default. If we activate it, the geometry won\'t be rendered, but instead, a matrix will be outputted, which we can process in all ways we can think of. Luckily, there is also the `[jit.gl.mesh]`{.literal} object, which we can then use to render a matrix that contains point positions, as shown in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_09.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ALJVsl4.jpeg)
:::

In the[]{#ch07s03.html#id749 .indexterm} previous screenshot, you can see that we can[]{#ch07s03.html#id750 .indexterm} just look at geometry data using a `[jit.pwindow]`{.literal} object to get a feeling of what the geometry might look like. For example, the picture that comes out of the plane doesn\'t have any green in it, why? Because the [**y**]{.strong} values are all zero. The `[jit.gen]`{.literal} object only adds up all its input in this case, so you see, we can simply start mixing shapes, in this case, by increasing the `noiseAmnt`{.literal} parameter. We also mix in the very mighty `[jit.bfg]`{.literal} noise generator to end up with a morphing organic shape.
:::

::: {.section title="Shaders and FX"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch07s03.html#ch07lvl2sec50}Shaders and FX {.title}
:::

So []{#ch07s03.html#id751 .indexterm}there are `jit.gl.shader`{.literal}, `jit.gl.slab`{.literal}, and `jit.gl.pix`{.literal}, all []{#ch07s03.html#id752 .indexterm}for hardware-accelerated FX and []{#ch07s03.html#id753 .indexterm}data/image[]{#ch07s03.html#id754 .indexterm} processing. The `jit.gl.pix`{.literal} object might be the easiest one to get into, unless you are already capable of writing shader programs. Before we can use a shader, which is a program that runs on the GPU, we have to get our picture, our matrix, on the GPU, where it will be stored as a texture. There, a shader can work on it, output another texture, and we can render the result. In the following screenshot, you can see how a shader might be used to apply FX to a regular movie:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_10.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/91lLlw5.jpeg)
:::

The []{#ch07s03.html#id755 .indexterm}previous screenshot depicts what happens on the[]{#ch07s03.html#id756 .indexterm} GPU and what on the CPU isn\'t hundred percent[]{#ch07s03.html#id757 .indexterm} accurate (for example, a metro certainly does not process on the GPU), so[]{#ch07s03.html#id758 .indexterm} take it with a grain of salt. It should, however, show you the bigger picture and demonstrate that we can switch back and forth, and how it is done. Beware that this switching back and forth takes quite some time, and therefore shouldn\'t happen too often in our chain. Ideally, we just send commands and images to the GPU and let it do all the work. Note how we get our texture to be drawn to the window (which is not visible in the screenshot). Finally, we apply the resulting texture to a piece of geometry, a plane. This plane, `[jit.gl.videoplane]`{.literal}, is special since it has the ability to always be positioned so that it fills the camera view. That is why we just make our texture fill the entire screen, and can\'t see that actually we are rendering a plane in 3D space. Cumbersome (but logical) as it might seem, it is a very widely used technique and an efficient way to get our results out to the display.

Now, we[]{#ch07s03.html#id759 .indexterm} know that switching back and forth is not very []{#ch07s03.html#id760 .indexterm}efficient if we do a rendering of 3D shapes and want to[]{#ch07s03.html#id761 .indexterm} apply FX; the rendered result must be somewhere on the GPU[]{#ch07s03.html#id762 .indexterm} already. Can we put FX on that without going to the CPU and rendering again and so on? Yes, it just needs some careful timing with regards to what to render at what point. We would need to first render our scene into a texture, process that texture, then put that texture onto a video plane, put that video plane on stage, and render again, and all this is done in one frame. Take a close look at the following screenshot; here, we managed to get that setup running. The `[jit.gl.render]`{.literal} object knows the message `to_texture texturename`{.literal}, so this is how we render to a named texture. All geometry objects have an attribute `@automatic`{.literal}, which controls whether the object is drawn automatically, or we need to send it a bang for it to be handed over to the renderer. If we switch off that attribute, we gain full control over the order in which objects are drawn.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_07_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/gfpCZtN.jpeg)
:::

So you see, once we set up the order of events, we can just process the whole scene. Here, we applied a simple hue and saturation processing using `jit.gl.pix`{.literal}, but more advanced []{#ch07s03.html#id763 .indexterm}shaders can easily be implemented here. Also[]{#ch07s03.html#id764 .indexterm} note that we could have split up the rendering[]{#ch07s03.html#id765 .indexterm} further[]{#ch07s03.html#id766 .indexterm} to apply our effect just to the floor or just to the ball.
:::
:::

[]{#ch07s04.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

This concludes our short overview of Jitter. Although we concentrate on using TouchDesigner in this book for 3D real-time graphics, don\'t underestimate Jitter. Also, think about it in this way that both Jitter and TouchDesigner send commands to OpenGL, so in essence, they are capable of the same thing. Also, don\'t forget that Jitter is great for processing lists or higher dimensional arrays, such as matrices. The data doesn\'t have to be visually meaningful. Some great examples are the famous FFT Jitter patches by Jean-Francois Charles that accompanied his article [*A Tutorial on Spectral Sound Processing Using Max/MSP and Jitter*]{.emphasis} in the MIT Computer Music Journal. Find both the patches and the article on his website at <http://www.jeanfrancoischarles.com/>.

In the upcoming chapter, we will get to know Max4Live. We are going to get an overview of how to implement our patches into Live, which will give us a powerful tool for sequencing, composition, and more.
:::

[]{#ch08.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 8. Max for Live"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Max for Live allows us to use Max/MSP/Jitter inside Ableton Live. Plugin design could hardly be any []{#ch08.html#id767 .indexterm}easier than the [**Max for Live**]{.strong} ([**M4L**]{.strong}) system. We can easily build custom audio FX, instruments, MIDI FX, or sequencers, or even control the Live application using a custom Max patch. The lack of a full-featured timeline/sequencer inside Max is resolved by porting our Max patches to Live, and if we ever need something inside Live that isn\'t there, chances are that we can quickly patch it together. As in every complex piece of software, there are a couple of pitfalls for the beginner and general particularities inside the Max for Live environment. So here, we\'ll try to shed some light on how to use it and what the differences between regular Max patching and the creation of a Max4Live device are. In this chapter, we are going to cover the following topics:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Introducing the fundamentals of Max for Live
-   Parameters and saving
-   The Live API
-   An example device -- a parameter modulator
:::

::: {.section title="Introducing the fundamentals of Max for Live"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

When we want []{#ch08.html#id768 .indexterm}to make use of Max inside Ableton Live, we will need to add a Max for Live device. We have three options for doing this:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   Using a Max instrument
-   Using a Max audio effect
-   Using a Max MIDI effect
:::

This just reflects Live\'s architecture. There are audio and MIDI tracks. An audio track can just contain audio; therefore, we can only use an audio effect in it. MIDI tracks, on the other hand, can contain all three different devices, or to put it differently, the following:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   A Max instrument takes MIDI input and outputs audio
-   A Max audio effect takes audio input and outputs audio
-   A Max MIDI effect takes MIDI input and outputs MIDI
:::

This actually forces us to think about what comes in and what goes out of our Max patch before building it, which is a good design strategy anyway. We can of course also change our opinion later on by just copy-and-pasting what we built into a different device type.

If we now choose to drop in a new Max instrument, we are presented with a window, as shown in the []{#ch08.html#id769 .indexterm}following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/spWe5Mt.jpeg)
:::

The device has the same buttons as any other Live instrument in addition to the [**Edit**]{.strong} button. The device\'s interface just shows us our patch, which means that the patcher loaded by default has [**Presentation**]{.strong} switched off in its patcher inspector ([*Shift*]{.emphasis} + [*command*]{.emphasis} + [*I*]{.emphasis}). This is something I would personally change first since the device\'s vertical limit line, which you can see in the following screenshot, is also a bit distracting while patching. Also, we just typically want to see the patcher\'s presentation mode inside Live. If we push the [**Edit**]{.strong} button, we can actually start patching, but let\'s take a close look at what\'s different in Max here:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/gvv8oxW.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s first explore the icons that are added to the toolbar compared to a regular Max patch.

The second icon from the right, [**Preview On**]{.strong}/[**Off**]{.strong}, switches between the saved version of the device and the version with any edits we may have made. Essentially, it means switching between Max and Live, since during editing, both of them are actually active.

To the left of the preview button, we find the [**show containing project**]{.strong} button, which we know from regular Max patches. It tells us that every Live device is utilizing Max\'s project feature.

Second and third from the left, we find two more new icons: the [**Freeze Device**]{.strong} button and the [**Conflicts**]{.strong} button. Both of them, like the project feature, have to do with dependency and conflict checking. When distributing a device, we can freeze it, which means it collects all dependencies and stores them with the device. These features really matter if we plan to []{#ch08.html#id770 .indexterm}distribute our devices; it\'s not rocket science, but you should []{#ch08.html#id771 .indexterm}have a good read about freezing before distribution or migration at the following links:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   <http://cycling74.com/docs/max6/dynamic/c74_docs.html#live_freezing>
-   <http://cycling74.com/docs/max6/dynamic/c74_docs.html#live_resolveconflicts>
:::

So the new buttons yield some pretty neat features, but they don\'t matter too much when we have just started using M4L.

::: {.section title="MIDI in/out"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch08.html#ch08lvl2sec51}MIDI in/out {.title}
:::

As the comments suggest []{#ch08.html#id772 .indexterm}in the default patch, we can get MIDI into our patch using the `[midiin]`{.literal} object. The `[midiin]`{.literal} object is pretty cumbersome to use in []{#ch08.html#id773 .indexterm}many cases, for example, if we are just interested in the arriving MIDI notes or CCs. Just because the `[midiin]`{.literal} object is the only one in the default patch, don\'t think that we have to use it; the familiar `[notein]`{.literal} and `[ctrlin]`{.literal} objects work just fine, and so do their output pendants in the case of a Max MIDI effect.

Of course, the functionality of these objects is a little different inside Live. By using them, we receive the MIDI signals that go into the Live device; we cannot access different MIDI ports or channels directly.
:::

::: {.section title="Audio in/out"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch08.html#ch08lvl2sec52}Audio in/out {.title}
:::

We send and receive []{#ch08.html#id774 .indexterm}audio signals to and from Live using `[plugin~]`{.literal} and `[plugout~]`{.literal}. Both of them have the additional feature of just forwarding their inputs to their outputs when we are working with them inside Max. This doesn\'t happen in []{#ch08.html#id775 .indexterm}Max for Live. Sometimes, it\'s very convenient to develop something inside Max and switch to M4L later, so in this case, it might be handy to use `[plugin~]`{.literal} and `[plugout~]`{.literal} this way, as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/GQpYqjT.jpeg)
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Synchronization"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch08.html#ch08lvl2sec53}Synchronization {.title}
:::

All our familiar []{#ch08.html#id776 .indexterm}tempo notations work inside Max for Live, so a `[metro 4n @quantize 4n]`{.literal} object will sync perfectly with Live\'s transport.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch08.html#tip08}Tip {.title}

It is often useful to use a metro with the `@autostart`{.literal} attribute set to `1`{.literal}, which will just turn it on or off depending on Live\'s transport play/stop state.
:::
:::

Since we can\'t []{#ch08.html#id777 .indexterm}change the vector size inside Max for Live (it\'s fixed to 64 samples), and therefore we can\'t obtain control over timing accuracy, we might consider going for sample-accurate timing. In order to do this, we\'d use `[plugphasor~]`{.literal} to get a phasor that is synced to Live\'s transport. The `[dspstate~]`{.literal} object will still get us a sample rate and so on, and `[plugsync~]`{.literal} can be used to retrieve information about transport settings such as BPM.

We can have a signal sent and received between individual devices using `[plugsend~]`{.literal} and `[plugreceive~]`{.literal}. The `[Send]`{.literal} and `[receive]`{.literal} objects work just fine, although there might be some latency involved. Sometimes, it\'s nicer to do inter-device communication via UDP/localhost.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch08.html#note35}Note {.title}

When using `[udpsend]`{.literal} and `[udpreceive]`{.literal}, beware that only one application can bind to a specific port to receive a signal, and if Max is using a certain port, Live can\'t bind to that port. What this means is that during development, it might be necessary to turn off Max for Live and reload the device to make []{#ch08.html#id778 .indexterm}the port available for our device inside Live.
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

[]{#ch08s02.html}

::: {.section title="Parameters and saving"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

From []{#ch08s02.html#id779 .indexterm}dealing with normal Live devices, we know we can automate []{#ch08s02.html#id780 .indexterm}dials or other parameters, we can MIDI-assign them with just a few clicks, and just have Live be aware of the parameters. But how do we do this with our Max for Live devices? How do we tell Live which parameters are important and should be embedded in Live\'s management of parameters?

Let\'s build a really simple audio effect, one that can attenuate its input and invert the left and right signal. We\'ll use a `live.dial`{.literal} object to control the amplitude and use two `[toggle]`{.literal} objects for signal inversion from left and right. We\'ll arrive at something like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/E9A5saX.jpeg)
:::
