As you can see, the patch itself is extremely simple, and all we did with the parameters is we configured the `[live.dial]`{.literal} object\'s `Short Name`{.literal} attribute to give it a nice [**Amp**]{.strong} heading, and []{#ch08s02.html#id781 .indexterm}switched its `Unit Style`{.literal} and `Range`{.literal}/`Enum`{.literal} attributes to make it read reasonable dB values. The `[Live.dial]`{.literal} object is one of the Max for Live GUI objects, and therefore, it is []{#ch08s02.html#id782 .indexterm}set up to be integrated into the Live system by default.

This []{#ch08s02.html#id783 .indexterm}means we can automate and assign MIDI controls in it right away. If we want to expose our two toggles to the system, we can (and in praxis, we probably would) exchange them with the `[live.toggle]`{.literal} or `[live.text]`{.literal} object. However, what if we really like our regular toggles or if we need some other object like a `[multislider]`{.literal} object that doesn\'t have a Live pendant? We need to set the `enable`{.literal} attribute of the object\'s []{#ch08s02.html#id784 .indexterm}parameter mode to `1`{.literal}. After this, many []{#ch08s02.html#id785 .indexterm}additional attributes appear in [**Inspector**]{.strong}, as you can see in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/aYG1YND.jpeg)
:::

Some of the attributes may be grayed out. We need to assign our object a certain `Type`{.literal} attribute, so Live knows []{#ch08s02.html#id786 .indexterm}whether this item stores a list, a single integer, a float, and so on. If, for example, we had a `[multislider]`{.literal} object, its values could be a list. How would Live be able to show an automation of a list? Or think of a `[textedit]`{.literal} object; what\'s the modulation of a string? In these cases, the type would be `Blob`{.literal}. So Live would be able to save the state of whatever the actual type of the values is, but we wouldn\'t be able to do much with the value inside Live (modulation, automation, and MIDI []{#ch08s02.html#id787 .indexterm}assignment wouldn\'t make sense). So in the case of our toggle, via its attributes, we just tell Live that it\'s an integer that ranges from 0 to 1, and voila, we can use presets, automation, and all the other comfortable features of Live\'s parameter system.

Most of the other newly []{#ch08s02.html#id788 .indexterm}appeared attributes depicted previously are pretty self-explanatory, but if you have trouble understanding one of them, don\'t forget the [**clue**]{.strong} window.
:::

[]{#ch08s03.html}

::: {.section title="The Live API"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

With our []{#ch08s03.html#id789 .indexterm}combined knowledge about Max and about the objects that interface with Live, we can now create all kinds of audio FX, instruments, and MIDI FX. However, one of the points that makes Max for Live way more powerful than any [**Virtual Studio Technology**]{.strong} ([**VST**]{.strong}) plugin is that we can actually control the host application and get []{#ch08s03.html#id790 .indexterm}information about the host into our device. Live\'s [**Application Programming Interface**]{.strong} ([**API**]{.strong}) is the way to access Live through Max for Live. However, in order to control Ableton Live from within a device, we []{#ch08s03.html#id791 .indexterm}have to tell Max exactly what we want to query/control, and []{#ch08s03.html#id792 .indexterm}since the Live API is not a chaotic pool of parameters but a hierarchical system, we need to understand how to maneuver to a certain parameter of Live. This is what the [**Live Object Model**]{.strong} ([**LOM**]{.strong}) looks like:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/gmLvUHe.jpeg)
:::

The LOM []{#ch08s03.html#id793 .indexterm}looks really complicated, but note that first of all, if you []{#ch08s03.html#id794 .indexterm}look up the LOM in the Max help system, this picture is []{#ch08s03.html#id795 .indexterm}interactive and meaningful. You can click on the boxes to get to a specific object\'s reference. Secondly, we don\'t need to know the whole LOM; we rather use this to look up the path of an object that we\'d like to use. Let\'s look at how to use these paths and the objects responsible for access to the Live API:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/jfNNG5s.jpeg)
:::

In the preceding screenshot, to the left, you can see all the main objects that can be used to access Live. The `[live.path]`{.literal} object is used to translate a path from the LOM to an ID number, which []{#ch08s03.html#id796 .indexterm}can be used by `[live.object]`{.literal} for example. When `[live.object]`{.literal} receives an ID, it can be thought of as representing one of the boxes in the LOM. Each []{#ch08s03.html#id797 .indexterm}box, such as a track, for instance, has individual [**functions**]{.strong} and []{#ch08s03.html#id798 .indexterm} [**properties**]{.strong}. A property of a track would, for example, be its name. Using the `get`{.literal} message in `[live.object]`{.literal}, we can query its name. Using the `set`{.literal} message, we can set its []{#ch08s03.html#id799 .indexterm}name. All the paths and objects, their functions and attributes, and []{#ch08s03.html#id800 .indexterm}which attributes can be written or read are []{#ch08s03.html#id801 .indexterm}documented in the LOM. You will see that each property of an object has an individual set of possible access methods: `set`{.literal}, `get`{.literal}, and `observe`{.literal}. Some objects only support a read operation, such as the `get`{.literal} object. Others can also be set. The `observe`{.literal} method can\'t be used with `[live.object]`{.literal}, but this is what happens when we hand over an ID to `[live.observer]`{.literal}. It\'s similar to reading (`get`{.literal}) the object all the time and outputting a value if it changes.

An example for a function of the `track`{.literal} object is the `stop_all_clips`{.literal} function. Obviously, it will stop all fired or playing clips. The function can be called by sending the message `call stop_all_clips`{.literal} to `[live.object]`{.literal} that is assigned to a track.

In the previous screenshot, you saw how an absolute path, using `live_set tracks 0`{.literal}, can be used (we got that from the routes in the LOM picture) to make `[live.path]`{.literal} generate an ID for the first track. We assign that ID to `[live.object]`{.literal} and are then able to control that object. Here, we just query the track\'s name.

To the right of the previous screenshot, you see that since the LOM\'s objects are organized in a hierarchy, we can also use relative paths. Starting from `this_device`{.literal}, which represents an object []{#ch08s03.html#id802 .indexterm}that is the device this patch is in, we can access its parent, such as the track the device sits in. In what track does the device sit in? Of course, it\'s []{#ch08s03.html#id803 .indexterm}the first track, so in this case, both methods lead to the same target.
:::

[]{#ch08s04.html}

::: {.section title="An example device – a parameter modulator"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Let\'s put []{#ch08s04.html#id804 .indexterm}to use what we just learned. We won\'t talk about the devices that you will probably build most often, such as simple synths, samplers, FX, MIDI FX, and so on. Why? Because it\'s just Max/MSP with the addition of using `[plugphasor~]`{.literal}, `[plugin~]`{.literal}, and `[plugout~]`{.literal}.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch08s04.html#tip09}Tip {.title}

Go ahead and build a simple Max for Live synth on your own at this point!
:::
:::

Here, in order to get []{#ch08s04.html#id805 .indexterm}accustomed to using the Live API, we will take a look at a device that really interacts with Live instead. We are going to make a simple LFO, which is able to modulate parameters of Live. This way, we can add noise to a selected effect parameter, or if you expand the effect, start using arbitrarily complex sequencers to start expanding Live\'s capabilities of non-linear composing.

Our device should be simple to use; when we want to click on a parameter, click on a button in our device, and it should memorize that parameter. After this, it should start modulating right away. Our modulation signals will be standard LFO waveforms; the important thing here is to get the concept going. After that, we can start building more complex control signals.

Later, you see a section of the finished device that excludes the LFO, which is coming in on the bottom left. To observe which parameter is selected, we will use `[live.observer]`{.literal}. We get an ID, but this ID is not usable right away. The `[live.path]`{.literal} object outputs two IDs on reception of a path: one that follows the path and one that follows the object. What\'s the difference? The one that follows the path will point to a different object if we move the track of the modulated object or if we insert an effect before that device (changing its path). Therefore, we need the ID that follows the object, which will always be the same in these cases.

So having the ID from `[live.observer]`{.literal}, we resolve its path and use `live.path`{.literal} to get the object ID.

As you know, Live will store parameters of GUI objects, so we can use `[live.number]`{.literal} to make Live []{#ch08s04.html#id806 .indexterm}remember what parameter we modulate. The `[live.number]`{.literal} object is an object whose value can be modulated by default. Its parameter []{#ch08s04.html#id807 .indexterm}visibility attribute by default is switched to `Automated`{.literal} and `Stored`{.literal}. We don\'t want to modulate that parameter, and we []{#ch08s04.html#id808 .indexterm}really don\'t want to think about what chaos could occur if we do, so let\'s just switch that to `Stored`{.literal} only.

After that, we use `[live.remote~]`{.literal} to control the parameter at the signal rate. We could have also used `[live.object]`{.literal} with a message like `set value 0.5`{.literal}. However, since this is an LFO, we use `[life.remote~]`{.literal} as follows:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_08_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/pxsB8nv.jpeg)
:::
:::

[]{#ch08s05.html}

::: {.section title="Summary"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

First and foremost, we learned that if we are good at Max/MSP, we are quite capable of building lots of things in Max for Live. We didn\'t go into building audio FX and spectacular synths since these are just straightforward Max/MSP tasks. Rather, we concentrated on how the Live API works, how to access Live, and how to control it. Although we haven\'t explored Max for Live a lot, it should be obvious that the possibilities are huge. At this point, switching around freely between detailed linear composition in Live or complex non-linear experiments in Max for Live should be easy. Any small utility you imagine inside Live should be at your fingertips, opening up the whole Ableton Live system.

In the next chapter, we are going to explore visualization and start to learn about TouchDesigner, which will enable us to easily realize our visual ideas and experiments.
:::

[]{#ch09.html}

::: {.chapter title="Chapter 9. Basic Visualization Using TouchDesigner"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

Let\'s finally explore how to visualize our analyzed data. We\'re going to choose [**TouchDesigner**]{.strong} ([**TD**]{.strong}) as[]{#ch09.html#id809 .indexterm} a tool for this task. This chapter should give us both an overview of the software and introduce some practical techniques that will help us to work with TouchDesigner. It seems to be a really easy and intuitive piece of software at first, and well it is, but it also is very deep. There are lots of little hidden gems, some of which will be revealed here to give us a jump start. In this chapter, whenever you feel comfortable with the interface, you can probably take a break and try to set up a UDP connection between Max and TD to motivate yourself, and start visualizing sound analysis data. Remember that the OSC in CHOP will come in handy, but we will soon see what this means. In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The need for TouchDesigner
-   Getting help
-   TD basics and its user interface
-   The components and structuring a project
:::

::: {.section title="The need for TouchDesigner"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

TouchDesigner[]{#ch09.html#id810 .indexterm} is a software that allows us to build very efficient tools for real-time video processing, 3D rendering, procedural visual work, multi-touch interfaces, and much more in a very easy visual way. Its flexibility has been used in numerous large-scale commercial projects, so, in general, we can be sure it\'s rock solid. As with Max, it\'s a very open system that can be expanded upon easily with custom additions. Added to it is the fact that we can use Python within TD, which makes it an even more powerful system. However, why don\'t we just stick with Jitter? Jitter is great for many things, and certainly everything we see in the TD-related chapters can somehow also be done in Max/Jitter. However, some things would require us to write a good amount of [**OpenGL Shading Language**]{.strong} ([**GLSL**]{.strong}) code while working solely inside Jitter and trying to []{#ch09.html#id811 .indexterm}achieve these same things. Max came from algorithmic composition and moved towards audio processing. TD, in contrast, genuinely []{#ch09.html#id812 .indexterm}comes from graphics. Naturally, it has some advantages over Max\'s Jitter, which I won\'t list here since software is an ever-changing development. Even still, there are other languages such as [**vvvv**]{.strong} and [**Processing**]{.strong}. Let\'s just say I personally think TD is the most intuitive one, without being less powerful; on the contrary, it is very powerful. Also, besides its bare capabilities, TD encourages experimenting, artistic expression, and technical development in a nearly optimal balance. So let\'s get started.
:::
:::

[]{#ch09s02.html}

::: {.section title="How to get help"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

There are []{#ch09s02.html#id813 .indexterm}several resources that offer help in TouchDesigner. First and foremost, there is the Wiki, which can be accessed from within TouchDesigner, along with the forum and the tutorials. There are lots of video tutorials online too. Finally, there are the operator snippets. Accessible via the [**Help**]{.strong} menu in TD, we can find examples of how different operators work and start using these right away. If you are lost while finding help on 3D or procedural modeling, look into other environments\' tutorials too, or look for more general information about 3D, OpenGL, or modeling techniques. Since we are modeling procedurally in TD, and TouchDesigner is a derivative of a SideFX software called Houdini, you might also look at Houdini\'s video tutorials on modeling. Houdini and TouchDesigner don\'t work exactly the same, but they have many things in common, and we can adopt many techniques documented rather for Houdini users.
:::

[]{#ch09s03.html}

::: {.section title="Basics and UI of TouchDesigner"}
::: {.titlepage}
:::

After[]{#ch09s03.html#id814 .indexterm} you have downloaded, installed, and opened TD, you[]{#ch09s03.html#id815 .indexterm} will find something like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_01.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/uwI4O1h.jpeg)
:::

What you see is a default network that consists of a couple of operators or OPs wired together. Just as in Max, we create things by wiring up boxes or nodes; in TD, the boxes are called operators instead of objects, and what you end up with is called a network instead of a patch. The signal flow is also slightly different as you can see. In Max, inlets were at the []{#ch09s03.html#id816 .indexterm}top of an object while outlets were at its bottom; this []{#ch09s03.html#id817 .indexterm}encouraged a top-to-bottom signal flow. In TouchDesigner, the flow is from left to right since inputs are at the left of an OP and outlets are at the right. Now, press [*F1*]{.emphasis} to get to what\'s called performance mode, and press [*Escape*]{.emphasis} to get back into editing mode. In performance mode, we can essentially output our creation to a screen without having the editor rendered, so obviously, this is more efficient for performance. Let\'s now delete everything we found in this network and create our very first one from scratch. After we learn about scripting in TD, we will create our obligatory [**Hello World**]{.strong} network, going though the UI a bit at the same time.

::: {.section title="A scripting prologue"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec54}A scripting prologue {.title}
:::

We won\'t go[]{#ch09s03.html#id818 .indexterm} too deep into scripting and expressions []{#ch09s03.html#id819 .indexterm}right at the start, but there are some things that you should know about before we begin. Scripts and expressions can be very short and powerful, so don\'t be afraid of them. The most important thing to know is that in TouchDesigner, there are actually two languages: Tscript and Python. Why is this so important right at the start? We probably need an expression to do something for us, but we aren\'t able to code it ourselves. So we find something on the Internet and try to put it in our network, but it doesn\'t work. Why? This is probably because it\'s the wrong language. Since TouchDesigner 088, Python has been the default scripting language. If we try to enter a Tscript expression, we have to tell TouchDesigner that this particular expression is not Python code; otherwise, we\'ll just get an error. Switching between languages is done in the []{#ch09s03.html#id820 .indexterm}parameter dialog, as we will see shortly. Since []{#ch09s03.html#id821 .indexterm}all this wasn\'t the case in earlier versions, you might have to do that extra step of switching to Tscript if you are watching a video tutorial made with an older version of Touch for instance, or you\'ll have to look up the corresponding Python expression.

So, when should we use each language? Usually, you should always strive to use Python. However, we don\'t have all the functionality of Tscript yet, and Tscript sometimes has the advantage of being much shorter, since it\'s written for TouchDesigner. For example, to get the frame number at an instance, we use `$F`{.literal} in Tscript and `me.time.frame`{.literal} in Python. In general, don\'t be afraid of scripting; it\'s extremely powerful, and mostly, our scripts can be very short. Also, TouchDesigner helps us a lot when we enter expressions into parameters; Python expressions are often structured in a hierarchical manner. Take the previous example where we have a class called `me`{.literal}, which stands for the operator that expression is located in the `Op`{.literal} class. This class has a member called `time`{.literal}. This again has a member called `frame`{.literal}. Now, TouchDesigner helps us by evaluating what we select with the cursor if it\'s only a part of the expression we entered. So if we entered `3 + 4`{.literal} and selected this, the tooltip will show [**7**]{.strong}. This is an incredibly helpful debugging tool since it allows us to see what part of what we entered might be a mistake. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_19.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/1awSFGU.jpeg)
:::

I said that []{#ch09s03.html#id822 .indexterm}we wouldn\'t go into scripting too deep but we will []{#ch09s03.html#id823 .indexterm}still start using them pretty soon, since it makes things a lot more easy and tidy.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note36}Note {.title}

One can use TouchDesigner without any scripting for years and be quite happy with it. In many cases, you could get the same results without scripting; it just takes more time. Sometimes, we simply don\'t have that time, especially if it\'s 5 minutes before a show.
:::
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Hello World"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec55}Hello World {.title}
:::

You\'ll quickly []{#ch09s03.html#id824 .indexterm}notice we can navigate around in the network with[]{#ch09s03.html#id825 .indexterm} the left mouse button and zoom using the mouse wheel. Holding the middle mouse button and dragging up and down also zooms in and out. This is true for most of TouchDesigner\'s interface, be it zooming in to data (CHOPS), video (TOPS), or geometry in 3D space (SOPs). It is highly recommended that you use a three-button mouse or a mouse with a mouse wheel when working with TouchDesigner. In order to create operators, we just double-click on the network or press [*Tab*]{.emphasis} to bring up the [**OP create**]{.strong} dialog, as shown in the following screenshot. Notice that for all categories except the COMPs\', we find a slight difference in color that seems to be randomly distributed over all the OPs; some are light and some are a bit darker. The darker ones generate or []{#ch09s03.html#id826 .indexterm}read back data (such as noise, oscillators, and movie players), and the lighter ones process data (filters, effects, and so on). Also, think []{#ch09s03.html#id827 .indexterm}about switching on [**Expose All**]{.strong} to really see all the operators in the [**OP create**]{.strong} dialog.

There are some possibilities to speed up the creation of OPs. If we press [*Ctrl*]{.emphasis} while selecting an OP, the dialog will not disappear, so we can create multiple OPs at once. If we do the same with [*Shift*]{.emphasis} pressed, they will even be connected in series automatically. Also, we can just start typing the beginning of the name of an OP when the dialog is open and press [*Enter*]{.emphasis} to create the OP.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_02.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/2ZTghD2.jpeg)
:::

From all the available operators, this dialog allows us to choose which ones are ordered into categories or operator families.

::: {.section title="COMPs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec43}COMPs {.title}
:::

The official documentation on Components can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Component>. Components[]{#ch09s03.html#id828 .indexterm} are divided into subcategories, 3D panels, and other. We will[]{#ch09s03.html#id829 .indexterm} revisit Components later, but for now, let\'s just say components []{#ch09s03.html#id830 .indexterm}are a bit like special subpatchers in Max; they can contain networks and have different features. A Geometry COMP has 3D translate parameters to move the contained geometry around, whereas a Slider COMP doesn\'t have these parameters but rather a parameter for its color, for example.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_03.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/3vAcnWj.jpeg)
:::
:::

::: {.section title="TOPs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec44}TOPs {.title}
:::

[**Texture Operators**]{.strong} are[]{#ch09s03.html#id831 .indexterm} operating on textures. TD comes from 3D[]{#ch09s03.html#id832 .indexterm} rendering; therefore, all images (stills or movies) are transported to the GPU. We find effects for image processing in this category. All of the Texture Operators are GPU accelerated, but some things such as movie decoding are done by the CPU, and therefore can be very efficient. Don\'t forget that images are just data; if we want to use TOPs in order to work on data other than images, that\'s fine too; just the format has to be right. We will talk about moving between[]{#ch09s03.html#id833 .indexterm} OP families later. More information can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=TOP>. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_04.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/jRSE22L.jpeg)
:::
:::

::: {.section title="CHOPs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec45}CHOPs {.title}
:::

[**Channel Operators**]{.strong} ([**CHOP**]{.strong}) are there for control signals. For example, an audio file can be loaded[]{#ch09s03.html#id834 .indexterm} using the audio file in CHOP; an LFO can be created []{#ch09s03.html#id835 .indexterm}with an LFO CHOP. However, more generally speaking, we could say that CHOPS are there for movement, animation, control of parameters, and control signals. CHOPs can contain any number of samples and channels. Also, there are CHOPS for OSC in/output, Audio in/output, DMX, and many other interfaces. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_05.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/DfJY7Pp.jpeg)
:::

More information []{#ch09s03.html#id836 .indexterm}on CHOP can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=CHOP>.
:::

::: {.section title="SOPs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec46}SOPs {.title}
:::

The official documentation on [**Surface Operators**]{.strong} ([**SOPs**]{.strong}) can[]{#ch09s03.html#id837 .indexterm} be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=SOP>.

Surface []{#ch09s03.html#id838 .indexterm}Operators are operators that act in 3D space. They allow us[]{#ch09s03.html#id839 .indexterm} to model in a procedural way, transform, scale extrude, twist, and edit geometry procedurally. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_06.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/wIchByf.jpeg)
:::

SOPs are processed on the CPU and then sent to a render TOP to be rendered to an image. Their CPU consumption heavily depends on the number of points they are dealing with. Also, SOPs can contain different types of data, such as polygons, NURBS, meshes, primitives, and bezier.
:::

::: {.section title="MATs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec47}MATs {.title}
:::

The official []{#ch09s03.html#id840 .indexterm}documentation on [**Materials**]{.strong} ([**MATs**]{.strong}) can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=MAT>.

Materials[]{#ch09s03.html#id841 .indexterm} are[]{#ch09s03.html#id842 .indexterm} used to shade geometry, so this determines the look of our 3D objects. We can adjust the color, achieve different reactions to light, and more. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_07.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/O8lAmCX.jpeg)
:::

We are going to visit them later.
:::

::: {.section title="DATs"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec48}DATs {.title}
:::

The[]{#ch09s03.html#id843 .indexterm} official documentation on [**Data Operators**]{.strong} ([**DAT**]{.strong}) can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=DAT>.

Data Operators []{#ch09s03.html#id844 .indexterm}allow us to enter, edit, and process text, code, and tables, and[]{#ch09s03.html#id845 .indexterm} they handle incoming data as OSC, UDP, TCP/IP, MIDI, and so on, as well as output data using these protocols/interfaces. These powerful operators can help us visualize data in yet another fashion; they can process data in many ways, and their code can be triggered by events. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_08.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/BWafGVn.jpeg)
:::

Now that you have a feeling of the material to play around with, let\'s select a couple of OPs for our first network; let\'s put down a Noise TOP and a Movieout TOP. Now, just hook the noise TOP\'s output up to the inlet of the Movieout TOP. What are these TOPs doing? The Noise TOP generates noise and the Movieout TOP records an image or video on the disk. However, we can always find out how a specific OP works if we select it and click on the little question mark in its parameter dialog, which is also depicted in the following []{#ch09s03.html#id846 .indexterm}screenshot. To hide or show the parameters window, use[]{#ch09s03.html#id847 .indexterm} the shortcut [*P*]{.emphasis}.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_09.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/uEfJoUD.jpeg)
:::

Operators in TouchDesigner are typically more powerful than objects in Max. For example, in Max, we have the `[+]`{.literal}, `[- ]`{.literal}, and `[* ]`{.literal} objects; in TouchDesigner, we just have the Math CHOP, which does it all. It\'s just two different concepts: fewer more powerful things or more less powerful ones. It\'s more or less the same. This should illustrate the importance of the parameter dialog since this is the place where we actually define an operator\'s behavior.

Now, this is what you should see:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_10.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/XsBQNpD.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s bring in[]{#ch09s03.html#id848 .indexterm} some movements right away. We want to animate the []{#ch09s03.html#id849 .indexterm}noise, so let\'s use a CHOP to do that, although, truth be told, the best way to do it in this case is using an expression. So we\'d like to do what\'s called [**exporting**]{.strong}[]{#ch09s03.html#id850 .indexterm} a CHOP\'s channel to a parameter, which means we are simply controlling a parameter with a channel of samples. In order to do this, we\'ll put down two other operators: a wave CHOP and a null CHOP.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note37}Note {.title}

The null CHOP will be referenced by another operator. We could have used the wave CHOP right away, but what if we decide that we want to multiply it before exporting, or putting other CHOPs in between before the value goes out? It\'s a good practice to use null operators at the end of the connected OPs to be referenced if we want to process the value further at a later point. It makes things a bit easier and tidier.
:::
:::

Hook up the output of the wave to the input of the null CHOP. After that, press and hold [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + [*A*]{.emphasis} and click on the name of the channel we want to export, in this case `chan1`{.literal}, inside the null CHOP and drag it onto the noise TOP. A long list appears; choose [**Export to: Translate Z**]{.strong}, which is one of the noise CHOP\'s parameters. We could also have dragged the channel on the desired parameter in the parameter window directly. Voila, the noise is moving! Since the default wave in the wave CHOP is a sine wave, the noise is moving back and forth, so go to the wave CHOP\'s parameters and change it to a ramp. Also, increase the period of the wave to 10 seconds. This corresponds to the length of the timeline and therefore, we get our noise to move constantly with the propagation of the timeline.

Since the animated noise goes to the Movieout TOP, if you wish, you can now take stills of a noise or record a movie using the Movieout TOP\'s parameter dialog. So now, if you create something awesome in the future, you know how to record it immediately and in real time if you choose so. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_11.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/ig335xc.jpeg)
:::

Let\'s look at []{#ch09s03.html#id851 .indexterm}what we have just done in a bit more depth. We used the []{#ch09s03.html#id852 .indexterm}parameter dialog and operators, both of which have a lot of hidden features, which, if we know, make our lives a lot easier.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The operators"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec56}The operators {.title}
:::

Depending on the[]{#ch09s03.html#id853 .indexterm} family an operator belongs to, its GUI looks []{#ch09s03.html#id854 .indexterm}slightly different, but let\'s first look at what\'s common to them all. All nodes have a name and a viewer, can have input and output, have a[]{#ch09s03.html#id855 .indexterm} [**middle mouse-click**]{.strong} info box, and have certain flags. Inputs are at the left of an operator and outputs are on the right side, as you surely have already noticed. The [**middle mouse button**]{.strong} info is a very powerful tool to see what\'s going on with a specific OP.

Try it and find out how much time our noise TOP needs in order to cook a frame! Flags are not parameters, but they configure the OP\'s behavior and appearance. Let\'s take a look at the flags all OPs have, in the order from top to bottom, as you can see in the following screenshot on a COMP:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_13.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/AWW0vEx.jpeg)
:::

::: {.section title="The viewer flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec49}The viewer flag {.title}
:::

The viewer flag []{#ch09s03.html#id856 .indexterm}toggles[]{#ch09s03.html#id857 .indexterm} the Viewer, so the central window shows an OP\'s data as on/off.
:::

::: {.section title="The clone immune flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec50}The clone immune flag {.title}
:::

We\'ll come []{#ch09s03.html#id858 .indexterm}to cloning later, but you can imagine it as a technique[]{#ch09s03.html#id859 .indexterm} used to replicate parts of networks with a master network/node. If we turn on the clone immune flag, the connection to the master gets loose, and the instances can be edited.
:::

::: {.section title="The cooking flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec51}The cooking flag {.title}
:::

Cooking is[]{#ch09s03.html#id860 .indexterm} a synonym for processing. If the cooking flag is turned off, we[]{#ch09s03.html#id861 .indexterm} force TD to not cook this operator. TD cares about what is necessary to be cooked in a very intelligent and efficient way, so an operator might not be cooking although this flag is on. If its input/parameters are not changing, a new output is not needed to be computed and therefore it won\'t cook.
:::

::: {.section title="The bypass flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec52}The bypass flag {.title}
:::

Obviously,[]{#ch09s03.html#id862 .indexterm} The bypass flag the first input or simply deactivates[]{#ch09s03.html#id863 .indexterm} cooking if it has no input.
:::

::: {.section title="The lock flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec53}The lock flag {.title}
:::

This flag[]{#ch09s03.html#id864 .indexterm} is[]{#ch09s03.html#id865 .indexterm} used to protect the data that the node holds from any change. It won\'t cook and won\'t react to the changing of input or parameters.
:::

::: {.section title="The viewer active flag"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec54}The viewer active flag {.title}
:::

The viewer[]{#ch09s03.html#id866 .indexterm} active flag is the little plus sign at the bottom right of the window. This[]{#ch09s03.html#id867 .indexterm} flag is probably the most often used of the ones we mentioned until now. You can toggle it on/off with the shortcut [*A*]{.emphasis} for the selected operator, [*Alt*]{.emphasis} + [*A*]{.emphasis}, to activate it momentarily for all operators, and press [*Shift*]{.emphasis} + [*A*]{.emphasis} to toggle the [**Always active**]{.strong} option for all operators. Instead of just showing the viewer, which the viewer flag does, it activates it and makes it interactive. Interactive means we can, for example, inspect our 3D geometry, inspect the individual channels of a video, or zoom in. Try it out with the network we already have; with the viewer of our noise TOP active, click in the viewer and drag it to move or use the middle mouse button to zoom in. Use [*H*]{.emphasis} to go to the home of the viewer if you are lost. Notice that the shortcut [*H*]{.emphasis} also works for homing our network view. The viewer active flag opens up a lot of possibilities depending on the OP type. For example, we can use the shortcuts [*A*]{.emphasis}, [*R*]{.emphasis}, [*G*]{.emphasis}, and [*B*]{.emphasis} to view just the specific alpha/color channel of a TOP. Try checking the right-click menu for all the options that come up when the viewer active flag is set.

CHOPs and SOPs have a very rich set of options and possibilities when the viewer active flag is set to on. With its viewer active flag on and field guide enabled, you can see a Moviein TOP only showing its blue channel and displaying pixel values in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_12.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/RVWiGfI.jpeg)
:::

Another example of the possibilities of the viewer active flag is shown in the following screenshot. It\'s a wave CHOP that has been configured to show all kinds of things: a time bar, editing tools, and interactive handles, so we can adjust the parameters as the phase of the wave, for example, just by dragging around a handle displayed directly on the wave.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_21.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/x5f1cZW.jpeg)
:::

Specific[]{#ch09s03.html#id868 .indexterm} to the different operators, there are other flags and features[]{#ch09s03.html#id869 .indexterm} we will get to know in the course of this chapter.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The parameter dialog"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec57}The parameter dialog {.title}
:::

The official []{#ch09s03.html#id870 .indexterm}documentation guide on the parameter[]{#ch09s03.html#id871 .indexterm} dialog can be found at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Parameter_Dialog>.

The parameter[]{#ch09s03.html#id872 .indexterm} dialog holds all the parameters of the corresponding operator. It is split up into pages and can be viewed/hidden with the shortcut [*P*]{.emphasis}. Also, it can be viewed in a floating window by right-clicking on a certain operator\'s [**Parameters**]{.strong}, or a pane can display it.

Probably the most important thing to know about the parameter viewer is that if we want to adjust a numerical value, we can use the middle mouse button to open what\'s called the ladder menu. This menu behaves like a horizontal slider, which allows us to adjust the value interactively, but even more, we can choose the step size out of some different powers of ten.

Every []{#ch09s03.html#id873 .indexterm}parameter that\'s actually available has a little [**+**]{.strong} sign []{#ch09s03.html#id874 .indexterm}next to it if we hover over its name. Clicking on it expands the parameter to give us some information of what\'s actually going on with it, and allows us to edit the parameter in a more sophisticated way. If we expand the translate parameter of our noise TOP, we will see something like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_14.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/oKdopc1.jpeg)
:::

This expansion revealed a different name, `tz`{.literal}, three little boxes, and a [**null1:chan1**]{.strong} string. The name `tz`{.literal} is the parameter\'s actual scripting name, and we will come to a little scripting later on. The string displays the path to the operator and the channel we used to modulate this parameter, that is, the null CHOP\'s channel 1. The three little boxes allow us to choose whether we want to use a constant, an expression, or a CHOP export to adjust the parameter. For example, you could press the bluish button to switch to expression mode for that parameter and enter `me.time.frame/600`{.literal} to achieve the same result for this parameter.

At the top of the parameter editor, we find a couple of very helpful buttons; let\'s go through them one by one:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   [**Operator Help**]{.strong}: This []{#ch09s03.html#id875 .indexterm}leads us to the corresponding Wiki page of the OP.
-   [**Python Help**]{.strong}: This takes us to the Python Wiki page of the OP or rather to one []{#ch09s03.html#id876 .indexterm}of the classes that belong to that OP []{#ch09s03.html#id877 .indexterm}and its[]{#ch09s03.html#id878 .indexterm} members and methods.
-   [**Operator Info**]{.strong}: When this is []{#ch09s03.html#id879 .indexterm}clicked, the information about the OP is shown, such as the number of channels for a CHOP or the CPU cook time. The same information can be accessed via a middle mouse click on the OP itself.
-   [**Comment**]{.strong}: This[]{#ch09s03.html#id880 .indexterm} allows us to place a comment on the OP that is visible in the middle mouse button information box.
-   [**Copied Values/Clipboard**]{.strong}: This allows us to inspect what values we have copied[]{#ch09s03.html#id881 .indexterm} and are therefore present on the clipboard.
-   [**Switch Language**]{.strong}: This[]{#ch09s03.html#id882 .indexterm} allows us to switch to which language we wish to use in this operator\'s parameter dialog, that is, between Python and Tscript. Also, this button will try to convert any present expressions to the language we switched to.
-   [**Expand/collapse parameters**]{.strong}: This shows[]{#ch09s03.html#id883 .indexterm} us all the expanded or collapsed parameters.
-   [**Show non-default parameters only**]{.strong}: This []{#ch09s03.html#id884 .indexterm}is an extremely helpful feature, especially if we try to find out what\'s going on in a network we didn\'t build ourselves or don\'t know very well. As it says, this button will only show parameters that have actually been changed.
:::
:::

::: {.section title="Wires and links"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec58}Wires and links {.title}
:::

The official []{#ch09s03.html#id885 .indexterm}documentation guide on [**wires**]{.strong} and [**links**]{.strong} can be[]{#ch09s03.html#id886 .indexterm} found []{#ch09s03.html#id887 .indexterm}at <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Wire> and <https://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Link>.

We have []{#ch09s03.html#id888 .indexterm}already []{#ch09s03.html#id889 .indexterm}made a small network using wires []{#ch09s03.html#id890 .indexterm}and hooked up operators. We can only wire operators of the same family, but links, indicated by gray arrows, can go between OP families. In the following screenshot, you can see both wires and links; the wires are on top and links are beneath:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_23.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/rKzE40S.jpeg)
:::

The[]{#ch09s03.html#id891 .indexterm} most[]{#ch09s03.html#id892 .indexterm} common example of a link is []{#ch09s03.html#id893 .indexterm}exporting[]{#ch09s03.html#id894 .indexterm} CHOP. A CHOP actually feeds its data into any operator type. In general, we can think of links as a kind of reference; for example, if we put down an info CHOP and type `noise1`{.literal} into its operator parameter (or simply drag-and-drop the noise TOP, not the channel this time, on the parameter), we reference the noise TOP in the info CHOP, which results in a link. We\'ll learn more about moving between OP families, which is a tremendously powerful tool, but first, let\'s take another look at linking OPs: parameter referencing. Suppose you have two noise TOPs and want to layer them. We probably don\'t know what resolution we need yet, but for sure, we want the []{#ch09s03.html#id895 .indexterm}resolution of each OP to be the same. What we []{#ch09s03.html#id896 .indexterm}can do is right-click on, for example, the [*x*]{.emphasis} resolution []{#ch09s03.html#id897 .indexterm}parameter in one noise CHOP, choose [**copy parameter**]{.strong}, go []{#ch09s03.html#id898 .indexterm}to the other TOP\'s [*x*]{.emphasis} resolution, right-click on it, and choose [**paste reference**]{.strong}. Now, these two are linked, which will also be indicated with the familiar arrow. If we expand the parameter now, we will see an expression pasted in there: `op("noise2").par.resolution1`{.literal}. Again, without going into too much detail about the code, be aware that you could edit this expression to, for example, make one noise TOP always half the resolution of the other. Go ahead and try!

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note38}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

Finally, we want to put our Noise on top of an incoming movie. Actually, let\'s multiply them all together. However, we want one noise to have the same resolution as the movie, and the other one half of it. Also, we\'d like both noises to move around a bit. Remember the script from the scripting prologue? If not, use a CHOP export again. To get the resolution of the movie, we can use an info CHOP, and for multiplication, let\'s use a composite TOP. The solution is in the `03_Hello_world_links`{.literal} component.
:::
:::
:::

::: {.section title="The select OP"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec59}The select OP {.title}
:::

The select[]{#ch09s03.html#id899 .indexterm} OP plays a special role among the connections. It []{#ch09s03.html#id900 .indexterm}enables us to get the contents of any OP in our network at any time. You can think of it as Max/MSP\'s send and receive system, but if you do, you might wonder why it\'s so complicated. We always need to know the path of the sending operator, which seems to be a bit cumbersome at first. What we achieve is that, like every node in our network, it has an imaginary `send`{.literal} operator attached only if needed. If you take a look inside the select OP COMP inside the `03_Hello_world_links`{.literal} component, you can see a couple of examples on how to use the select OPs. We can hardcode its source to just have a wireless connection, we can choose to retrieve only individual channels of a CHOP, and we can even change the source via scripting.

Without going into too much detail about the actual expressions, you should notice the power of this routing system. To make this even more attractive, assume that our sources (the Moviein TOPs in this case) are created by some heavy processing. Cooking, that is, processing of data is only done if it\'s really needed; therefore, if we don\'t display them, they won\'t cook. So, using a select OP, or also a switch OP for that matter, can dynamically manage what is actually processed.

Take the []{#ch09s03.html#id901 .indexterm}use of the select OPs in this example with a grain of salt[]{#ch09s03.html#id902 .indexterm} though. They work just fine as they are configured, but one problem might be that their paths are relative. So, if we decide to, for example, put them somewhere else into our network, say, we just want to throw them into the [**exercise_solution**]{.strong}[]{#ch09s03.html#id903 .indexterm} COMP for some reason, we will break the connection and change the path. This issue will be addressed in a later chapter and can be dealt with using modules for example.
:::

::: {.section title="A closer look at timeslicing, CHOPs, and exporting"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec60}A closer look at timeslicing, CHOPs, and exporting {.title}
:::

We have []{#ch09s03.html#id904 .indexterm}already seen that CHOPs can come in two flavors: sometimes[]{#ch09s03.html#id905 .indexterm} we see a graph in their viewer and sometimes it\'s just one []{#ch09s03.html#id906 .indexterm}value. So, they can simply hold one or multiple samples. Fair []{#ch09s03.html#id907 .indexterm}enough, but what does it mean when we say a CHOP holds multiple values? We []{#ch09s03.html#id908 .indexterm}have already exported CHOPs that hold a[]{#ch09s03.html#id909 .indexterm} graph and have many values; did they all influence the parameter at the same time or get messed up somehow? No, of course not. If we just export a graph, so to speak, a CHOP holding many samples, then the individual samples of this CHOP are played in sequence, and looked up by the progression of the timeline at the bottom of our interface. We can, on the other hand, also look up the values of a CHOP ourselves. Look at the first example in the `05_CHOP_examples/timeSlicing`{.literal} COMP. Here, we used a time slice CHOP to get one value out of many, but we will come to time slicing in a second; it has more to offer than just that. In the second example, the time slicing parameter in the noise CHOP has been activated, which results in only one value at a time. In our first example, notice that constraints have been used to create noise only from frame 200 to 400; this is not possible if we turn on timeslicing. Also, notice that the values are not the same although the parameters are equal. The first method is great for linear/deterministic work. If we need noise (but it should always be the same no matter how often we play back a sequence), the first method is the way to go. The second method, on the other hand, is ideal for nonlinear work; a noise CHOP adjusted this way won\'t care about the momentary frame number, and since it doesn\'t reset at frame one, we don\'t have a possible discontinuity there.

In the third example, we used a wave CHOP instead of noise. We use a lookup CHOP to query the value from the wave CHOP, and a constant CHOP to generate the index to look up in the wave. Works fine, right? So should we not use that technique simply because it\'s more complicated? No, the real point here is to understand what time slicing really does for us. It not only reduces a bigger number of samples to a single one in time, but also makes sure everything runs smoothly. If we are doing some heavy processing, we might skip some frames sometimes. Time slicing computes all samples, regardless of the frames being skipped. This makes sure our animations are accurate and smooth, or to put it differently, a Time Slice or timeslice is the time between the last cook frame and the current cook frame. In CHOPs, it is a set of short channels that only contain the CHOP channels\' samples between the last and the current cook frame.

Most CHOPs cook a timeslice of channels to assure that curves are smooth and pulses are accurate, even if the TouchDesigner process is skipping frames. We can see the effects if we turn off the bypass flag of the Hog CHOP in the third example. The Hog CHOP simulates heavy []{#ch09s03.html#id910 .indexterm}processing and we can therefore observe the[]{#ch09s03.html#id911 .indexterm} steepness []{#ch09s03.html#id912 .indexterm}of our lookup method. One []{#ch09s03.html#id913 .indexterm}important thing[]{#ch09s03.html#id914 .indexterm} to notice is[]{#ch09s03.html#id915 .indexterm} that if we only see one value in a CHOPS viewer, it doesn\'t automatically mean that CHOP is time sliced. We\'ll have to inspect that CHOP using the middle mouse info box to see whether it has been time sliced.

Some CHOPs are designed to be always time sliced, for example the LFO CHOP, which you can compare with the wave CHOP in the third example. Notice the discontinuity at the wave CHOP\'s trails in frame 1, whereas the LFO CHOP runs through the shifting phase in regard to the frame number at each cycle of the timeline.

For some []{#ch09s03.html#id916 .indexterm}more examples and further explanation, please refer to <http://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Time_Slicing_Vid>.

Last but not least, consider the bottom part of the time slicing component. The different time slicing methods are displayed here, and you can see their behavior in a trail CHOP as in the other examples.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note39}Note {.title}

The most important example here is probably the use of the trail CHOP to inspect our results. Don\'t forget that you can always pause the timeline to more deeply inspect the trail. This way, we can always find out a lot about our signals or an operator\'s behavior. A record CHOP can also be used for this.
:::
:::

Now that we have a basic understanding of what the data presented in a CHOP\'s viewer means and how data might be time sliced, that is, interpolated, let\'s look at different export methods. In the `05/_CHOP_examples/exportMethods`{.literal} COMP, we can see different methods being applied. On a CHOP\'s common page, we can choose what method we\'d like to use. Typically, these won\'t matter too much for us, but in some cases, it can be very handy to be aware of this. In general, if we don\'t care for the method, the default is used: the DAT table by index, which is shown in the uppermost null CHOP and is also depicted in the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_20.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/WTYqcsE.jpeg)
:::

By exporting []{#ch09s03.html#id917 .indexterm}a CHOP using our drag-and-drop technique, automatically, a []{#ch09s03.html#id918 .indexterm}table DAT is created, docked to that CHOP, and []{#ch09s03.html#id919 .indexterm}referenced in the CHOP\'s [**Export table**]{.strong} parameter, and []{#ch09s03.html#id920 .indexterm}the CHOP\'s export flag is turned on. The export flag is the little []{#ch09s03.html#id921 .indexterm}green[]{#ch09s03.html#id922 .indexterm} button at the bottom of CHOPS and DATs and allows us to turn the exporting option on or off.

Docking is quite handy sometimes, and we can also dock OPs together simply by right-clicking on an OP and choosing [**Dock to**]{.strong}. This will cause the docked OP to always have a location in the network that is relative to the OP it\'s docked to; and it can be hidden/shown by clicking on the little docking arrow. In the preceding screenshot, you can see a DAT that is docked to the CHOP and it\'s exposed. The ramp TOP, by default, also has a DAT docked to it, which contains some of the ramp\'s parameters. But in this image, the docked DAT is hidden and only indicated by the little docking icon.

Now, why should we use a different method? It\'s all about creating/changing CHOP channels dynamically. By adjusting the index value in the switch CHOP in our example, we can choose which of the noise CHOPs to forward to the three exports. You will see that the exports react differently on the change of the channel name. Suppose we are using an [**OSC In**]{.strong} CHOP []{#ch09s03.html#id923 .indexterm}as our source. If we use the [**by index**]{.strong} method, no matter what address or channel name is present, the first channel will be used. This sounds like something we wouldn\'t want. To avoid this behavior, we can, for example, use a select CHOP to isolate the specific channel or use the [**by**]{.strong}[]{#ch09s03.html#id924 .indexterm} [ **name**]{.strong} method directly. On the other hand, sometimes, we don\'t really care what the name of a channel is; we just want to export what\'s present. Yet another case might be that we just want to determine where a CHOP is exporting to by giving the channel a []{#ch09s03.html#id925 .indexterm}certain name, probably []{#ch09s03.html#id926 .indexterm}dynamically. Experiment []{#ch09s03.html#id927 .indexterm}with the different methods for a []{#ch09s03.html#id928 .indexterm}bit and []{#ch09s03.html#id929 .indexterm}see []{#ch09s03.html#id930 .indexterm}how they react.
:::

::: {.section title="Panes"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec61}Panes {.title}
:::

The official []{#ch09s03.html#id931 .indexterm}documentation guide on the panes can be found []{#ch09s03.html#id932 .indexterm}at <http://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Pane>.

If we look[]{#ch09s03.html#id933 .indexterm} at TouchDesigner\'s interface, we could say it is divided into the following three parts:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   The toolbar, including menus, buttons, and so on
-   One or more panes
-   The Timeline/Playback controls
:::

Most of the top part of the preceding points is pretty self-explanatory; we will talk about timing at a later moment, so let\'s dedicate our attention to the pane for now. The pane, by default, holds the network editor, in which we can connect operators, and is switched to full screen (meaning we only have one pane). At the top of a pane, there is always the [**pane bar**]{.strong} option, which[]{#ch09s03.html#id934 .indexterm} allows us to configure what a pane displays. A pane can be split up horizontally or vertically, and it can be made floating and so on using the rightmost button in the pane bar. Also, it can hold information about the network editor and also about the geometry viewer, a panel viewer, a TOP viewer, and more. The type of display of a pane can be chosen using the leftmost button in the pane bar. What it actually displays (what TOP displays in the TOP viewer) is determined by switching on the display flag of the chosen operator(s).

In the following screenshot, the blue little display flags have been activated and therefore the contents of the TOPs are displayed as a backdrop of the pane:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_17.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/klpFZpv.jpeg)
:::

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note40}Note {.title}

[**Exercise**]{.strong}

Create[]{#ch09s03.html#id935 .indexterm} a new file and make a pane layout with one larger[]{#ch09s03.html#id936 .indexterm} network editor and two smaller ones: a TOP viewer and a CHOP viewer. Now, create a simple network that is loading a movie, blurring it (blur TOP), and adding (using the add TOP) it to the original. Modulate the blur amount using a noise CHOP and a math CHOP to bring the noise into a range that makes sense for the parameter, so no negative numbers; scale it up a bit. Now, turn on the display flag of the add TOP and the math CHOP (or did you use a null CHOP to export?). You will notice that the operators\' contents are not only displayed in their corresponding viewers, but also in the backdrop of our network editor. This can be extremely helpful, but also a bit distracting sometimes. If you wish to only have the displays in the viewers, you can right-click on the network editor, navigate to [**Display**]{.strong} \| [**Backdrop TOPs**]{.strong}, and select [**Backdrop CHOPs**]{.strong} to switch it off for that pane. After all that, you can save the pane layout using the add layout, which is the [**+**]{.strong} button on top of the pane bar. The network you should have created can also be found in the `04_CHOP_export`{.literal} COMP.
:::
:::

After the exercise, you should have something that looks like this:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_22.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/xwtNkuP.jpeg)
:::

Notice that []{#ch09s03.html#id937 .indexterm}each individual pane\'s path has to be configured[]{#ch09s03.html#id938 .indexterm} correctly; we have seen that we can go levels up and down, so these panes need to know where the operators are and where it should display. We can point it to the path of a selected COMP by pressing the [**\>\>**]{.strong} button in a pane\'s path. So since we are talking about paths now, let\'s take a closer look at how our networks are structured, and let\'s talk a bit about COMPs or components.
:::

::: {.section title="Components – structuring a project"}
::: {.titlepage}
## []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl2sec62}Components -- structuring a project {.title}
:::

Components, or []{#ch09s03.html#id939 .indexterm}COMPs, are not only a powerful way to []{#ch09s03.html#id940 .indexterm}structure our networks but they can also do lots of other things for us. They[]{#ch09s03.html#id941 .indexterm} are, to start with a comparison, Max\'s subpatcher, abstraction, and bpatcher, all in one and more.

::: {.section title="Where am I?"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec55}Where am I? {.title}
:::

You might have noticed that we are always shown a path like `/project1/ >>`{.literal} in the pane bar. In any TouchDesigner file, if we press the little house icon at the left of that path, we can go to [**root**]{.strong}, so `/`{.literal}. As we used subpatchers in Max, we can have a tree-like topology in TouchDesigner. By default, in root, there are a couple of components and one that\'s called [**project1**]{.strong} in which we usually start to build things. We can move up and down by just zooming in to a COMP with the mouse wheel to go in or down and by zooming out to go one level up. Also, the shortcuts [*U*]{.emphasis} and [*I*]{.emphasis}, for up and in, allow us to move vertically through our files. Double-clicking on a COMP or pressing [*Enter*]{.emphasis} when a COMP is selected also allows us to go inside.

::: {.note title="Note" style=""}
::: {.inner}
### []{#ch09s03.html#note41}Note {.title}

To get a good overview of what\'s happening on the level that we are in at the moment, we can use the shortcut [*O*]{.emphasis} to get a little overview window, or even better, the shortcut [*T*]{.emphasis} to get a table view of all the operators of the component we are in.
:::
:::

So COMPS are[]{#ch09s03.html#id942 .indexterm} used to encapsulate things, but which one should we choose for []{#ch09s03.html#id943 .indexterm}a certain part of a network that should be encapsulated? Well, first of all, not all COMPs are just there to encapsulate things for us. Some of them fulfill a special functionality, as the camera COMP acts as a camera in our 3D renderings, or the animation COMP will hold our animation curves. We can get in there to see what\'s happening in them, but typically, we won\'t do a lot in there. The camera COMP, for example, only holds a 3D model of a camera for display reasons. The real all-round []{#ch09s03.html#id944 .indexterm}encapsulation COMPs are the [**Base**]{.strong} COMP and the [**Container**]{.strong} COMP. However, the []{#ch09s03.html#id945 .indexterm}container already adds some functionality, which we might not need; it\'s able to create a GUI. Since we quite often will want a GUI for an encapsulated network, it\'s very common to use a container COMP for everything, but the base COMP is what we should think about first, when it\'s just about encapsulating. Then, we might think about what is supposed to be in that capsule. Will it have a GUI? Will it be 3D? Depending on these thoughts, we might change our choice to probably use a container for instance. Take the Max analogy again; the base COMP is a subpatcher, and the container COMP is equivalent to the bpatcher. Don\'t be tempted to use a bpatcher just for everything. The OP creates [**Dialog also Groups**]{.strong} COMPS in the following three different columns:

::: {.itemizedlist}
-   3D
-   Panels
-   Other
:::

Most of the components in the example files are base COMPS. If you also create one and take a look at its parameters, you will see that we can\'t do a lot there. It basically has all the parameters that we find on the common page of all the other COMPS. So let\'s see what we have.

The [**Node view**]{.strong} and [**Operator viewer**]{.strong} options allow us to configure what the COMP\'s viewer shows us. A base COMP can only display another OP\'s viewer; that\'s why we can\'t choose a lot there. The OP that will be displayed is of course configured by the [**Operator viewer**]{.strong} parameter. The default here is `/out1`{.literal}. There is some subtlety in this little string. The `.`{.literal} parameter means me, so `/`{.literal} means inside of me, and `out1`{.literal} is the name of the operator. The nice thing is that this operator doesn\'t yet exist when we create the COMP. However, as soon as we create an output of our COMP, it will, by default, have the name [**out1**]{.strong}. Also, it doesn\'t matter if we create an `out`{.literal} CHOP, `out`{.literal} SOP, or TOP; they all will, by default, have the name `out`{.literal} and a number, so the first one we create will automatically be displayed. If you look at the `06_comps`{.literal} component, you will find that the `out1`{.literal} TOP is what\'s displayed in the viewer. Its image is created by the line COMP, which is a base component with a CHOP input and a TOP output. In there is a little TOP feedback network I invite you to examine. One interesting point to notice is how the CHOP input works. Noise is connected to the line COMP, but if we disconnect it, the internal LFO\'s component is used. Input in TouchDesigner have the ability to have a default source, which is overridden if we connect something to that inlet.

We are still at []{#ch09s03.html#id946 .indexterm}the Base COMP `6_comps`{.literal} parameter window. We\'ll skip the clone parameter to address it later and take a quick stop at the path variable. The path variable allows us to assign a variable to the component\'s path. Remember we said there are problems while using the select OP? This is one way to overcome these problems. Inside the `selectOP_using_pathVariable`{.literal} component, the path of the select OP has been set to `var('compsVar')+'/moviein1'`{.literal}, which evaluates as `/project1/06_comps/moviein1`{.literal}, since we assigned the `06_comps`{.literal} component\'s path to the `compsVar`{.literal} variable by use of the path\'s variable parameter. In Python, we can get the value of such a variable using `var()`{.literal}; in Tscript, we can just use `$`{.literal} to access a variable, for example `$compsVar`{.literal}. All this is pretty handy, and you see that our expressions can be very short and useful. The `+`{.literal} sign in the expression just means the concatenation of two strings, in this case, the `'/project1/06_comps'`{.literal} string and the `'/moviein1'`{.literal} string. Notice that if we try to enter an expression into a parameter, we have to expand the parameter and click on the little bluish button to indicate that we are entering an expression. If we encounter an error, the middle mouse info box can give us hints of what\'s wrong. Also, as you can see, an evaluate DAT can help us see what the result of an expression is. We just have to configure its output parameter to expressions.
:::

::: {.section title="Creating our first UI"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec56}Creating our first UI {.title}
:::

The previous []{#ch09s03.html#id947 .indexterm}section might have been a bit theoretical, so []{#ch09s03.html#id948 .indexterm}let\'s get some more fun stuff going; let\'s create a really simple GUI. You\'ll find an example of this at `07_UI`{.literal}, but try to build this yourself first. I guess you have played around with TOPs or even SOPs already along the way, so try to think of something you\'d like to control with a GUI, or maybe you want to control one of our Max patches with TouchDesigner? The [**OSC out**]{.strong} CHOP is your friend in this case. Otherwise, just copy the `07_UI/basic/processing`{.literal} COMP and only create the GUI for it. The processing COMP is just a container COMP whose background TOP parameter has been adjusted to show our output.

To build a really basic UI, we just put down a container COMP. In there, we put down a couple of slider components. While we are inside the container, we can press [*V*]{.emphasis} to view its panel in a floating window. If we\'ve done that, we\'ll see only one slider because they are all stacked upon each other. We can now offset each slider individually using their `x`{.literal} and `y`{.literal} parameters, or we just go to our container\'s parameters and set [**Align**]{.strong} to, for example, [**Layout Vertical Top to Bottom**]{.strong}. This way, they will be aligned automatically. The individual slider\'s [**Align order**]{.strong} parameter decides whether a slider is on top or at the bottom in this case, or how they are ordered. Now, our sliders don\'t seem to fill the whole container\'s panel. So let\'s reduce its width and height parameters to fit the content. Finally, let\'s use[]{#ch09s03.html#id949 .indexterm} a merge CHOP to combine all the outputs[]{#ch09s03.html#id950 .indexterm} of our sliders and send them to an out CHOP for processing. This was easy, wasn\'t it? So let\'s introduce some expressions to make this a bit slicker. First of all, don\'t hesitate to look inside the slider\'s components. We can do lots of things in there; for example, we\'d like to name our sliders and also probably see their values. So for the next GUI, let\'s create a good slider.

We are going to make use of a neat little trick; if you put down a slider, select it and press [*F11*]{.emphasis}, you are able to switch around what you wish to see in the slider\'s viewer. Actually, we thereby adjust the component\'s [**Node View**]{.strong} and [**Operator Viewer**]{.strong} parameter. If we do that switching around, the COMP displays either its panel, the `out1`{.literal} node in it, or a node called `define`{.literal}, which is not present by default. If you go into `07_UI/aNiceSlider/container1`{.literal} and try the [*F11*]{.emphasis} trick, you can see and edit a table where you can redefine some values such as the name and range.

The network inside the actual slider may seem a little scary at first, but it\'s actually quite simple. The [**nullDisplay**]{.strong} TOP[]{#ch09s03.html#id951 .indexterm} is used as a background TOP for the slider COMP. The name has been retrieved from the table using a select DAT. This might be an easier way than doing it with an expression. In the panel CHOP, which is grabbing our mouse position if we move the slider, an expression has been used to directly get the name from `define`{.literal}. In the Math CHOP, the table is referenced to rescale the channel correctly. Here, we are using an expression, `op('define')['lowerRange', 1]`{.literal}, to directly access DAT\'s range values for the lower range limit for example. The `Op()`{.literal} part indicates that we want to access an operator; `op('define')`{.literal} indicates we are looking for an operator called `define`{.literal}. The notation with the square brackets simply is a way to access cells in []{#ch09s03.html#id952 .indexterm}a DAT table, which we can always look up at <http://www.derivative.ca/wiki088/index.php?title=Working_with_DATs_in_Python>.
:::

::: {.section title="Hierarchy"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec57}Hierarchy {.title}
:::

Quite a lot []{#ch09s03.html#id953 .indexterm}of scripting has been used in this example to[]{#ch09s03.html#id954 .indexterm} make the sliders always fit the width of the container in which they are. For this, the expression `me.parent().par.w`{.literal} has been used. This again is an expression to remember; it\'s very handy but also easy to remember if we understand it. The `me`{.literal} part indicates the operator in which the expression is located. This operator has a parent in hierarchy, in the path. So in our example, slider2\'s parent is the `container1`{.literal} component. Inside the parenthesis, we can decide how many levels we wish to go up. If we don\'t pick a number for this value, or don\'t give the method an argument, the default is used: we go one level up. So `me.parent()`{.literal} is the same as `me.parent(1)`{.literal}.

At this point, we arrived at `me.parent(1)`{.literal}, an operator again. The expression stands for the `container1`{.literal} component in this case. It references it in a relative way of course. Now, we can start []{#ch09s03.html#id955 .indexterm}querying this operator\'s parameters using []{#ch09s03.html#id956 .indexterm}the `.par`{.literal} notation and choose which one by again putting a dot and a name after this. That\'s how we arrive at `me.parent().par.w`{.literal}, where `w`{.literal} stands for the width parameter, and we can find that out by just hovering the mouse over any parameter\'s name. All of this just returns a number that defines our slider\'s width.

There is another notion of hierarchy in TouchDesigner. Some component types can be put in parent-child relationships without the need of putting one inside the other but by merely using a connection. Look at the [**08_hierarchy**]{.strong} COMP in the provided file. Again, a very primitive UI is built, but notice that it is structured via the top and bottom connectors of the container COMPs, as depicted in the following screenshot. This is going to be particularly useful when we deal with 3D geometry since geometry COMPS inherit their transform parameters this way.

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_18.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/CaimTLY.jpeg)
:::

If you wish, check[]{#ch09s03.html#id957 .indexterm} out the width and height parameters[]{#ch09s03.html#id958 .indexterm} of the individual components in there. You will see that if you change the parameters of the topmost container, all others will scale correctly since some simple expressions have been used. Don\'t forget the [**Non-default parameters only**]{.strong} button in the parameter\'s dialog to quickly see what has been changed. Making a UI easily scalable saves a lot of time.
:::

::: {.section title="Abstraction"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec58}Abstraction {.title}
:::

Let\'s forget about all the fancy scripting again for now and think about what we can do if we have []{#ch09s03.html#id959 .indexterm}achieved something with or without it. Let\'s go back []{#ch09s03.html#id960 .indexterm}to the common page of the parameter dialog of our base COMP. What we also find there is the external `.tox`{.literal} parameter. TouchDesigner files are saved as `.toe`{.literal} files, so where can we get a `.tox`{.literal} file from? We can right-click on any component and choose [**Save component**]{.strong} from the context menu to save out a tox of that file. This file can now be referenced in any component\'s external tox parameter. This, of course, is a very helpful tool if one works with lots of custom tools or also in collaborative work, but we have discussed the advantages of abstraction in the Max section already. One interesting expression in this context might be `project.folder`{.literal}. Project is a class that references our project file, and it has a member named `folder`{.literal}. This returns the folder in which our `.toe`{.literal} file is located.

So if we build a bigger project, we might end up with a project folder that contains subfolders such as audio, image, and so on. TouchDesigner is able to do this for us automatically via [**File**]{.strong} \| [**Create Project Folder**]{.strong}. One of the subfolders might contain all our abstractions, that is, `.tox`{.literal} files. These might be referenced in components via `project.folder+'/tox/myAbstraction.tox'`{.literal}. This way, if we move the whole project folder, it will still be working and self-contained. As you see, keeping a tidy file structure is quite important here, but before migrating a project, you should always try the `opfiles`{.literal} command. Open a textport or switch an open pane into the textport mode, switch its language to Tscript, and type `opfiles`{.literal}. This will cause TouchDesigner to print all the used files, and you can see whether there are any references to things outside your project folder or whether absolute paths are used somewhere that could break due to migration. An example of an external tox referenced in a relative manner is the [**05_CHOP_examples**]{.strong} COMP. Its source file lies in a folder called `tox`{.literal} that in turn lies right next to the actual TOE file.

After doing all of this, having made sliders ourselves, exported, and imported components, let\'s take a look at what TouchDesigner offers us natively without us having to do all that we have just done; let\'s have a look at the palette.
:::

::: {.section title="Palette"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec59}Palette {.title}
:::

The palette[]{#ch09s03.html#id961 .indexterm} can be opened and closed via the [**Open palette**]{.strong} button[]{#ch09s03.html#id962 .indexterm} that is to the left of the pane layout buttons. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_15.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/3SpR7e6.jpeg)
:::

The[]{#ch09s03.html#id963 .indexterm} palette is a collection of pre-made, ready-to-use components. They[]{#ch09s03.html#id964 .indexterm} have a different functionality, such as 3D modeling, UI elements, and image processing. Also, they are a great source for learning, so take a look inside some of these! The nice thing is that we can add our own ones simply by dragging them onto the palette. Also, we can create new folders via a right-click. In fact, the palette is simply a file browser. Our custom components lie at `C:/Users/username/Derivative/Touchdesigner088/`{.literal}. If you are annoyed by the fact that we have gone through a rather hard way of building a UI and afterwards discover the palette where lots of great UI elements are ready to use, you should know that it\'s good to at least know the fundamentals of these. The time, we\'d like to modify what\'s there will come soon enough.
:::

::: {.section title="Local"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec60}Local {.title}
:::

Components form a local context for variables and inherit these through the hierarchy. A classic use []{#ch09s03.html#id965 .indexterm}case for these variables is resolution. TOPs, in general, use[]{#ch09s03.html#id966 .indexterm} their input\'s resolution, aspect ratio, and bit depth/pixel format. However, some TOPs won\'t have an input in our network, such as noise TOPs, ramps, and so on. So if we decide to change the resolution at the last moment before our show or the minute we want to leave our installation, we just have to change a variable in a central place. So that\'s how we do it; in our network, we just put down a base COMP we call `local`{.literal}. In there, we create a table DAT, call it `variables`{.literal}, give it two columns, one for the variable name and one for the value, and voila, we created a neat place to store our variables. Refer to the following screenshot:

::: {.mediaobject}
![9716OT_09_16.jpg](https://i.imgur.com/GSMf0tB.jpeg)
:::

Look into the [**09_local**]{.strong} COMP; you\'ll see this construction and you\'ll see a noise TOP that uses the variables in its parameters. Now, there is another base COMP in there. This one has its own local COMP again, which can override the inherited variables. This inherit-and-override behavior and the notion of local can do a lot more than just store variables.

For example, we can easily add a local time to a component. This is done by right-clicking on the network and choosing [**Add Component Time**]{.strong}. When this is done, we get a (possibly) independent new timeline, and all OPs inside that component will reference this new timeline. Also, we can define our own reusable scripts inside modules inside the local COMP. Obviously, the local COMP is a very special place and we will use it a lot in the upcoming chapters.
:::

::: {.section title="Clones"}
::: {.titlepage}
### []{#ch09s03.html#ch09lvl3sec61}Clones {.title}
:::

We have []{#ch09s03.html#id967 .indexterm}learned a little about clones already since we have a clone []{#ch09s03.html#id968 .indexterm}immune flag on every OP. Cloning is a powerful technique that is related to abstraction. The idea is that we have a master component somewhere in our network, for example, a slider we created or a 3D modeling network. Changing the master changes the clones, but each individual OP within a clone can be excluded from being identical to the master\'s corresponding OPs. In the example, the slider from the UI component has been used as a master. We still want to configure the names independently though, right? So we just turn on the clone immune flag of our define table DAT, and the slider is ready to be cloned. Cloning is simply done by creating a component of the same type as the master. On the common tab in its parameter dialog, we provide the path to the master and end up with a clone. Later on, we will see how this, in combination with the replicator COMP, can be a very quick method to create big complex networks.
:::

::: {.section title="What's happening in root?"}
::: {.titlepage}
